The Red Page
October
Of the 46 entries for a sonnet on our
October, six were not sonnets. We were not
asking for spring poems. A common fault
of the sonnets submitted was that many
were mere catalogues. Thus it happens
that out of sheer weariness of fauna and
flora this sonnet by “A Woman who Never
has Time to Look at the Scenery” strikes
the critic as worth print:—
Now comes October, firstling of the Spring,
With juicy lamb, arid sauce that’s made with mint,
Green peasfc and salads, onions without stint—•
Such culinary joys the month doth bring.
Pull down the curtains, out the carpets fling 1
Bring hither mop and broom, and now by dint
Of working hard and long, with spurt and sprint, f
We give the season proper welcoming.
Then, in the afternoon, when all is clear,
We hie us to the city, there to view
Creations to the heart of woman dear—
October’s hats of every shape and hue ;
Ah, sigh opt so, suppress that envious tear—
The grocer’s bill is long, long overdue!
The keynote of most of the sonnets was
October's waywardness: she is “mistress of
caprice,” “fickle,” a “maid of moods”; but
several .sonneteers’ found solace from her
uncertainty in the surf. One. Ernest Casey,
more fortunate, found it in beer.
Forth, from old rat-holes come the wakened snakes,
Ravening (yet heedful of remembered foes),
To seek for mice and lizards in the grass.
So from its winter sleep my thirst awakes
To seek those fountains whence the good beer flows
And makes a brown October in the glass.
Pleasant sonnets, submitted by B. C.
Marriott Watson, “G.8.1X,” 11. D. Allen
and “Le Beau Temps” are too slight in in-
tention for publication here. The weeding-
out process resulted in seven nice sonnets.
M. Forrest’s sonnet, beginning finely—-
“October has his satchel full of gold”
is not well welded into compactness. Its
jumpiness obscures its sense, and no single
image emerges from the rich details. Mado-
line Murdoch takes the conventional meta-
phor of October turning from Spring’s love
to Summer’s caresses—and treats it con-
ventionally. Witness her sestette
How beautiful and faithless, with her lips
Where yet September’s vernal kisses cling—
Curled amorously to lure the Summer on!
She passes swiftly, smiling as she slips
Into her ardent new love’s arms. And Spring
With many a stricken backward glance, is gone!
Note how much more boldly W.L.D. treats
the same conceit * —
October is a wedded flirt whose spouse,
Declining Spring, lord Time condemns to see
The stripling Summer all audaciously
On west wind blow the fade his early-vows;
Yet, not quite won and full averse to rouse
'Twixt new and old love aught of enmity,
Lo, she recoils within her chastity,
But first one kiss the brown-faced boy allows.
So half disdainful of her bridal joys,
So half regretful of her vernal train,
The fickle month most insecurely stands
And doubtful of herself her charm alloys
By moods extreme, now sunny, now like
rain,
While to and fro stretch out her wav’ring
hands.
Marie E. J. Pitt becomes geographical.
(By the way, all complete Sonnets quoted
will be paid for.)
A fickle goddess moved of many moods,
Capricious, fretful as the homeless wind
A-moan amonffi the bull-oaks, and as blind
As sunstayed owl marooned in day-lit woods,
And so it haps, that, while the halcyon
broods
And northern uplands, all her glories bind,
And Sydney, bowered in bloom, and verdure
twined
Dreams softly through her white beatitudes,
Where southern capes uplift like mighty
horns "A
Of mihotaurs fast-frozen on the marge
Of grim Antarctic, Melbourne, veiled and
gray
Waits like a beggar scourged and turned
away,
Nor spurns the crumbs from banquets fair
and large,
Spring’s niggard honey, licked from Winter's
thorns.
The three remaining sonnets are admir-
able, though one is regretfully cast forth
because technically it limps. This is by
Duncan Brown:—
Time of lost winds of import vague that veer
From ends of all the earth and mocking
bring,
Now wanton incense of the passing spring,
Now wintry menace and a chilling fear,
Or summer's scorching breath —strange days
whose clear
Bright dawns send noontide winds to fling
A tumbling mass of cloud and thundering
Of distant storms that threaten, looming
near.
Australia’s month —wan days of change and
doubt,
Of sudden light and dark, when men go to
And fro with eager questioning, and all
Her dreams of quiet growth in fearful rout
Have fled before the ruddy, awful hue
Of old-world clouds whence Destiny may
fall.
The missing foot in the sixth line could,
of course, be justified were a looser method
of scafision allowed in so rigid a form as
the sonnet. “Bright dawns” is a large
mouthful for one iambic foot: with scan-
sioß on Bridges’ stress principle each word
would be equivalent to a foot. But the
sonnet must be more strictly scanned;
and the author has simply been led by the
“length” of those two words into thinking
that he had filled up his line.
“S.E.N.’s” sonnet is strictly conventional,
but admirably done. It has the lower
merit of chronological accuracy, yet escapes
the bathos of a too definite detail. Tire
fourth and fifth lines have the caesura in
the same place—an obvious defect.
Priestess of flow'rs- —her acolytes the bees —
She comes, sun-kiss'd anon, now chastely
cold;
Grey-garbed she weeps or sings in shining
gold,
October, Mistress of Caprice! The seas
Begin for her their siren song; the trees
Full-freighted with shy, nestling birds,
unfold
Their new green cloaks and all the flowers
hold
The fragrance of her rare and rain-wash’d
breeze.
Sweet wilful maiden-month of fronding
fern.
Hers is the ever-changeless charm of change:
Before her Aoaits and waves the whisp’ring
wheat;
To her the waratahs ’ red altars burn;
The wattle dies for her along the range
And casts his golden glories at her feet!
Thus we are left with this delightfully
unorthodox presentation of October, written'
in Maqriland. The octette intrigues you,
though it does little more; but the sestette
is supei'b. A looseness of rime is forgiven
for the sake of the sonnet’s worth. The
prize goes therefore to A. Gladys Kernot,
Wellington, MX., for
OCTOBER.
She came so fleetly that you scarce knew
where
Nor when she slipped from pale Septem-
ber's fold;
A lissom witch in apple-green and gold, _
Ten thousand prisoned sunsets in her hair:
Reincarnate —yet ah! so softly fair;
So warmly new, so wonderlessly old —
But who would dream so sweet a wench
could hold
A dusty niche on Time's old calendar f
Yet she is here, bareheaded, in the street,
A rose between her teeth, an eye that mocks
The maundering traffic; with a carmined
lip, _ 4
And little sudden ways, and darting fdet;
Her hair a-tumble with the equinox,
And Spring's third baby slung across her
hip.
MAOfiILAND.
A. GLADYS KERNOT.
The Cheerful Cubist
Do\ you cube? If not, why not become a
Cubist? Or a Cubiste? It is quite easy.
Our correspondence class will in one lesson
make you see things cubically, and after
that any intelligent art student can cube
to his or her heart’s content. Our profes-
sors are Albert Gleizes and Jean Metzinger,
both incorrigible Cubists, specimens of
whose cubes are herewith reproduced as a
guarantee of respectability. From Cubism
(Unwin) we extract the material of our
first correspondence class. Cubism is not a
melancholy profession: anyone can be happy
though cubic. “The joy of confining unlim-
ited art within the limits of the picture is
worth the effort which it requires.”
J*
Of Cubism C6zanne is the Old Master.
He is “one of the greatest of those artists
who constitute the landmarks of history,
and it ill, becomes us to compare him with
Van Gogh or Gauguin. He suggests Rem-
crandt. . . .From him we have learned that
to alter the coloration of a body is to cor-
rupt its structure. He prophesies that the
study of primordial volume will open un-
known horizons to us. His work, a homo-
geneous mass, shifts under the glance, con-
tracts, expands, fades or illumines itself,
irrefragably proving that painting is not —
or is no longer—the art of imitating an ob-
ject by means of lines and colors, but the
art of giving our instinct a plastic con-
sciousness.”
WOMAN ON HORSEBACK
(Salon des Independants, 1912), by Jean
Metzinger.
Do you get that? The first exercise is to
give your instinct a- plastic consciofisness.
The rest is easy. The main thing is not to
imitate an object by means of lines and
colors. Note in the example of Jean Met-
zinger how admirably your professof has
succeeded in not imitating his “Woman on
Horseback” by any means whatever.
** Under penalty of condemning all modern
painting, we must regard Citbism as legiti-
mate, for it continues modern methods, and
we should see in it the only conception at
present possible of the pictorial art. In
other words, Cubism is at present painting
itself.
Unless you are a cube you cannot paint.
The only artists are Cubists. Obviously,
unless you want to get left, the only thing
for the art student is to cube without delay.
And do not dare to be decorative. “We
ought to regard the decorative preoccupation
if we find it in h painter, as an anachronistic
artifice, useful only to conceal impotence.”
And do not despair if the public dislike your
cubes. Be more bravely cubic, and in the
end the public will applaud. “A man will
enjoy to-day what exasperated him yester-
day. . . .How should comprehension evolve as
rapidly as the creative faculties? It follows
in their wake.”
Jt
The next lesson is to beware of master-
pieces—even culyc masterpieces. “Decorum
demands a certain degree of dimness, and de-
corum is one of the attributes of art.”
Above all, let no one be decoyed by the
apoearance of objectivity with which many
impudent artists endow their pictures. The
fact commonly invoked, that we find in
a painting the familiar characteristics which
form its motive, proves nothing at all. Let
us imagine a landscape. The width of the
river, the thickness of the foliage, the height
of the banks, the dimensions of each object
and the relations of these dimensions —these
are secure guarantees. Well, if. we find
these intact upon the canvas, we shall have
learned nothing as to the talent or the
genius of the painter. River, foliage, and
banks, despite a conscientious representa-
tion to scale, no longer “tell” by virtye of
their width, thickness and height, or the
relations between these dimensions. Torn
from natural space- they have entered a
different kind of space, which does not
assimilate the proportion observed. This
remains an external matter. It has just
as much importance as a catalogue number,
or the title at the bottom of a picture-frame.
Anybody, even a R.A., or Lister Lister,
can paint a place that looks like the place. The
aim of all good young Cubists should be to
PORTRAIT OF A MAN,
by Juan Gris.
paint a place that doesn’t look like anything
at all except a cube root. Accuracy of rep-
resentation is quite superfluous. Our cubic
professors remind us that “we visit an exhi-
bition to contemplate painting and to enjoy
it” —just as you may enjoy the specimens
given herewith: “not to enlarge our know-
ledge of geography, anatomy, etc.”
Let your picture imitate nothing; let it
nakedly present its motive, and we would be
ungrateful were we to deplore the absence of
those things—flowers, or landscape, or faces
-—whose mere reflection it might have been.
But, at present, your instructors regret that;
the reminiscence of natural forms cannot be\
absolutely banished. Careful study will
bring to light at least portions of the woman
and fragments of the horse in Metzinger’s
masterpiece. Remember that “an art can-
not be raised to the level of a pure effusion
at the first step.” But there is no harm in
your trying.
The next lesson can be comprehended only
by extremely cubical Cubists, but it is in-
serted here for our advanced students.
Every inflection of form is accompanied
by a modification of color, and every modi-
fication of color gives birth to a form.
There are tints which refuse to wed certain
lines; there are surfaces which cannot sup-
port certain colors, repelling them to a
distance or sinking under them as unun r
heavy a weight. To simple forms the fun i"
mental hues, of the spectrum are allied, and
fragmentary forms should assume soarklW
colors. F llu h
Pay no attention to those hide-bound
teachers who tell you that -you should re
present things as they are. It is the mis
sion of every ambitious Cubist to deliver
things from their commonplace appearance
as, for instance, Gleizes has delivered his
“Trees.”'
TREES,
by Albert Gleizes.
From the fact that the object is truly
transubstantiated, so that the most accus-
tomed eye has some difficulty in discovering
it, a great charm results. The picture
which only surrenders itself slowly seems
always to wait until we interrogate it, as
though it reserved an infinity of replies to
an infinity of questions.
But do not hope to paint cubic master-
pieces without the aid of familiar symbols.
You have to decide whether you should
scatter these symbols all through your work,
mix them intimately with personal symbols,
or boldly exhibit them, magical discords,
shreds of the great collective lie, at a single
point of the place of the higher reality
which you appropriate to your art. If you
painting a cow. you will find it neces-
sary tb circumnavigate that cow, in order to
seize several of its successive-- appearances,
then you must fuse these northern, eastern,
southern and western aspects into a single
image, thus “reconstituting it in time.” Only
in that cubic way will you be able to pre-
sent the Cow, all the Cow and nothing but
the Cow. (The celebrated cubist master-
piece of a nude descending a staircase really
does show a nude descending a staircase,
though the artist had to paint 28 nudes and
11 staircases and put them all into the one
picture before he succeeded in “reconstitut-
ing” the lady “in time.”)
J*
The final lesson is to disregard the crowd.
The (iltimate aim of painting is to touch
the crowd; but painting must not address
the crowd in the language of the crowd; it
must employ its own language, in order to
move, dominate and direct the crowd, not
in order to be understood. “The artist who
concedes nothing, who does not explaiii him-
self and relates nothing, accumulates an
internal strength whose radiance shines on
every hand.”
That radiance you may acquire if you
take our Cubist Correspondence Course.
Particulars and fees on application.

diet. The volume is clearly and convinc-

verious verse
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Old Portrait.
“Life is brief, soon our hearts grow old —
Let’s be merry and jest a bit!
Sin and sorrow? ’Tis mostly gold,
Woman’s heart the rare gem in it!”
So I said, and your eyes flamed, once —
Flamed and flashed in a dazing way—
Once when I was the dullest dunce. . . .
Brown-eyed girl, did the dreams decay?
You went your way, and I, too, then
Learned life’s lessons in many schools,
Loved fair women and loved true men ;
Wise men laugh at the taunts of fools!
Some roam far to the tents of Shem,
Some grow famous —with rags of pride
Some go under ; who cares for them?. . . .
Time sweeps on in his heartless stride.
I have lived as the common clay,
Walked where thousands have walked
before,
Poor in pocket and fame as they;
Rich, perhaps, in a thought,the more;
I have sorrowed ; and loved and sinned,
Groped in darkness and walked in light,
Sang with angels in rain and wind,
Fought with devils of storm and night!
Old friends, sweethearts, long dead and gone
Into the limbo of past delights,
Haunt the dusk as I linger on,
Dreaming dreams of mid-summer nights.
Bright eyes beckon and lure —ah, once
None so beckoned and lured as they ! —-
Once when I was the dullest dunce. . ..
Brown Eyes, where fled the dreams away?
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
The Moons of March.
If Ju..e should hold a windless night
Filled with the heralding of rain
The moons of March shall shine again.
These alien hills shall know the light
That lit the forest on the plain,
If June should hold a windless night
Filled with the heralding of rain.
And nought shall rob me of this sight,
And nought shall shield me from this pain.
If June should hold a windless night
Filled with the heralding of rain
The moons of March shall shine again.
Victoria.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.
An Afternoon Reverie.
The seas are wide and hazy,
They have no heart to leap ;
I feel too glad and lazy
To even want to sleep;
I am as goddesses of old
Who lay on clouds uncamisoled.
The bright sun is a sinner.
He wantons with my hair;
The breeze with fingers thinner
Fondles me everywhere ;
Arcadian lovers these who play
In sight of George the King’s highway.
A mad car wildly speeding
Spins through a dust of gold,
The drowsy bee is feeding
Where crimson petals fold;
But I, amid soft cushions set,
Live, breathe, am happy, and forget.
When sea-born Cytherea
First saw the earth and sky
She looked about to see a
Nice grassy place to dry ;
And, spreading out her splendid hair,
Lay down without a thing to wear.
Her white doves came to flutter
Around on snowy wings,
She lay in joyance utter
Of sweet imaginings;
And over her that sun of old
Scattered his laughter edged with gold.
The shining lord of day meant
As well as he could mean ;
And if I wear some raiment
And am not beauty’s queen,
I understand the kind intent
Of all his present merriment.
There is a gentle madness
In everything around,
A thin, etherial badness
That makes no stir or sound;
A sort of fine indecency
Of insolence that pleases me.
Because I am a woman,
Because my lips are red,
A touch of something human
Is still about me shed ;
In all the earth, and sea, and sky
Tall lovers on my beauty spy.
My arm that sunbeam misses
Which fell against my dress,
But I can feel the kisses
The little breezes press
About my forehead crisp and clear
With whispers no one else can hear.
The seas are glad and hazy.
They scorn a watch to keep.
So fair, and faint, and lazy,
I think they are asleep ;
But I must rouse me from my dream
For things are other than they seem.
However nice the sea be,
However good the hour,
I must arise as Hebe
And dip my arms in flour ;
Yea, even toil as Mary Ann
To please six large brown feet of man.
To toil as any slavey
With baking dish and pot,
Producing paste and gravy
To serve it piping hot —
I wonder if amid the stars
Venus has things to cook for Mars.
jy.s.w.
MARY McCOMMON WEALTH.

BUYING-OFF THE BUMP.
Westralia has raised its third recent small loan, each one being on worse terms than its predecessor. The Premier, Mr. Seaddan. explains that if he had not accepted
the terms he sould have been compelled to close down upon railway construction, and upon the Agricultural Bank, the demands upon which were tremendous. —Daily paper.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the liditor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 20, 1913.
THE BEEF TRUST, AUSTRALIA AND
THE EMPIRE.
It does the average Australian Liberal a lot
of good to get away from Australia. He
finds in a little while that the distant Brit-
ish fields are not nearly so green as they
used to appear to him during his annual
Empire Day address at the Dog’s Leg Creek
Public School. He is made to realise that
th§ strikers of other lands are often more
unreasonable than those of Australia; also
that the poor are poorer, and the rain wetter,
and the cold colder, and things generally a
deal less alluring. About the same time he
begins, usually, to shed a lot of the political
clothing without which he would not have
appeared before an Australian audience for
a fortune. The working of this immutable
law has mysteriously changed G. H. Reid,
Freetrader and Conservative, until he not
only believes that one of the world’s biggest
Trusts is menacing protected Australia, but
that it threatens also to do what revenue-
tariffists have declared to be impossible
hitherto—namely, to get a footing in the
home of Cobdenism. Here are some extracts
from an interview with him :
The effect of all that I have learnt of the opera-
tions of the Beef Trust is that the danger from it is
increasing. . . .They [the Trust bosses] are prepared
to give any amount of money to secure tenancies of
any of the stalls at the Smithfield Meat Market in
London.... This obviously has a motive beyond ordin-
ary business. .. .The men most interested in the Aus-
tralian trade, as buyers from the pastoralists and
exporters to Great Britain, are very nervous concern-
ing the outlook in Australia and in London. They
say that a dead set is being made at them. They
are particularly nervous over the recent develop-
ments of the Swifts in Australia. I can quite under-
stand their anxiety from all I could learn in the
United States.... The Trust will be more than
liberal to the pastoralists in the beginning. But
if it manages to knock out Australian buyers, the
pastoralists will find they will have to pay back in
future low prices every penny of the enhanced price
obtained when the Trust first came upon the scene.
These Trusts are not out to benefit any but them-
selves. When they give a favor you may be sure
that you or someone else will have to pay for it—
and a good deal added, too.
In brief, G. H. Reid, who is in a unique
position to know, believes that the U.S. Beef
Trust is here, and that its main object is
to. corner the meat supply of the United
Kingdom—a fact which The Bulletin has
been mentioning repeatedly, amidst cries of
dissent and derision from Liberalism, for the
past three years.
The vast Yankee combine has no ijnme-
diate use, as this paper has often stated, for
the Australian trade. It is too small. The
objective is for the present the huge collec-
tive stomach of the United Kingdom. So
long as Australia remains free from the
Trust’s tentacles, Britain is safe. Not till
the Commonwealth and Maoriland —the only
cattle-raising countries outside Europe that
are free from the control of the Swifts and
the rest- —fall into line with the United States
and the Argentine, will the Beef Trust be
enabled to fix the price of meat daily for
Britain at Smithfield, as it now fixes it for
the U.S. in Chicago. G. H. Reid was not
speaking in his recent warning wholly as an
Australian. He was speaking more as one
who has spent five pleasant years in Eng-
land ; who has been hospitably treated there
and consequently feels a natural sentiment
of liking for the inhabitants ; and who hopes
to. return there. When he said that “any-
thing like Trust control of the poorest of
the people is repulsive to one,” he meant not
the Australian people but the British people.
It may be admitted frankly that the Aus-
tralian producer and consumer may not be-
gin to suffer from the operations of the Trust
for years to come. Yet nothing is surer than
that, ultimately, Australia will be required
to face its Calvary. As G. H. Reid, after
personal experience in America of a sort
that is not available to men outside official
positions, remarked :
These Trusts are not out to benefit any but them-
selves. Thev are out to crush anyone who competes
with them, and, once in command, to get as much
as they can.
* * » *
So from every point of view, whether as
a separate entity or as part of the Empire,
it behoves Australia to get busy in this mat-
ter. Let it be understood, from the very
jump, that, as the law stands, the Trust that
G. H. Reid fears, is immune from danger, so
far as the Commonwealth authorities are
concerned. According to the Privy Council
decision in the Vend case, the Government
is required to prove to the satisfaction of a
court of law that the combine which is being
proceeded against has acted “with intent to
restrain trade to the detriment of the
public.” If there was ever a chance to
prove intentional detriment it existed in the
Vend case. The Crown had here the ap-
parent good luck to get hold of the minute
books of the combine —documents which
proved to the satisfaction of normal human
beings,. if not to that of a court of law, that
the price of coal had been raised artificially
in four Australian States; that a shipping
firm which defied the Vend had been made
insolvent by it; that there had been short
delivery of coal on the part of the Vend, and
likewise delivery of coal of a sort that had
not been ordered. The Privy Council laid
it down that there was no intent to injure
the public in all this —though it was ’ ad-
mitted that the public had been injured, as
an incidental of the combine’s manoeuvres.
Similarly, the Privy Council would certainly
lay it down that intentional detriment was
not to be assumed if the Beef Trust raised
prices, against the local consumer ; drove
recalcitrant pastoralists, meat purveyors,
etc., out of the business; delivered bad
meat where good had been specified, and in
general behaved as Trusts mostly do when
they find themselves in a sufficiently strong
position. Nor could intentional detriment
be legally argued from the fact that the
Trust, having got a footing at Smithfield
markets, used its control of the Australian
trade to charge Britain’s 60-odd millions of
meat-eaters any price it pleased for one of
their staple articles of diet. Who will
question, in the above circumstances, that
the Federal law, as it stands, can be laughed
at with impunity by the combine which has
installed itself in Queensland?
In the course of debate in the Australian
Parliament the other day the Attorney-
General exclaimed :
You suggest that because somebody called Swift
is said to be a member of a Trust that carries on all
sorts of iniquities in America, that company should
not be allowed to operate in Queensland, as" long as
it observes the law?
Irvine was plainly scandalised at the bare
idea that Parliament should “exercise its
power against particular persons,” or deal
injuriously with any individual company “so
long as it observes the law.” Yet here is
the germ of the one scheme that is likely to
prove effective in scotching the dan-
gerous combine that menaces not only Aus-
tralia, but the United Kingdom as well.
The indictment of G. H. Reid suggests that
no measures can well be too strong. Recent
history shows that none that we have at our
command are of the least avail. In
such circumstances it would be per-
fectly legitimate for the Australian
Parliament to pass a special Act in-
sisting that any business carried on by
Swifts, Armours and the rest of the com-
bine-members, either ■ personally or through
their agents, should be deemed to be a Trust
till the contrary is proved. This would force
the. parties into a witness-box with all their
business documents, to disclose on oath
exactly what they are up to. It may be a
drastic measure, but there are some things
that can only be assailed with heavy
weapons—a reason why people never go
tieer-hunting with air-guns.
HOUSE FAMINE.
In its struggles with the problem of the
Increased Cost of Living, Justice Heydon’s
Court, sitting in Sydney, lately got as far
as the matter of house famine. This was
interesting, being in some degree a local
business which might possibly be controlled
by local legislation, whereas the inquiry
into the increased cost of food was, to a
great extent, international, and to some
extent Plain Drivel. The world’s population
is increasing faster than the world’s food
supply. There are now at least eight
populous countries bidding for the surplus
victuals of those countries which have a
surplus, whereas 70 years ago there was
only one—sometimes not even one. The
buying countries are becoming more and
the selling ones are growing less. The
dealer won’t sell material for a loaf for
2d. in Sydney if London is holding out a
claw-like hand with 4d. in it. And a coun-
try which has to send away such a heap
ot substance to pay interest on its
foreign debt should be the last to complain
if prices go up so that 14 bushels of wheat
will pay interest on £lOO worth of lOU
instead of 28 bushels being required. When
a country has got into the blessed condition
of paying its foreign bills with much less
gram than of old, any inquiry into the
matter is a case of touching the paint to
see if it is dry, or blowing down the gun to
see if it is loaded, or looking into the Ark
like the famous men of Beth-shemesh to
discover if it contains any luggage, or ex-
ploring the Holy of Holies as Pompey did
in one of his inflated moments, after which
he had no more luck. Doubtless something
heroic might be done if the Australian
Government constituted itself the sole buyer,
at a fixed price, of everything in the way of
food that Australia produces, and sold to
the local consumer at a low figure, and sold
f. e surplus to the foreign consumer at a
high figure, and thus made two distinct
prices. But even if the Government which
attempted the scheme didn’t happen to be
rushed with an axe by the maddened pro-
ducer, the scheme would require such a vast
organisation that it is out of the range of
immediate politics. In a thinly peopled
land where many people don’t even see the
Government once in six months a public
monopoly of food would be an elaborate
business.
Still, as the writer was trying to men-
tion before he drifted away into a tangled
waste of side issues, the N.S.W. Cost of
laving Commission lately reached the ques-
tion of house famine. That is to say, it
reached the question of house famine in
Sydney that being the only place which
sufters from the disease to any great extent.
(Bourke, for instance, has got tenant
™me, and a person who even looks as
though he might want a house has to keep
landlords off with a gun.) Rents in the
suburbs of the Ma capital have an upward
tendency. Landlords, just like wharf lab-
orers and grocers and auctioneers, take as
much as they can get, and as houses aren’t
increasing in proportion to the increase of
population landlords can sometimes get a
good deal. The bloated owner in these days of
corpulence is unusually arrogant about re-
pairs, which, after all, is a verv ancient
and frequent symptom in times" of pros-
perity. Between Antiochus, of Syria, who
was so blinded by prosperity as to think he
could blot out Rome, and John Smith, of
Botany, who, in the madness of his pride,
adds 2s. 6d. to the rent of the dilapidated
three-roomed wooden cottage, the difference
is only one of degree. The same might be
said of Ajax defying the lightning at one
end of the scale, and Patrick Hogan defy-
ing the Arbitration Court at the other
Then, again, it is alleged, on the best of
authority, that very many high-priced
hovels in Sydney and its suburbs should be
written off as uninhabitable. They certainly
should—whole streets, if not whole munici-
palities of them. But they are the natural
result of a squalid casual Legislature, and
a municipal system which allows some 40
petty collections of mayors and aldermen
and town clerks to run a great city in their
own petty and incompetent way. Very few
peonle are much better than they are com-
pelled to be, and the landlord is a case in
point.
* * *
If the house famine wasn’t so acute the
pressure of competition would drive the
Jaudlord to reform, despite the slackness of
the 40 municipal councils and the sluggish
habits of the State Legislature. But the
scarcity is here, and must be reckoned with.
The frantic borrow and boom and conges-
tion policy of the Holman-McGowen
Government has set Sydney growing at an
unparalleled rate. The borrow and the
boom and the expansion pushed the price of
allotments sky-high a year or two ago,
though they are hardly so sky-high to-day,
for the boom is obviously beginning to
crack in places. The sudden demand for
high-priced labor on public works paid for
out of loans, and the general boom effect,
made toilers scarce and dear. Everything
wa* inflated and sudden and artificial'. The
house-shark considered the possibilities in-
volved in building cottages on top-price
allotments, with top-price labor and top-
price materials, and then letting them at
slump rates when the loan-boom perished.
He considered the chances of a Fair Rents
Court, which annoyed him greatly, seeing
that he wanted unfair rents as long as he
could get them. He viewed the prospect of
building for a fast-growing population,
which might soon be a falling population.
Then a great many of him sat down hard
on the houses he already possessed, and ab-
stained from repairs.
In ancient days Jacob, who is described
as a smooth man and probably knew his
way about in consequence, dwelt in tents.
He wasn’t troubled by the spasmodic rise
in the hire of a three-roomed wooden shack
at Botany. Now the tent-dwelling habit
has, to a great extent, died out, and the
rent of the shack is a matter of conse-
quence. Until we have public ownership of
cities, and the closed ports are opened, and
there is more than one big city in each
State for the worker to live in, and Govern-
ments cease to make sudden booms by
means of sudden loan frenzies, and leave off
constructing sudden slumps by means of
sudden failures in loan flotation, rent must
needs be a matter of shocking unreliability.
The landlord is by nature a beast of nrev
yet there are excuses for his prey and hU
beastliness. The grocer and the butcher
can cease giving credit to the man who
doesn t pay, and write off the old bad debt
and the man who doesn’t pay seldom comes
along and wrecks the shop because of the
stoppage of supplies. The landlord must
keep on giving credit to his bad tenant until
he can get him out by lengthy legal process
and if the tenant uses the back fence for
firewood in the interval there is little re
dress, for the tenant can always sav that
the fence was destroyed by the bov next
door The private landlord is a gambler
tor high stakes, and like most experienced
gamblers he is cautious. Therefore he is
apt to hold off when the tail end of the
boom is in sight, and the tail end of the
•N.b.W. loan boom is visible nowadays to
the naked eye. Even ex-Premier McGowen
who has always had a craze for putting his
honest savings into house property isn’t
building much at present.
LIBERALISM GETS ANOTHER
INSTALMENT OF SENSE.
The Commissioner who was appointed to
inquire into the working of Industrial Arbi-
tration in N. S. Wales presented his report
last week. There is nothing revolutionary
about the proposals which he makes, nor
are his comments on the existing industrial
situation distinguished by novelty. Most
of his suggestions and criticisms are, in-
deed, years old. Yet they are startling by
reason of their source, and by the reception
they have met with in unexpected quarters.
Had he made the more important of them in
the days when John See was Premier, he
would have been condemned by Liberalism
for a madman ; and had he voiced some of
them during the many elections and bye-
elections which were fought largely on the
Arbitration issue he would have been de-
scribed as an Anarchist. As a case in point,
Commissioner Piddington disparages the
clumsy Wages Boards system—a system on
which Liberalism based the whole of its
hopes a few years ago. Also he recommends
the appointment of more Arbitration Judges.
And finally he expresses the opinion that
arbitration, though it has not prevented
every strike (any more than our penal laws
have prevented every crime), has been, on
the whole, a success. Whereat the S. M.
Herald, the one-time deadliest foe of this
variety of “Socialism” :
It is satisfactory to learn that, despite all the
assertions that arbitration has proved a failure, there
is no considerable body of opinion among employers
or employees in favor of abandoning it.
Further:—
It is satisfactory that the Commissioner does not
find our present arbitration system, evolved with so
much labor, to be all in vain, but that he thinks
that with a few modifications it can be made to
fulfil its purpose successfully.
Which is equivalent, really, to the statement
that Liberalism is delighted to find that its
original criticisms of this vast reform prove
Liberalism to be a short-sighted ass.
* * * *
The earlier history of the movement, when
read in relation to the present position, illu-
minates brightly the absurdity of paying
serious attention to Toryism’s fears and
protests and warnings on any question. In
1904 the See Administration announced in
the N.S.W. Legislative Assembly its inten-
tion to amend the then Act. It proposed,
in the first place, to appoint two Arbitra-
tion Judges instead of one. It suggested
that they should be given larger powers;
amongst others, the power to appoint sub-
sidiary tribunals to deal with industrial
details—those details which, as Commissioner
Piddington notes, are continually getting in
the way of useful work at the present time.
And thirdly, and chiefly, it proposed to allow
industrial agreements to be made privately
and without the parties being required to go
before the Courts. This, of course, was the
original intention of Parliament and of the
framers of the Act. Unfortunately, that
most formidable adversary, Chief Justice
Griffith, defeated it. A certain agreement
had been made in amicable privacy between
an employer on the one hand and
a set of employees on the other; and it had
been registered as such in the Industrial
Arbitration Court. One of the parties asked
the Court to make it a common rule of the
industry. An employer in the particular line
of business affected took out a prohibition
order. The N. S. Wales Full Court, pre-
sided over by the late Chief Justice Darley,
held that voluntary agreements were in
every way legal under the Act, and bumped
out the protesting employer accordingly. He
went to the High Court, which said that it
was not permissible for employers and em-
ployees to make an industrial agreement be-
tween themselves unless there had previously
been an industrial dispute ; also that an in-
dustrial agreement could not be enforced by
means of a common rule. Up to this time
fully twQrthirds of the State’s employers
and employees had been toiling, with com-
plete satisfaction to all concerned, under
voluntarily-made agreements. The per-
nicious, not to say insane, effect of Grif-
fith’s decision was to drive the whole of
them to court, with the result that the busi-
ness of that institution was thrown into a
state of chaos, and that of the general com-
munity likewise. And every day, and every
week from the date of the decision in ques-
tion, the situation got worse.
It was at this critical stage that the Party
Government issue obtruded itself —a chronic
habit, by the way. with that most deplorable
issue. The insanity of arbitration in all its
forms was as much a battle-cry of Liberalism
in those days as the imbecility of local
defence was to become a while later. The
Act was left unamended by the Carruthers
Government (which succeeded the See
Administration) as a matter of political tac-
tics ; and it was administered with deliberate
lack of sympathy, in the hope that it would
break down completely, and thus discourage
the electors with the whole principle of
arbitration. Meanwhile the Liberal press
pounded fiercely at the Act with the same
object, and at every Liberal meeting and in
every prominent club and pub the futility
of arbitration and the advisability of getting
back to the grand old strike method of
settling differences were insisted on vehe-
mently. The campaign was a failure, to a
certain extent, nevertheless. The common
sense of the constituencies triumphed, inas-

be disregarded by any office-seeking politician
of sense, was delivered, requiring, if not the
particular form of arbitration then in vogue,
at least some form of the alleged insanity.
Liberalism then bowed its head and intro-

Here, then, is a case where the whole
gamut of conservative inconsistency has
been negotiated. Arbitration is condemned
in the beginning as wildly impracticable,
Socialistic, and in every way infamous.
After determined efforts have been made to
kill it, Liberalism accepts it grudgingly, and
ultimately dowers the State with it in an
emasculated form. The final stage is for
this amazing party to announce, by its jour-
nalistic spokesmen, that arbitration of the
whole-hearted, comprehensive brand which
was advocated by alleged anarchists nine
years ago is the only thing to save the country
—a form of arbitration that puts several
Judges at the head of affairs ; that practi-
cally disregards the “free worker” ; and that
makes the hated principle of preference to
unionists an essential to success. It is this
sort of thing that encourages vastly
the advocates of progressive measures, inas-
much as it enables them to realise that in
due time such reforms as Unification and
genuine decentralisation will be advocated
by the party that has fought them most bit-
terly hitherto.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Coining Liquor Polls in N. S.
Wales.
The triennial clamor prior to the taking of
the local option polls in N. S. Wales is
over the land once more, and all is vague
argument and confusion of facts and figures.
As in the political party system and the
strike lunacy, so in the liquor struggle, the
general public is a secondary consideration.
The No-license cranks are fighting like fury
to batten up the bars, ostensibly because
they are bars; anyhow, their chief gunner
(the Rev. Hammond) fired a volley the
other night in. the Town Hail, to the effect
that it was not the desire of the Alliance
to interfere with the private drinking habits
of the people. Which is plain deception or
rank folly- In Maoriland, while they
were working up their strength, the
No-license leaders encouraged the popu-
lace to believe that the fight was
only against the public bars, and
the blue-ribbon Jeremiah who went around
wailing for the whole hog of Prohibition
was urged to bag his head and not spoil
the pitch. But as soon as the pitch was
ready, or the time was ripe, or the potato
cooked, or something, there was a prompt
call to the politicians to stand and de-
liver national prohibition as an issue on the
ballot paper, or lose their political exist-
ence. That much is evidence of deception
in the statement that the No-license party
is “not out to interfere with the private
habits of the people, but to endeavor to
remove the public sale of liquor.” If there
is no deception, then the scheme must be to
concentrate the guzzling of liquor in the
homes of the people; and if that is the policy
of the N. S. Wales Alliance, The Bulletin
hastens to the grindstone with its biggest
axe. On the other side, the liquor trade is
battling like a one-eyed giant for the preser-
vation of its own business and the hundreds
of thousands of pounds sunk in it. So the
mere voter is given a choice of voting for
the fellow who wants to shut the licensed
drinking houses and the person who desires
to keep them open. As to whether the
public would like to express an opinion on
any other phase of the great liquor ques-
tion, nobody seems to care a tinker’s panni-
kin. Thousands of citizens would vote for
a trial of State or municipal sale and con-
trol of alcohol if an opportunity were given ;
but as (11 the No-license party would howl
in anguish at such an issue being offered
to the voter on any pretence; and (2) the
brewery party would elevate an ear to it
only if it tinkled with a promise of com-
pensation ; and (3) the politician who lives
by votes is afraid of the big battalions,_ the
State-control advocates are disfranchised.
Thus local option, to at least that extent,
is a fraud. And its results are a fraud as
they stand. They are a fraud in the
United States, Canada and Maoriland; and
they would be a fraud in Australia. Side
by side with a spreading area of closed bars
in the Land of the Wooden Nutmeg and the
ex-home of the Moa, the consumption of
liquor has steadily increased. In Maine
(U.S.) the law of prohibition can be car-
ried out only in complaisant rural districts.
A well-known prohibition sheriff in that
State, Rev. S. F. Pearson, put this on
record:—
We got the law.... And then we said, “We are
safe now. The law will protect us.’’....What has
been done? With the law have we been able to
abolish the saloons? No! Drunkenness is on the
increase, especially among the young men. We are
utterly powerless to take the saloon away from
drinking men.
In the towns, such as Portland, the asinine
law sits crowned with a fool’s cap. Arthur
von Briesen, president of the Legal Aid
Society of New York, in the course of an
address at the end of 1910, described Port-
land as “a. city of Jaw-breakers.” Admiral
Evans, of the U.S. Navy, declared to the
New York World that nowhere did he
have such trouble with drunken sailors as
in the harbors of Portland. In Maoriland,
the drink bill has grown from £2.265,900
to £4,081,162 since Na-license became the
fashion nearly 20 years ago. In 1895,
with only one “dry” electorate, the
expenditure per inhabitant was £3 os. 5d.;
in 1912, with 12, it was £3 14s. llid. It
has been argued by No-license advocates
that the value of liquor consumed in the
barless areas is not a quarter as much as it
is in the districts where the bars are open.
Buj; there is no way of getting at the posi-
tion. For instance, if No-license were car-
ried in the King division in Sydney, the
amount of liquor taken into and consumed
there by the residents (presuming it could
be checked —which it can t) would be vastly
reduced. But that wouldn’t account by a
long way for the drinks purchased by King
division residents while on pleasure or busi-
ness in adjacent “wet” divisions; So in
Maoriland ; and there are all sorts of other
channels through which alcohol runs un-
checked into the arid places. Sly grog is
one of the worst, although it may not be
one of the most prolific. And there are no
means of learning the extent of it. Yet
this much is ascertainable from the Year
Book for 1912 —the fines inflicted in 1910
throughout the Dominion amounted to
£1860; in 1911 they blew up to £2670. If
the object of the Alliance is to dis-
cover the effect upon a country when
its liquor is cut off there is only
one way to do it. That is to prohibit
the sale, manufacture, importation and con-
sumption of every kind and breed of in-
toxicating drink. It would be a stupendous
job, but it might be done with an army of
spies, informers, police, coast-guardsmen,
Customs officials, etc. If that is not the
object of all the rumpus, much valuable
time is being wasted. Any scheme which
merely closes the bars on one side of the
street and leaves them open on the other,
or closes them on both sides of the street
and starts off an endless procession of kegs,
barrels, bottles, flasks, demijohns, cases and
so on to private houses, “blind tigers,” sea-
shore caves, vacant lots and dens of all de-
scriptions is a fool of a scheme, and no
solution of the liquor problem at all.
On the Danger of Investing All Police-
men with Imperishable Haloes.
This paper has no disrespect whatever for
the Svdney policeman. Considering his pay
and his fewness, he does rather creditably,
year in, year out. But he is not an arch-
angel; in fact, he is rarely so much as an
ordinary hedge-saint, and for magistrates to
treat him as though he were is fraught with
all sorts of danger to law-abiding citizens.
Consider, as a horrible example, a legal
disturbance which was staged at the Cen-
tral Police Court the other day. Constable
J. H. Wilson (14st. 121 b.) charged a con-
tractor named Ward (7st. 131 b.) with hav-
ing wantonly assaulted him while under
the spell of liquor. Ward’s rebutting
story was, briefly, as follows. He was on aLa
Perouse tram, in a condition of utter sobriety.
Wilson, who was also on the tram, was
in uniform, likewise, so Ward says, in drink.
He used (still according to Ward’s story)
an entire box of matches in a futile effort
to light his pipe, and in other ways adver-
tised his condition to the derisive multi-
tude. Ward betrayed a certain pardonable
merriment. Wilson left his own compart-
ment And came to Ward’s. Arrived there
he addressed unprintable remarks to the
seven-stone citizen, threatened to arrest
him, smote him about the head, and ulti-
mately did arrest and charge him as above-
mentioned. Now, the writer doesn’t pretend
to have been at the hearing. He has nothing
but a newspaper report to go on. He well
appreciates that one witness may create a
good impression and another the reverse,
and that the magistrate’s decision may be
biased quite legitimately thereby. But
in this Ward-WiLson affray, unless what
look like ample reports are grossly mislead-
ing, there seems to have been no room for
doubt as to who was the inebriated nuisance,
and who the inoffensive and sober citizen. A
number of total strangers—amongst them
the conductor of the tram, the driver, and
half a dozen passengers—testified that Ward
did, nothing of a provocative nature except
to warn the person whose matches were
being borrowed that he was in for a match-
famine, or waggish words to that effect. On
the other hand, they swore —as one witness,
practically —that Wilson was drunk, that
he came into Ward’s car and hit him, that
his language was awful, and that his action
in arresting Ward was utterly uncalled for
and unjust. Incidentally, the the tramway
emuloyees swore that Wilson had tried
later |p square them. W ILSON called not
one single witness from the tram to speak
on his behalf; but he got a couple of doc-
tors and two pdliqemen to say that, when
they sa ic him (whicfji was some time after
tli'e row), he was sober. These same autho-
rities, by the way, were agreed on the point
that Ward was also sober. In order,
therefore, for the jpffigistrate to be able to
swallow Wii.soN'st version of the affair, he
was required to bfelieve (1) thgt a 7-stone
citizen, who was sober, wantonly hurled
himself at a 14-stone constable in uniform,
poured obscene language all over him, and
hit him with an umbrella; (2) that the
constable had done nothing to merit the
assault; (3) that two reputable tram at-
tendants, and a number of presumably re-
putable tram-travellers, perjured themselves
in defence of a quarrelsome little man whom
they did not know from Abraham ; (4)
that the same people conspired together to
ruin a stainless policeman who had already
been sorely mishandled in the execution of
his duty; 15) that, though the policeman
did not produce so much as one human
being, respectable or otherwise, out of the
whole tram-load, to stand up on liis behalf,
and say that the others were liars and
criminals, he was, nevertheless, a sober officer
who had acted correctly throughout. Yet Mr.
Smithers, S.M., was able to find, “on the
evidence of the doctor” (who, as aforesaid,
had not seen the constable till after that
most sobering of experiences, a public row),
that Wilson was sober. Also, he felt
called upon to hand him a warm testi-
monial on the subject of his “absolute so-
briety.” And though he discharged the
accused, he let it be understood that his
(the accused’s) conduct had laid him open
to profound suspicion; and at least one of
the latter’s witnesses was warned gravely
that he had “overstated the case.” The
absurdly exaggerated attention which most
Sydney magistrates pay to the evidence of
constables has been a growing scandal for
some time. In the case under discussion, it
seems that the limit has about been reached.
If a policeman can earn a public testi-
monial from the Bench after behaving as
witnesses swore Wilson behaved, he can
do pretty well anything of a violent nature
with complete impunity. And that state of
affairs constitutes a very serious peril to
the frequent sort of citizen to whom so much
as one police-court expose may mean ruin.
The Nationalisation of Medicine.
A Collins-street doctor —and a confessed
Tory at that —writing recently to the Age,
roundly condemned the Cook Govern-
ment’s Insurance scheme on the ground that
it is designed to make provision only for
that section of the population which mostly
is able and willing to make provision for it-
self, while neglecting the “uninsurable
class”—the nomads and casual workers, who
at present lean, to a great extent, on char-
ity. Furthermore, the Cook scheme must
hugely and inequitably advantage the in-
surable class by making employers and
the State pay the greater part of the said
classes’ sick benefits. Wherefore the
Anti-Socialist medico declared straight
out for socialised medicine, backing
up his proposition by these axioms and de-
ductions : —Nearly all disease originates in
the poorer sections of the population, the un-
insurables; filth and filth infection cause
nine-tenths of the preventable deaths occur-
ring before the age of 65; the uninsurable
citizen is a constant menace to the public
health ; it is impossible for a community to
be healthy so long as the poorer citizens are
afflicted; therefore all efforts to ameliorate
society should concern themselves with the
eradication of disease rather than with its
mere treatment. To carry out such a policy
the 6000 doctors in Australia should, he
believes, be organised into an army of
health, as public servants. Starting such
public servants at, say, £2OO a year, work-
ing up through an average of £4OO per an-
num to a maximum of, say, £l5OO, and with
the inducement of much more highly-paid
administrative positions, worth from £3OOO
to £5OOO, this would mean an annual outlay
by the State of between £2,500,000 and
£3,000,000. It looks a large sum, but more
than that is paid now. It would give an
average of one doctor to every 800 persons—
a better service than that of any other
country. And they would be preventive,
instead of mere curative, agents. To put
the item of cost another way, it would mean
6d. per head per week of the adult popula-
tion, while a further 3d. per head would
finance all our existing hospitals and pro-
vide every district section of the national
medical service with an up-to-date, highly-
equipped receiving hospital and clinic. The
benefits would be a gift at the price, for
10 years of this preventive system of fighting
disease would see much of our prevailing
disease and destitution disappear, and our
death-rate would sink to the irreducible
minimum. It is a fine, large vision* -jand
nothing stands in the way of its realisation
except ancient custom and a lack of educa-
tion.

Pubs and a Great Opportunity.
“Larrakeeyah” (who knows his Northern
Territory) writes: —
I see that Dr. Gilruth has been decrying the
hotel accommodation of the Northern Territory. In
a sense he does well to be angry. But why doesn’t
he do something to procure a change for the better?
In March, 1912, when it was known that a Parlia-
mentary party was coming up this way, a movement
was set on foot to petition the Government for State
hotels. Why doesn’t Gilruth throw all his weight
in this direction ? Many of us in the N.T.—not
Wowsers, or teetotal cranks, or wild-eyed Socialists
or other freaks, but just plain, observant citizens —
are all for complete State control of the importation
and sale of intoxicants, with the erection of State
hotels where required, and compensation to existing
businesses. This movement is, however, bitterly
opposed by the greater part of the local A.W.A.
The ground of Socialist opposition to a Socialist
measure would be difficult to define., but these seem
to be the most logical of the objections: (1) It
is supported by some business men who are known
to be anti-Socialists; (2) it is supported by some
known-to-be total abstainers, iwiuding the Anglican
and Methodist parsons. It is felt that only drunks
should hic-cough in support of such a measure, and
that anything supported by parsons should be opposed
on principle. And so the edifying spectacle has been
presented of a Socialist audience pelting the advo-
cates of a Socialistic measure. If the Socialist party
has any faith in its own principles, the Northern
Territory offers a unique opportunity for carrying
out the policy of State control in its entirety. The
population is small, licensed houses comparatively
few, and oompensation as yet a simple problem.
Isolated State hotels in competition with Private
Enterprise do not solve the problem ; but, if firmly
and wisely managed, a complete system of State-con-
trolled liquor traffic in the Northern Territory should
be an object-lesson to Australia. It would strike a
deadly blow at the sly-grog sellers (mostly Chinese)
who flourish locally, and whom no amount of police
regulation can suppress. It is a disgraceful truth
that the Chinaman waxes fat in his illicit trade by
the support of many N.T. workers. In fact, he
never before reaped such a harvest as he has since
the advent of large numbers of White Australians
who, though they would “down tools” if asked to
work with a Chinaman, do not scruple to enter his
den and sell their principles for the difference
between the publican’s and the Chow’s bottle of
beer —about 2d. Under a system in which the State
is the sole importer of liquor the most lucrative
trade the Chinaman has in the country would be
taken from him. State control is not prohibition.
The. latter encourages the smuggler; the. former
1 leaves no room for him, for it meets all reasonable
demands, and guarantees purity of quality and fair-
ness of dealing. It is to be hoped that when
Andrew . Fisher cornea hack to office he will look
seriously at . this. question and have the courage
of his convictions, for the N.T. affords an oppor-
tunity to refute and confound the social reform
quack and demonstrate a sane and final solution of
a difficult problem.
“G.V.W.” : The Federation of Labor leaders
here in Maoriland have as much strategy
as a timber stack. For quite a while it
has been known that, with a sympathetic
Government in power, the federated em-
ployers have been anxious for a trial of
strength with the workers. And it is a
lamentable fact that a few rabid leaders
have hurried to oblige them. So far on the
road to insanity have some of these people
travelled that a faction of the strikers were
bent on preventing provisions being sent to
the seamen on the vessels anchored in Wel-
lington harbor. Yet, if the strike was to
succeed at all, the support of the seamen
was essential. The strikers had already
sufficient antagonism on hand as a result
of the slaughtermen’s strike. It was the
cocky who was made to suffer then instead
of the big meat cos., and the result is seen
in the enthusiasm with which large number?
of small farmers rushed to the help ol
Labor’s enemies in the subsequent trouble.
Again, when the strike leaders were refused
the use of the Basin Reserve by the Tory
Mayor of Wellington, they displayed more
of the same brand of tactlessness. To the
tune of the Marseillaise they marched to
the ground, tore down the locked gates,
trampled over everything, and badly dam-
aged a popular public reserve. Thus more
sympathy that might have proved valuable
was alienated. The subsequent speeches
made matters worse. One alleged leader
explained that the Federation of Labor had
been settling strikes for weeks, and it would
settle this one, even if “it meant setting the
country on fire.” He also announced that
“if special constables and members of the
Permanent Artillery were to be used he
would undertake to march on Wellington
with 10,000 or 15,000 armed men.” Like-
wise :
If the employers would act in a conciliatory spirit
it would be all right, but if they put on “scabs” to
load and discharge cargo into ships there would not
be any ships to leave port, and not a wharf for the
“scab” to work on.
I reckon the time is ripe to found a training
college for Labor leaders. There’s a whole
pyramid of business awaiting it.

“O’Phimerty” on an unpleasant topic:—
As one who has had considerable experience in the
hospitals of Victoria and Tasmania, I am with The
Bulletin and against “Xylem” (B. 30/10/’l3), in
regard to venereal diseases. Says “Xylem” : “The
more we treat syphilis as a disease of shame, the
less victims will it demand of our children.” The
average young fellow who gratifies his sexual instincts
through impure means has no conception of the risks
to which he exposes himself. One such informed
me he had been told that the complaint was “no
worse than a cold.” He had never heard of ataxia,
the frequent and awful resultant of syphilis. He did
not know that there was any possibility of imparting
the disease to offspring. His ignorance is typical of
thousands. Does “Xylem” seriously contend that the
dispelling of such ignorance will increase impure
sexuality ? Certainly treat syphilis (and gonorrhoea,
too, which is nearly as bad in its results,
and even more difficult to eradicate) as a
disease of shame, but not in the sense of leaving
youth ignorant about it. Rather let shame be
rational, based on ascertained knowledge. I cannot
understand why this should increase the number of
victims, any more than why the posting of a warning
should increase the number of people who fall over a
precipice. While on the subject, may I add that the
great danger in the treatment of syphilis is that it
is so easy by mercurial medicines to get rid of its
external signs? The patient then imagines he is
cured, and absents himself from treatment. As a
matter of fact the cure has scarcely begun, and in
the course of a few years there is a tragic price to
pay, sometimes in the victim himself, sometimes in his
children, often in both. I have seen human suffering
in many forms ; but I have seen nothing more fearful
than the effects of congenital syphilis. No man should
be discharged as cured until he has had at least two
years’ constant attention, with a rigorous examination
at the end.

The newly-formed Town Planning Associa-
tion of N. S. Wales deserves a hearty wel-
come, as it will bring together professional
and other kinds of brains which ought to
co-operate in turning the present ridiculous
plan of Inner Sydney topsy-turvy, while
laying down an Outer Sydney plan for the
future. Architects, surveyors, builders,
land-dealers, lawyers, planners and others
have the care of the new baby. The work
ahead of them is titanic. Sydney was
practically laid out by the convict wood-
cutter and the bullock waggon and the
wandering First-Fleet goat; hence a pic-
turesque bend in the main streets denotes
nothing more than the original site of a
bigger-than-usual gum-tree or a piece of
jutting sandstone. The objects of the new
body are:—
(1) To promote modern town planning in respect
of (a) reforming crudities in the older city plans,
(b) developing model suburbs, and (r) planting new
garden cities.
(2) Improvement of (a) civic architecture (Syd-
ney has a new stvle of architecture every 20ft..
nearly every one of them horrid), and (b) creating
manufacturing areas (Germany has done wonders in
this).
(3) Educating public opinion by lectures, giving
exhibitions, and by establishing a chair of town
planning in the Sydney University.
The sooner the work is put in hand, the
fewer the changes that will have to be made
when it is done.
The Flagship.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out of the busy Quay
The evening ferries glide
On a swiftly-ebbing tide
That seeks the outer sea.
Oh ! grey hour of release!
Grey is the evening sea ;
The flagship’s majesty
Blends with this hour of peace.
Mighty and stark it lies
At the helpless city’s feet;
In office, mart and street
The guarded daylight dies.
The guarded daylight dies !
How many days will go
Into the dead past so?
Tell us, you wjio are wise.
Turret, and bridge, and mast!
The winging, wand’ring gull
Glimmers along the hull.
Will it come to this at last?
Here is the price of peace !
Grey ship that blends with grey
Waters that may not stay,
Your vigil may not cease!
Is it too much to pay?
Can Freedom be too deal ?
Say while the ship is here —
Is Peace worth while to-day?
Who can assess a dream—
The dream of peace come true?
Not till our Hour falls due
And the first shrapnels scream.

The Greater J. D. Williams Amusement Co., Ltd. W. Barrington Miller, General Manager.
Archdeacon Boyce, of Sydney, passed the
polite contradiction to that cheap advertising
parson, S. D. Yarrington, on November 10.
In the previous week, this Yarrington
(the same who cried on the housetops that
he knew opium dens in Sydney where there
were white girls, but did not produce the
addresses of these places, so that the police
could break up the “joints”)—this Yarring-
ton, speaking at Granville, said :
The fact is revealed already that we have to face
the White Slave traffic in Sydney.
Whereafter Archdeacon Boyce informed
Premier Holman, as a responsible speaker
at a representative deputation, that
he - • not thin . k the .White Slave traffic yet
existed in Australia, but it might come into exist-
ence.
A couple of weeks ago, at Brisbane, the
sensation fakers of the Yarrington pattern
got a monumental set-back. One of them
wrote to the police that a mysterious man in
a motor-car had attempted to take away some
children who had just left a suburban State
School. The police ascertained (1) that the
car belonged to well-known people, who were
visiting a respectable resident’s At Home;
(2) that the school-girls, who had read the
contents of a circular (presumably issued by
some alleged Social Purity League), explain-
ing the repulsive business, ran back to the
school and told the teacher that “the White
Slave man was waiting on the road”; (3)
that the whole thing was baseless. Another
alleged attempted kidnapping of a girl
(crippled, at that) turned out to be an outing
for the child, with a number of others, at the
invitation of generous citizens and the Auto-
mobile Club. Another result of the afore-
mentioned lovely circulars of “warning” was
that two young girls attending one of the
high schools told a circumstantial story of
how a man in motor-car had attempted to
kidnap them. Under examination one of the
girls confessed that the. yarn was pure fiction,
prompted _ by the circulars referred to.
Another lie was that a girl had been asked
to deliver a letter at a certain address, and
that she had taken it home to her mother,
who opened it and read, “Detain bearer till
I come.” The police discovered that the
girl named in the complaint was a woman of
more than middle age, whose mother had
been dead for many years. This country
isn’t suffering from White Slavery; but its
share of mischievous and unpunished Black
Perjury appears to be phenomenal.
This is what actually happened to the
Hansard proof which was “corrected” by
Speaker Johnson, of the House of Reps.,
and led to the suspension of McGrath, of
Ballarat, and the disfranchisement of his
electorate for the rest of the session:—

McGrath told his Ballarat constituents:
“We have absolute proof that the Speaker
has deliberately altered a Hansard proof.
The proof showed that the third reading of
the Loan Bill was not carried, according to
his own words, and he altered the proof to
make it read that it was carried.” From
that McGrath deduced that the Speaker
was “acting in a biased manner,” and was
“a bitter partisan.” McGrath’s deductions
may or may not be right: that is a matter
of opinion. But as to the alteration of the
nroof there can be no doubt whatever; and
the only question left is whether McGrath’s
conclusion wasn’t at least a reasonable one
in the circumstances. To do Johnson jus-
tice, he apparently saw that it wasn’t an un-
reasonable one, and “hoped that the matter
would be allowed to drop.” It was Cook
with the bare majority, who hungered for
the Oppositionist’s suspension. It was Cook
who moved so furiously for the suspension
that, when Johnson hoped that the matter
would be allowed to drop, he turned on the
Speaker, and threw at him quite as bad an
insult as McGrath’s. Johnson comes out
of the business none too well, but, compared
with Cook’s, his part in the affair is simply
glorious.

The Quinlans produced “Lohengrin” in
Sydney on Monday night, with the usual
cuts. The original version, without the
cuts, performed at Weimar in 1850, with
Liszt in charge, is said to have “made a
powerful impression.” No doubt it did.
The audience got home with the morning
beer, too stunned to give Mrs. Weimar any
satisfactory explanation of its long absence.
In the Sydney production Spencer Thomas,
in spite of a few weak patches, was excel-
lent as the knight with the swan millinery.
He sang the lyric gems that fell to his lot
as Lohengrin with more charm and refine-
ment than Coates, who bellowed the farewell
to Elsa in the 1912 production here. Jeanne
Brola was a misfit in the part of Elsa of
Brabant. Comparisons are brutal; still it
must be said that the lovely Polish soprano
Wayda, the distressful Brabant damsel of
the Melba production, left a record which
wipes out competition. William Anderson,
as King Henry the Fowler, looked like the
King of Tarts all on a summer’s day. He
assembled with his nobles, a motley crowd,
on the banks of the Scheldt. Henry was
his own area officer, whipping up recruits in
a deep, bass voice, when the troubles of
Elsa and the evil doings of Telramund
( Parker) and his wife Ortrud (Edna
Thornton) caused him to smack the trunk
of a tree three times with his sword. Gra-
ham Marr was the best herald within recent
memory. He was a large edition bound in
scarlet, and carefully sub-edited by Con-
ductor Eckhold. Lohengrin, who shot green
a n d ®^ ver lights from a sheath costume of
fish-plate armor, arrived in a small punt
drawn by the mystic Swan. This useful bird
was ordered to return (if Elsa played up)
about 10 to 11 p.m., and was more
punctual than the Bondi tram. Lohengrin
expects his best girl—whose champion he is
—to take him on trust. He expects too
much, and departs, by fowl ferry, as already
mentioned, in an exnuisite burst of music.
“THE GIRL OF THE GOLDEN WEST,”
AT HER MAJESTY'S, SYDNEY.
A TENOR TO THE GOOD
The girl (Jeanne Brola) cheats in her
gamble with the sheriff (Robert Parker) and
wins Spencer Thomas. He's a tenor.
for in this opera Wagner condescended to
be tuneful. The chorus voices in the wed-
ding scene were beautifully balanced; the
pages (in magenta stockings), who walked
round the bridal pair, with lighted tapers in
their hands, deserve a large bouquet apiece
for their fine, gleeful singing; the orchestra
under Eckhold was in its best Wagnerian
form; and the stage trumpeters showed
signs of improvement.
Puccini’s “Girl of the Golden West” is
bellowdrama pure and simple. The bellow
is supplied by the principals, who have a
stormy evening with the orchestra. Vog-
hera, who conducted it in last year’s pro-
duction, repeated his old offence of drowning
two-thirds of the vocalisation. Nevertheless
“Boys, I am Stony !” and such lyric snaps as
‘Poor Dog Tray” and “Your Old Grandma
Is No More,” rose above the bang of the
drum. Parker repeated his fine performance
as Ranee the Sheriff. Spencer Thomas (who
threatens to leap into prominence as a tenor
of consequence) was the piebald hero. Dick
Johnson, who is neither white nor black in
character, but just enough of both to be
adored by the Camp bar-maiden, Minnie.
Jeanne Brola is Minnie. This is her star
part, and she earned a truck-load of
flowers for her dashing efforts, through three
acts, to save the hero’s life. In a strong-
cast of T 8 persons, each singer showed a firm
determination to be heard above the uproar
of the Voghera department, even if he (or
she) had' to burst his (or her) windpipe.
The other first-night of the week was given
to “Cavalleria Rusticana” and “Pagliacci.”
The co. was happier in the first. The plump
d’Oisley was vocally satisfactory as
Turiddu, the village Lothario who has his
ear bitten (a cheery Sicilian way of settling
these matters) by the muleteer Alfio (Gra-
ham Marr). Jeanne Rrola was jealous in
the right key as the girl who loves Turiddu,
and faints to slow music when the ear-biter
slays Lothario for flirting with Mrs. Alfio.
Neither Mabel Dennis, as the frisky young
matron in question, nor Hilda Morris, as
d’Oisley’s stage mother, had many oppor-
tunities. The more important “Pagliacci”
was a poor production. Felice Lyne, as
Nedda, surrounded her bird-like trills with
a limited sense of its dramatic and vocal
possibilities. She looked like the best girl
in the Sunday-school, rehearsing for a break-
ing-up party. Francis Costa fought the
English language with mixed results in the
part of Canio, master of the troop of stroll-
ing players. The donkey who was
compulsorily trained made a brief appear-
ance with the arrival of the troop, and
shied at the front stalls before his exit.
Samuell, as the hunchback Tonio. sang the
Prologue so well that it remained the best
part of an indifferent effort to do justice to
Leoncavallo’s delightful music.

“The Confession,” now being heard nightly
at Sydney Royal, is an emotional orgy. H.
H. Roberts, as Father Bartlett, the young
priest who is loaded early in the first act
“PAGLIACCI," AT HER MAJESTY'S,
SYDNEY.
Canto (Francis Costa) cures his better-haf
(Felice Lyne) of flirting.
with the jumpy murderer’s confession, is
the worst offender ; but it must be admitted
that he does his duty fairly by his sainted
part, which is practically all posing and
blessing. He has to look like a stained-glass
window and give a lifelike imitation of a
reluctant clerical ice-chest which contains
an indigestible secret. It is a tough job,
but the audience is always sympathetic, and
be gets through cleverly without
making his Reverence seem too much
of a prig. Eugenie Duggan, as sister
of tlie murdered man and fiancee of
the priest’s brother, who is wrongfully
accused, does not lighten the grief
much. She spends a good deal of hex-
time clinching and weeping in holts
with Marion Willis, the grey-haired
mother of the sainted father and the
accursed. During their worst mo-
ments together, the young Father
(who is really more of a son) looks
intolerably good in the background.
The comic relief to all this is sup-
posed to be supplied by Patsy Moran,
a well-brushed Bowery hunchback
who seems to own the rectory. But
inasmuch as Patsy is played by an
attractive and hard-working young
actress (Olive Wilton), he isn’t con-
vincing. Patsy is rather like an intrusive
Principal Boy. All hands appeal to Heaven at
frequent intervals and bless one another with
great unction on the slightest pretext. It be-
ing obviously impossible for the stage
Father Bartlett to divulge his awful secret,
the playgoer knows that Joseph Dumont,
the jumpy murderer above mentioned, is
doomed. By waiting patiently, he eventu-
ally learns that Dumont dies in record time
of consumption. There are various gaps in
the construction of the play; but the pro-
gramme’s statement that 70,000 people wit-
nessed it in Melbourne indicates how little
these technical faults matter to the religious
playgoer.

There is a classic dog turn at the National
(Sydney) this week. It consists of Ted
Bailey and. his dogs posing as statuary
modelled on famous hunting pictures, and
is dedicated to the sportsmen of the
world. Man and animals are marble-
whitened. and the extraordinary apprecia-
tion of the various poses (the chase, acci-
dent, sickness, death, and so on) which the
three dogs exhibit is in the nature of a reve-
lation even to keen students of the canine.
At the Crystal Palace “The Feudists,” a
two-reel comedy, with John Bunny and Flora
Finch as the stars, provided entertainment
tor einemamacs who look for the bright side
of things. “The Australian Gazette,” as
usual, contained a fair assortment of sub-
jects, including, of course, the arrival of
George Reid ; but it did seem like rubbing it
in to make George chase “Hard Cash.” an
Edison film, across the sheet.
** * *
“Autumn Manoeuvres,” at Her Majesty’s
in Melbourne, is a musical specialty, very
passable, and repeatedly threatened with
originality. Certainly its music is not strik-
ingly remarkable, but there are moments of
genuine comedy in its satire on the British
Territorial, with his incompetence thick
upon him. Yet the joke is not always
against the amazing Captain Withers, who
inflicts his cheerful second-rateness on the
fashionable life of Ambermere Hall. Rather
he is calculated to score at the expense of his
patrician surroundings and win the sympa-
thies of the audience on the grounds of
superior humanity and mental balance. Any-
how, the mockery that might degrade him
in the eyes of an English mob rather elevates
, ln sight of an Australian crowd,
which hasn’t the other’s fine sense of caste.
Phil Smith plays this red-headed descendant
ot IS generations of grocers, the first big
role that Melbourne has yet seen him in. His
intermittent habit of being Smith instead of
W ithers is the only spot of mildew on an
otherwise realistic impersonation. A tre-
mendously romantic part is that of Captain
F alconer, who refuses to -stray across the
threshold of a former home which he had left
dark anger when it came into the hands
of a fair lady of his acquaintance. As shown
by Talleur Andrews he is a tuneful citizen
always,. though he seems to regard life more
as a painful duty than as a perfect pleasure,
and comports himself like the M.C. at an
undertakers’ picnic. Florence Young is
vocally pleasing as Alix Luttrell, but is
weak on the histrionic side. Celia Ghiloni
appears at her best as Lady Elleswater, and
gets her favorable impression in early. Miss
Lrunton gives a faithful picture of herself
in the pretty part of June Pomeroy, and
sings a somg in the third act that is the
essence of beguilement. Miss Vivien Talleur
dances effectively as the gypsy, Naomi. Of
the other ladies it only remains to be said
that, if Miss Hilda Vining would listen
intently to her singing as Lady Letty
Larkins, she might hear something to her
disadvantage. Robert Whyte, jun., is the
most imposing among the men who also ran.
His part is that of Major-General Pomeroy.
Reginald Roberts is Lieutenant Orde, repre-
sented as an ass who, if he lived on his wits,
would soon come to the end of his resources.
File male chorus is worth a word of appro-
val, and, as usual, MacCunn directs his
orchestral forces unimpeachably.

SARTO.
INJUNCTIONS AOAINST PASSING OFF.
LATEST CASE.—SCHWEPPES v. O’CONNOR.
In the Supreme Court, Queensland, before his Honor Mr. Justice Real and a jury of four.
(See Brisbane Couriers, August 27 to September 13, in which an Injunction was granted .j
During this case, which lasted 12 days, the following evidence was given, showing the
health-giving properties of Sarto and the amount of Sarsaparilla contained therein.
“George Alexander Byrn, member of the firm of Dixon and Byrn, assayers and
analysts, of Sydney, deposed that he had made analyses of a bottle of Sarto and a
bottle of ‘Sarsa.’ He found that ‘Sarto’ contained 11.47 per cent, of solids, .108 of
similicia, and the balance consisted of water and a small quantity of alcohol. Barsa
contained 11.15 of solids and .006 per cent, of similicia, and the balance consisted of
water and a small quantity of alcohol. There was, therefore, eighteen times as
much similicia in ‘Sarto’ as there was in ‘Sarsa. Similicia was the active
principle of sarsaparilla; it was a medicinal tonic. There were other properties
of sarsaparilla of medicinal value, but the active principle was similicia.
Mr Feez- “How does ‘Sarto’ compare with ‘Sarsa’ according to your analysis?
—“There is a' finer quality of sarsaparilla in ‘Sarto’ than there is in ‘Sarsa.’ ” “Is
it correct to sav that some sarsaparilla root may have no similicia at all. Yes.
“What does the presence of .108 per cent, of similicia indicate to you? — “That
only the best sarsaparilla root has been used in the preparation.’’ “Is that a
large or a small quantity for a drink of that kind?’’—“A very fair amount.’’
