At Melbourne Royal Peggy Admaston
continues to offer weak resistance to the
impassioned advances of Roderick Colling-
wood, what time her husband is making
suspicious inquiries over the ’phone. This
outside intrusion into a development where
even two are beginning to look like a crowd
is the one scene of genuine dramatic interest
in the play. In poignancy it excels the
much-advertised Divorce Court outburst,
which, apart from its displays of feminine
howling, merely serves to .tell the story over
again. It is rather interesting to note that
“A Butterfly on the Wheel” wins its support
through this habit of repeating itself, by an
emotional subterfuge, as it were.
si: * *
Du Calion is the present excellent joke of
the Opera House entertainment. Ilis con-
tinuous stream of talk while he balances
cleverly on a ladder shows a high-souled en-
deavor to entertain the audience under the
most trying circumstances. Bellora, the
mimic, has turned up again; Bonnie Leo-
nard is still looking like a nymph lifted off
a cigarette card ; while the Saxones are sing-
ing and dancing as before. As regards Gene
Greene, The Bulletin would accept him
es the Last Word in Ragtime even if he
didn’t take such a long time to reach it.

A sensational circumstance at Melbourne
Olympia on Saturday was “A Message from
Headquarters,” showing a Yankee hero
coolly dodging the violence that Dago ven-
geance had designed for him. Similarly
exciting was “The Sixth Commandment.” a
Western Amurkan thriller with sudden
death imminent, but never happening.
“Dan Creegan’s Ghost” and “A Tender-
hearted Crook” were the best comedies ; and
the kinetophone broke out in several selec-
tions from “La Mascotte.”

“Home Sweet Home” has been featured
at Melbourne Majestic, but a kangaroo hunt
per motor-car has been getting most applause
from the congregation. “My Lady of Idle-
ness” has also been popular, and the bio-
graph has had something further to say
on the overworked beauties of ancient
Venice. This week the Majestic is unreel-
ing “The Secret of the Bureau,” a Nordisk
drama full of love, jealousy and other human
failings.
Of this year’s 143rd art show in Mel-
bourne :
Dora Wilson is exhibiting 136 pastels and paint-
ings at the Athensum. The pastels form the most
interesting part of the show, and there can be no
doubt of the artist’s expertness in this medium.
“Dulce Domum” is an unusually large and ambitious
study, in which the effect of lamplight on a quartette
“AT CRIPPLE
CREEK ” (MEL-
BOURNE
PRINCESS'S J.
Cyril Mackay as
the noble Joe
Mayfield.
of naturally-grouped female figures is cleverly
handled. A strongly-drawn study of Oscar Rash as
the Moor who would a-wooing go, and a couple of
impressions of Genge are effective pieces of work;
and “The Lady Rowena,” “Sans Souci” and “Once
Upon a Time” are all charming, without being ex-
citing. A series of boy studies are, considering the
ability of the artist, strangely flat and uninterest-
ing, giving the impression that they have been ex-
ecuted with an ink roller and squashed in the pro-
cess. Of the oil paintings, “The Blue Girl” inspires
a subdued affection, tempered with regret that her
hands are so much less attractive than her feet ; and
of the water-colors, “Regrading the Road at Frank-
ston,” though quite different in theme and treat-
ment from most of the artist’s work, strikes this
viewer as best worth the money.

Fred Niblo is doing capital work in Ade-
laide, which finds more personal magnetism
in him than in anybody else in his line
since Charles Arnold. His “Fortune
Hunter” works out thus : Idea, novel; treat-
ment, conventional; details, fresh and
charming; acting, good all round, two labori-
ous studies of village “character” excepted.
Williams and Rankin are doing good cornet
work at the Tivoli, where Dood.v is dancing
and his lady partner is talking, and the
Mullers are hoop-spinning, and Merian’s dogs
stalk or totter or leap through their tragic
story of love, jealousy and revenge, and the
Fanton athletes have just been added to a
strong bill. At the King’s, Forsyth and
Boyne have been giving a new and really
thrilling tabloid drama of violence and sur-
prise, “Jack the Rat.” The Crimson Ram-
blers continue their first part (with varia-
tions), and a lot of much-heralded new folks
are just moving in. The Dandies, Roland
Henry and Co., at the Exhibition Gardens,
began by showing that they could sing well;
now they are proving themselves funny, too.
Wondergraph has “Rrotea,” which succeeds
in being different frqm ordinary films; and
West’s show the .Battle of Gettysburg, which
is a fake to marvel at.

From an Adelaide critic:—
The 16th “Federal” picture exhibition in Ade-
laide consists of Girls and Gums. Hans Heysen’s
“Red Gold” is rather a reaction from the fantastic
saplings he has delighted in. It is just two big
ironbarks (or their cousins), with cattle and land-
scape trimmings, bathed in a heavy sunlight glow.
To hail him as the Australian Turner, as some
already do, may be premature, but certainly his
coloring is superb. His water-colors mostly keep to
the same theme. Rose Lowcay and Mrs. Follen
Bishop (the latter staying now, with her distin-
guished husband, in Adelaide) bring their English
training to bear on the gum also, with conspicuous
success. Penleigh Boyd (Vic.) and W. J. Wil-
liams and Gwen Barringer (S.A.) tree up the
same bark ; and Gustave Barnes brings it with-
out difficultv into his view of Adelaide from
Montefiore Hill. Boyd shows also his St. Ives
(Royal Academy, 1912), in which he contrives to
make the little port look real Italian. Hayley
“A BUTTERFLY ON

ROYAL.
Lord Ellerdine.
The Judge.
Lever has sent out one big wharf-and-water canvas,
rather below his best form. Will Ashton is not
represented, though his .father, James A., has the
usual seascapes, careful and yet clever. Nicholls
Anderson (Vic.) depicts the great waste that is Can-
berra. The figure-studies (single figures, mostly)
are many, and good. Violet Teague (Vic.) has a fine
boy, standing against a light background; it seems
hardly credible that two big, harsh portraits of Mel-
bourne ladies can have come from the same brush.
Muir Auld (N.S.W.), Clewin Harcourt (Vic.) and
Archibald Collins (S.A.) score heavily in this line.
A feature of the show is a big batch of mostly-small
works from Brisbane, and from the Women Painters
of N.S.W. Gwendolyn Stanley (Q.) ambitiously
renders Delilah doing a Salome dance for Samson.
It is notable as one of the few attempts at dramatic
composition in the whole show. The best work
among the Sydney ladies is in quaint story-illustra-
tion, of the Rackham or Dulac type, by Ruby
Winckler and Nelle Rodd. The Art Gallery of S.A.
is understood to be “sitting on” the exhibition, with
a view to spending the usual few hundred pounds
in adding to its Australian Room.
If they have any perception at all, the
Peace Society (which is really the Society
for Unrestrained Violence) and the raging,
red-rag Socialists will soon lay down their
warlike tracts and peacefully accept the
compulsory-service idea like good citizens.
A Korean patriot dropped into Sydney last
week from San Francisco, where he edits a
paper called the New Korea. He told the
old, familiar story, i.e., that the state of the
peaceable Koreans, who didn’t worry about
defence till it was too late, is as near slavery
as the Japanese dare make it. The only
restraining influence is the fear of Russia ;
and even with that given in, the grip of the
conquerors on Korea’s throat is close up to
strangulation point. If two or three Koreans
meet together, the Japanese overlords see the
start of revolution and disperse the con-
spirators, who are probably talking about the
price of fish. Further, in order to make
dead-sure that the accursed warlike spirit
will never be kindled, such hymns as “On-
ward. Christian Soldiers” and “Stand Up
for Jesus” are prohibited. A gramophone
record of “Let Me Like a Soldier Fall” would
be sufficient to cause the arrest of a whole
social evening. When you tie up this pleas-
ant story with the testimony of the Vic-
torian police that the drills and military
discipline have practically killed the “push”
trouble, there doesn’t seem to be a scrap of
argument left for the upholders of universal
brotherhood and slackness.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Edward Maas (who ought to know, since he
married a daughter of the house of Rickards) tells in
“The Life Story of Harry Rickards,” recently prepared,
some entertaining vaudeville figures. Chung Ling
Soo (Robinson) got £4OO a week for eight shows of
50piin. each—the most money that the Tivoli man-
agement ever paid out to an individual artist. Walter
Kelly, the Virginian Judge, got £l5O a week for
his nigger yarns; Barnold collected £2OO weekly for
Drunken Dan and his sober pals; Little Tich got
£250 a week, and drew the money when he was at
sea as well; G. W. Hunter (comedian) got £6O
every pay-day, as against John Coleman’s £l5.
Julian Rose (Levinsky) was insulted with £175, and
Hackenschmidt had to drag along on £125. Chas.
Godfrey got £lOO in the days before salaries began
to soar. Peggy Pride’s little lot was £6O a week,
and Alice Leamar’s £4O. Biondi’s quick-changing
cost Rickards £125 weekly, and Carl Hertz was
down for £lOO. Hertz, by the way, always claimed
that he was the first man to introduce the cinema-
tograph to Australia, and Houdini (another Rickards’
importation) was the first man to “fly” here.
“Nil”: I want to say thanks for candid criticisms
of the imported opera co. It makes me feel justified
in having declined to add my name .to a list of
guarantors which was opened to induce the co. to
visit Adelaide. For years I have been howling in a
whisper for someone to get together an Aus-
tralian co. to produce grand opera. It is
much easier work than the production of knock-
about musical burlesque, and there are hundreds, if
not thousands, of good-looking, young, capable Aus-
tralians who could sing any (or most) of the im-
ported “artists” blind, deaf and bald-headed! This
writer has twice seen “Carmen” done by foreign
cos. On both occasions the title rSIe was taken by
a middle-aged lady of much tonnage who pranced
in a deliberate and tardigradous manner through the
“fascination” dance, and left no doubt in the mind
of at least one of the audience that Don Jose must
have been on a long sea trip, and had met Carmen
during his first wild jag when he came ashore. No
other excuse for his insanity seemed possible. Car-
men should be played—yes, played, —by a little
wisp of a hot-blooded, tantalising, irresistible Spanish
she-devil! And even if she can’t sing like Tetrazzini,
who Cares? Well, this scribe is never going to waste
an evening on “Carmen” done by imported grand-
opera pros, again. He’ll wait till someone wakes
up to the fact that if opera does not want acting, and
looks go for nought, then it is useless to waste time in
dressing. Whv not give the thing from the concert
platform ? Without there ! Bring me an Australian
grand opera company !
“St. Maori”: Busking companies in Maoriland are
having a precarious time of it during the strike.
Wellington is the jumping-off place in the North
for the South Island, and when the strikers took
charge of the wharves they wouldn’t allow any cargo
to be touched at all. Passengers’ luggage and mails
only were franked through the lines by the pickets.
When Dick Stewart, with the Royal Comics, came
along with half a hold full of scenery, he found that
while it could be unloaded by the theatre hands, if
it was slung up or handed over the bulwarks it
would be pulled to pieces. Wherefore it had to be
left behind. Since then the Comics have been play-
ing the “Quaker Girl,” “Sunshine Girl,” and “Miss
Hook” with the “Belle of New York” outfit (which
happened to have been shipped ahead) —and no one
has been much the wiser. For some reason or other
the strikers got confused over the item “wardrobe,”
and assuming that it meant the ballet-girls’ own
laceries, they let the stage clothes through. Chubby
Charlie Berkeley, in charge of the GenSe show, man-
aged better at Wellington. He harangued the Strike
Committee, told it some of his best stories, and paid
for numerous drinks; but the committee exhibited
magnificent solidarity, and hinted that it was as
much as its job was worth to sneak any cargo
through. Charlie, after sobbing quietly for an hour,
had a bright idea. They would stand the wardrobe
—right! With lightning speed he had the cloths
stripped off the battens, folded neatly, and stowed
into extra wardrobe baskets, dashed to Christchurch
to get new frames and dancing-stage made, and
returned smiling. So Genfie played with her own
scenery in Christchurch.
Three hundred men are working day and night
constructing “The White City,” Sydney’s new amuse-
ment ground. Everything is Australian-made;
nothing imported. Features will be a scenic railway
(run on the block system for safety) with nearly
two miles of rails, a water-canal 2000 ft. long. 12
boats for an underground “Wonderland,” a glass-
ware maze with 3000 ft. of plated mirror, in which
visitors can admire themselves in 38 attitudes simul-
taneously (the musical comedy girl will begin to
feel that she is getting a fair deal at last), and a
heap of other novelties. The show is to open with
a blaze (35,000 electric lamps) on the night of
November 29.
Geo. Willoughby promises Sydney the first Christ-
mas pantomime it has had for some years, with an
Australian principal boy (Carrie Moore) and a
book by A. E. Martin, a bright and frequent con-
tributor to The Bulletin. Lester Brown, a Yankee
with more reputation than accent, arrives bv the next
post to do the producing, and Wynn Jones has
already started training the chorus the way it
should go.
“J.F.H.”: The Australian who has the idea that
all English audiences must be big may be interested
to know that Pelissier, the just-dead leader of the
wonderfully successful “Follies,” told me that he
and they had played to as few as 12 people. This
midget house was at Freshwater, in the Isle of Wight
—the place where ex-G.-G. Tennyson lives. In my
own Australian day I remember making one of an
audience of eighth all told, in the hall of the
Mechanics’ Institute, Geelong. The performer was
the versatile Barlow, better known all over Australia
as the “Blue Tailed Fly.” If that wasn’t a record
house for smallness, it ought to be.
Ned Duggan (a partner. in the affluent
Bailey-Grant show these days) wouldn’t have this
story told for a whole property-room full of ginger
ale. Years ago, he was running a co. (George Mar-
low was a member of it) through the Smalls ot
N. S. Wales, and arrived at Oabbagetree to play
race-week with “The Ticket of Leave.” Duggan was
well enough known in the town, but some base scoun-
drel had passed the word along that he was stone-
broke. Hence, the pub proprietors were very sorry
that they were unable to give the co. accommodation,
owing to the crowd in for the races, etc., etc. It
was the old bellman who broke the news to Duggan.
(Spell-oh here while the echoes of the stricken actor s
language die away.) Then the great Inspiration.
Amongst the props, was a roll of dummy notes. These
Duggan grabbed, put on the outside the last nound-
note he possessed, clasped the lot with a band of
elastic, and meandered back to the Metropolitan bar.
With indignation streaming from him, he flashed his
wad of notes, flicked out the genuine one under the
boss’s nose, ordered drinks for the crowd, and then
cursed the hound who had spread the yarn that he
was broke. “Well, Mr. Duggan,” said the pub-
keeper, “the truth is, we did hear that, and I’m
very sorry. Will you have your rooms, anyhow?”
The indignant manager became gradually mollified.
“Very well,” he said, and (picking up the note),
“put the drinks to my account.” Then he went along
to the other pub and repeated the operation. “I
went out of that town,” says Ned, “with £lOO
clear, and if there had been a brass band in the
place it would have been called out by the humili-
ated hotelkeepers for the occasion.”
Who mentioned that melodrama had been slain by
the moving pictures? In Sydney Royal (“The Con-
fession”) has now joined the Criterion (“Within the
Law”) and the Adelphi as melotheatres.
The veteran journalist, “Dagonet” Sims, writes
the epitaph of the late chief of the London Follies
in a non-committal but suggestive sentence: “Poor
Pelissier. The tragedy of success!”
Returning to Australia shortly, under the banner
of Carroll, of Brisbane, Leroy, Talma and Bosco, the
“magicians.’’ Bosco, the corpulent gentleman who
used to produce a rabbit from nowhere, divide it into
twins, and ultimately subtract it from itself, was
recently a very sick illusionist, and never again
expected to harass a guinea pig or upset the peace
and quietude of a barnyard hen. Evidently Bosco
has got back his punch.
“8.J.H.0.”: A distressful feature of the present
Yankee invasion of Melbourne is that at several
big show-places employment is given only on condi-
tion that the Australian who acquires the job shall
simulate a Yankee drawl, adopt Amurkan methods of
walking, dressing and hair-cutting, and generally do
his darnedest to conceal the shameful fact that he
is a fellow-countryman of the unsophisticated citizens
whose gardain coin keeps the show going. Melbourne
is, for this and other reasons, full of bogus Yanks;
and in his way the fraudulent Uncle Sam is as ob-
jectionable as the youth who gets pseudo-blithered
on two shandies and the sight of the barmaid’s gold-
filled teeth.
It has come. A Sydney firm has been conducting
competitions throughout Australia to decide thr
mouth-organ championship. The contestants have
been reduced to 16, and arrangements are now being
made for the musicians to do their worst on the
stage of any Sydney theatre that will permit the
disturbance.
“Ike Pagliacci”: Louis Meyer, the London mana-
ger who invades Australia next year, didn’t casually
hit on the idea of a Commonwealth tour. He has
had Australia drilled into him almost from the
moment he began to mingle theatrical management
with his beloved job as picture editor of London
Opinion (he owns most of that paper). When Ethel
Dane began to hit ’em hard in “The Glad Eye,”
Meyer asked her where she started in the business.
“At the Semaphore,” she replied. “Semaphore?”
queried Meyer. “Near Adelaide,” explained Ethel.
“Adelaide?” gasped Meyer, and sent the office boy
for an atlas. Keightley, of Australia, won laurels
in Meyer’s production of “The Chaperone.”
Frank 'Bradley, of Adelaide, went to London,
and stood so long on the doorstep of one
manager’s office that he was ordered off.
“Sir,” he said, posing melodramatically, “you order
me out now, but, some day, sir, I will play Napoleon
in this theatre.” The declaration was a remarkable
one to make from a hostile doormat, but the prophesy
was all right. Bradley played Nap. in “Madame
Sans-Gene” in the theatre a little later. The Ade-
laide man then made some successes with Meyer’s
show on tour, and at last came the inevitable
“Where’d you learn the game?” Bradley replied:
“In Hindmarsh, Walkerville, St. Peters, Norwood
and Unley.” “Oh, !” said Meyer, “I suppose
they’re in Australia, too!” The Strand Theatre
manager didn’t look a bit surprised when Beaumont
Smith, dropping in casually, said: “I’m from Aus-
tralia,” suggested a tour of the Commonwealth, and
went away with a partnership in his pocket. Dis-
cussing the cast for “The Glad Eye,” Smith said:
“We’d want Ethel Dane, and Bradley would have to
come.” Meyer ruminated. “W T hat I can’t get at,”
he said at length, “is why you ever let them leave
Australia.”
“Arabella” : Eric Stone, the staid young gentleman
who entertains the masses at the Tivoli with very re-
spectable sketch-portraits of local and other cele-
brities, proves, like his artistic predecessor, Bert
Levy, to be a returned Australian. He was born and
educated in Melbourne; and the fifth person he met
on his recent arrival in Sydney was artist Colies,
with whom, as a kid of the same age, he attended
the big school on the Eastern Hill of the Victorian
capital. There did he oft-times wonder at his de-
praved brother’s energy in the way of decorating his
school books with pictures of Indian skirmishes, rail-
way smashes, murdered detectives and marine dis-
asters, etc., what time Latin nouns buzzed past his
ears in impotency. In later years, “Baldy” (as the
Tivoli man was called —though he had a tidy crop
of snowy hair) developed a really serious streak of
energy, which landed him in America, and then took
him to the United Kingdom and a good part of Eurone :
whilst Colies, who was badly bitten by the political
microbe, ventured no farther than the inter-State
boats would carry him.
“J.F.H.”: “Actorbat” is London stage slang for
the understudy of a leading actor in sensation scenes
involving risk to life and limb. It is not the actual
villain or hero who tumbles over the cliff or is
chucked out of the lighthouse into the seething
waves: it is a man made up to look just like him. It
is, in short, the actorbat. In Melbourne Charles
Warner had one who did the fall from the scaffold
in “Drink.” Charles paid him 2s. a night. He
demanded three. Charles refused. The actorbat said
nothing, but that night, when he was perched up on
the scaffold ready to fall, he held up three fingers.
Charles, looking up from the wings, shook his head.
The actorbat merely shrugged his shoulders and sat
tight. Charles saw what that meant, and franti-
cally held up three fingers. Then the actorbat did
the tumble in first-class style, and drew 3s. a night
for the rest of the season.
The Last Post has sounded for R. 11. Rey-
nolds, who served in British Army in the
Crimea and in Maoriland. From M.L. he
gravitated to N. S. Wales, where he got a
billet as Sergeant-Instructor of the local
forces. From soldiering he went into the
Richmond River timber trade, and with the
profits speculated wisely and well in land,
and died, at 80, well-to-do. Soldiering runs
in the family. One of his sons, a major in
the R.A.A., is learning the trade on behalf
of the Commonwealth in London; and an-
other holds a commission in the Congre-
gational unit of the army of black-coats.
PLEASURE’S PARADOXES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was not a dry eye in the place as the
affecting story was unfolded of the beautiful but
unhappy Lady Isabel Nobody should miss seeing
this film. —Melbourne show-notice.
Roll up, ye pleasure seekers,
Here’s value for your “tin”;
The old-established story
Of Lady Bella’s sin
Is being flicked within.
The day’s harsh toil is ended,
And pleasure waits for all;
But shun the spooning benches,
The festive music hall,
Where joy hangs like a pall!
Seek ye the picture palace
Where tears lie round in pools,
And sobs are sold for 6d.,
And sorrow’s kept on spools—
There Grief triumphant rules!
Some take their pleasures sadly,
Where tragic “comics” jest;
And some their sorrows gladly,
Who like a show the best
With “human interest.”
One seeks for fun at farces,
And wilts beneath the strain;
While he who buyeth sadness
Wins pleasure from his pain!
Can anyone explain ?
VICTORIA.

LOVE LAUGHS AT LOGIC.
PARENT: “What is your reason for wishing
to marry my daughter? ”
YOUNG MAN : “I have no reason, sir; I am
in love."
On Friday, October 31, the Dunedin
(M.L.) wharf lumpers dropped their cargo-
hooks. The strike was a trifle unexpected,
but mark how master minds can meet an
emergency. On Saturday, November 1, the
local Coal Dealers’ Association announced
a rise of ss. per ton in the price all round.
As 75 per cent, of the fuel consumed in
Dunedin comes by rail from mines near the
town, this statesmanlike dive at the petti-
coats of opportunity fills The Bulletin
with admiration. On second thoughts, it is
hardly admiration.
The people with the cottages to let and
the building lots to sell have had their way,
and the Sydney suburb of Rookwood gives
that name over to the city’s big graveyard.
As for itself, the municipality will hence-
forth be “Lidcombe,” a name compounded
on turf principles, Lidbury and Larcombe
being ex-Mayprs. Originally the place was
“Haslem’s Creek,” one Haslem having had
a beer shop there in the ’3o’s. The first
corpse interred in the cemetery was a Syd-
ney Hospital patient, and the first to leave
the mortuary station (Redfern) was that of
the patriot Dr. Bland.
John Norton lately bought for £20,000 a
fine block of land in Russell-street, Mel-
bourne, opposite the King’s—one of
the few available sites for a theatre, as it
has three frontages. This block is the
southern end of the half-acre purchased by
Anthony Ilordern I. at the second Melbourne
land sale in February, 1839. The price for
the lot was_£llo. When Anthony I.’s widow
died, in 1872, the property was put up for
sale in accordance with the will, and
brought £65,321. Peter Davis, an old-time
auctioneer, and a friend of the Horderns,
took out a special license in order that he
might nreside at the sale.

“Lex” on a weird form of insanity :
Beiliss, the Jew who was tried in Kieff and
acquitted last week on a charge of having murdered
a boy to secure blood for Jewish ritual purposes, had
a narrow escape. And even now the local Hebrew
community labors under an infamous and grotesquely
absurd suspicion. The one defence that was most
feasible, in all the circumstances, does not appear,
from the cables of the past month, to have been
advanced at all. Far the most likely theory is that
the unlucky Yuschinsky was slain by one of those
Sadistic, maniacs who are to be found in every modern
civilisation. His 47 wounds account amply for the
fact that he had been “bled white.” But they do
more. They suggest that the essence of the crime
was a mad lust for slaughter and a desire to inflict
the utmost pain possible. If blood was taken away
from the body, it only goes to strengthen the idea
that Sadism was responsible. Krafft-Ebing, in the
latest edition of his “Psychopathia Sexualis,” quotes
a number of cases where the blood of the victim
was kept by the slayer, apparently as a sort of
memento of his hideous deed. As to the pur-
poseless killing of perfectly inoffensive strangers, in
the most brutal possible manner, the literature of
sex-perversion teems with it. Zola provides a care-
ful study of a Sadistic subject in his “La Bgte
Humaine.” The central figure of this book never
saw certain people without being obsessed by an
overwhelming desire to stab them. “Jack the Rip-
per ’ was certainly one of these unfortunates. Have-
lock Ellis cites scores of similar cases. The real
murderer of Yuschinsky will never be found, in all
likelihood. The absence of understandable motive
in such crimes is the supreme protection of the
offender. They usually, as in the case of the just-
acquitted Beiliss, cause trouble to some innocent
person—-on the principle that someone must have
been guilty, and that the merest shadow of a motive
is enough in the last resort. This was what caused
the gaoling, on a charge of cattle-maiming in England,
of Udalji, the man whose case Conan Doyle took up a
few years ago. Conan Doyle affirmed all along that
the man whom the jury had convicted was innocent,
and that the cattle-maiming was a manifestation of
Sadism. It went on, unabated, after the alleged
maimer had gone to gaol, and ultimately the man
was granted a “free pardon”-—the quaint method
by which the British law admits an error. So, in
Russia, they may arrest Jews like Beiliss by the
thousand, and there may be pogroms every other
week, and yet Christians will be mysteriously stabbed
to death.
* * * *
“Snell”: Melbourne is never without a
noteworthy street crank or two, and the
most conspicuous in the public eye now is
a short, squat, bewhiskered faddist who
looks like a German anarchist subdued by
misfortunes, and puts in his time doing the
block that takes in Collins, Elizabeth,
Bourke and Swanston streets, reading a
large Bible. His general appearance is not
encouraging, but he has one other saintly
attribute apart from his Bible-reading pro-
clivities—his whiskers are a tangle, and his
hair is confusion thrice confounded. Some-
times he leans against large edifices, tracing
his way from Genesis i. to Revelation xxii.
with a forefinger like a fried sausage ; but,
as a rule, he drifts along the pave, digging
out texts with the same preposterous digit,
butting into the young and the fair, and
getting the fripperies of the worldlings en-
tangled in his voluptuous whiskers. Now
and again he seizes his prey, and carries it
to a quiet corner in an arcade or doorway
to read to it how the Philistines and Ammo-
nites oppressed Israel (Judges x),
leading .on to the sealing of the covenant
(Nehemiah x), or, D.v. and weather per-
mitting, wandering far inland, even unto
Jehoiakim’s judgment, and the casting of
Jeremiah into the dungeon of Malchiah
(Jeremiah xxx, 7; and xxx. 8). As a rule,
the captive is an elderly lady of puritanical
aspect, who regards it as a solemn duty to
bear with the reading of the Scriptures in
any circumstances, and who would consider
it an act of sacrilege or godless dreadfulness
to break away from the exhorter and ex-
pounder, however lowly; consequently the
same fagged female may be found "being
read to remorselessly for long and dreary
periods, totally disregarded by the police
and the Society for the Prevention of
Cruelty to Mammals. He has been actively
operating for a good while now, and it is con-
sidered high time he was allotted a vacant
space in which he could search the Scrip-
tures without encroaching upon the liberties
of the simple peoples.

“F.L.M.” : Dr. Strong’s Australian. Church
(Melbourne) has been helped out of the
financial difficulties that threatened its ex-
tinction by a legacy of £5OOO. .The donor, a
Miss Timms, who died some weeks ago, was
the last of the band of wealthy adherents
who founded the tabernacle when Dr. Strong
left Scots’ Church, in Collins-street, after
refusing to ram “hell fire” down the throats
of his congregation. The others are either
dead, or have slipped away from the church,
owing to Dr. Strong’s anti-jingo utterances
and Socialistic tendencies. Years asro Miss
Timms set aside a large sum to enable the
congregation to buy the present building, but
when she went to her cupboard it was very
nearly bare, owing to the hunger of an erring
agent. Felton, of the art bequest, was also
one of Strong’s moneyed supporters, and it is
known that an early will, in which he had
handsomely provided for the Australian
Church, was altered because the pastor or the
flock or someone wouldn’t go the way he
thought they should go.
Dear Bulletin, —I don’t know if you
heard the third act of the Indian gentleman’s
ad. for a typist with superb figure, etc., re-
printed in The Bulletin some time back.
Writer has personal knowledge of two girls
who applied—one from Wade’s side of the
harbor, the other from a Western suburb.
The latter maid got a long-winded, pathetic
reply. The advertiser was Captain ——
retired, of his Majesty’s Indian Forces, and
now owner of a prosperous business in Cal-
cutta or Burmah or somewhere. He was
young, a widower, and had a small child,
and he wanted a wife. He offered to cable
a passage, and besought applicant not to
cease corresponding. The other lass got a
note, directly accepting her services, and
cabling a passage ; and she was within two
days of sailing (without papa’s consent)
when a mutual friend blew the whole yarn
up at an afternoon tea-shop. References
were given to Mr. Fisher and Dr. Arthur.
The latter gentleman, I believe, indignantly
denies all knowledge of the writer. —Yours,
William Beetroot.
“Harborite”: The law is often an ass
when it does things, but it’s liable to be a
whole team of mules when it doesn’t do
them. As steamboats are, as a rule, strongly
built, the N. S. Wales law stipulates for
watertight compartments, life-saving appli-
ances, etc.; but as motor-boats are nearly
always lightly constructed, the owners may
please themselves regarding these matters.
They may also carry as many passengers
as they like and where they like. Occasion-
ally they drop a few where they don’t like ;
but the big disaster has not yet arrived.
The authorities did awake and ask a ques-
tion or two some years ago when the
motor-boat Agnes foundered; but as only
a few people were drowned they resumed
their slumbers after a few mutterings, and
have been asleep ever since.

(now dead), Mrs. Webster, Henry Gyles
Turner and F. Sinclaire. The Rev.
Charles Read is the latest to throw in his
ministerial marble. The committee is hunt-
ing in London for a successor, but the
atmosphere is cold, and somehow parsons
don t hear a call coming to them from this
way. The building, situated close to Fitz-

BACK TO THE VILLAGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Here we are again!” was the cheery aside George
Reid uttered to his wite as the belated express
crawled into the station. . . .Altogether, the return to
Sydney partook of the character of a victor’s' tri-
umphal march.— Morning paper.
“It is the same old George,” everybody said and
agreed.— Evening paper.
~Our Jarge come back last week. By gum, ’ee should
have ’eard they cheers!
I thought as they be goin’ to bust th’ drums inside
these yeers !
I tell ’ee, too, as I ’ave seen a sight of public fuss;
But it was nought to what our Jarge ’e got from all
of us.
An’ Jarge ’e smiled an’ cracked ’is jokes—’e be a
joker, sure!
I bet ’ee now our King ain’t never met a bigger cure!
They say Jarge sets ’em laughin’ so they can’t get
on nohow ;
They dukes an’ sich—all solemn folk—they look for
old Jarge now.
’E tells ’em all how fat he be—my ! don’t ’e go the
pace,
Till every jolly gentleman be moppin’ of his face.
Our Jarge ’e makes ’em laugh until they cry. Ay,
that ’e do !
’Tis good for this far village, lad, and good for me
and you!
Aye, lad, if Jarge be spared for us, our trade will
soon be large,
For there be nought in Lunnon like this jokin’ way
of Jarge.
Old Jarge ’e tells ’ow fat he be ’most twenty times
a day,
An’ all they earls an’ such they have to wipe they
tears away.
If that won’t teach they Lunnon folk trade lies
across the sea,
I ask ’ee now, in Heaven’s name, what sense in life
there be ?
And Jarge come back last week! I tell ’ee, lad, it
were a sight
To see old Jarge a-standin’ theer so happy and so
bright!
Our good old Jarge as speaks to kings an’ tells how
fat he be!
I tell ’ee now I’d give a crown to see his Majesty
"’hen old Jarge cracks that joke! Aye, lad, our
village won’t go down
While our Jarge be a-crackin’ jokes in grey old
Lunnon town!
N.S.W.

Heaven knows what he meant; but this is
what the little Observer of his native vil-
lage of Ballymena says that Sam
McG’aughey wrote to “William It. Young,
Esq., Galgorm Castle, Ballymena :
I have noted with interest in the papers Sir
Edward Carson’s stand against Home Rule. Although
it is 37 years since I left Ireland I have the same
feeling as I would have had had I remained there
during that time, that it would be an injustice to
the loyal Protestants of Ireland to hand over to the
tender mercies of the disloyal two-thirds, and I am
confident that if Ireland got Home Rule she would
not be satisfied. We see here the same thing, where
we have the freest institutions in the world —that
the Roman Catholic element is never satisfied. I
will promise you £5OO towards your fighting fund,
and will give it to you, and whatever sums you may
require, within the next few months, and herewith
enclose you an order for £250.
“I will give it to you, and whatever sums
you may require, within the next few
months,” the local villager of Galgorm
Castle says McCaughey said ; probably what
the ex-villager of Ballymena thinks he said
was that the £5OO would happen
along “in whatever sums you may re-
quire.” Anyway, only £250 was in-
closed for a start to save the country
from the “disloyal” people who want to
make it so attractive that the other
McCaugheys won’t leave it. Evidently our
McCaughey thought so little of the system
which he wants others to continue living
under, that 37 years ago he quitted it for a
country where there is Home Rule, and
where things are made so burdensome for a
Protestant like McCaughey that he hasn't
been able to become anything more than a
mere millionaire.
“Bobstay’s” puzzle :
At the present time there are two wrecked vessels
on Long Reef, near Manly (N.S.W.). One is the
steamship Euroka, the other the motor-boat Venture.
The Euroka, with seven men on board, got into diffi-
culties off Manly, and the crew decided to leave her.
They were provided with a roomy lifeboat, properly
fitted, and sailed comfortably into Sydney Harbor.
Besides this boat, required by the law 'to be on
hoard, the Euroka had a sufficient supply of lifebelts,
lifebuoys, etc. After the crew had abandoned the
steamer she drifted on to Long Reef. The Marine
Court sat, found no one to blame, and solemnly certi-
fied that the vessel was properly supplied with the
necessary life-saving gear. The' motor-boat Venture
went out to salvage the fittings of the Euroka, and
left Sydney with six men on board. As the law does
not ask motor-boats to carry life-saving appliances,
the Venture had none on board. Luckily, a dinghy
was towed astern, to assist in the salvage work.
When near the Euroka, the Venture shipped a big
sea and capsized, eventually piling up on Long Reef
alongside the steamer. The six men reached the
dinghy and pulled for the shore, but the boat, being
of the colander type, soon filled, and all hands were
left struggling in the breakers. After a severe
tussle they were washed up on the beach. The
Marine Court, however, did not take any notice of
this exciting circumstance. Officially, the law is not
aware of the existence of the Venture, and had the
whole six of her crew been drowned the Court would
have ignored the fact. Dozens of motor-boats go
outside the Heads every day without a boat of the
colander or any other variety behind or aboard of
them, and although the Navigation Act sets out the
appliances such craft must carry under risk of a
r’lOO fine, the risk is taken with great unanimity.
Maybe the law reckons the people who take these
risks are wooden-heads who will thereby float ashore
without artificial aid in case of accident. And,
maybe, the law’s right.

“Gordon G.” : A gentleman from the coun-
try was besotted enough to engage me the
other day to defend him on a charge of
embezzlement. The prosecution alleged and
proved everything against him conclusively,
and I found myself right “up against it.”
During the case a table was incidentally
mentioned, and witnesses were called to
prove that its innards had been used by my
client for the base purpose of secreting the
stolen property. Having appealed to the
jurors’ uncommon intelligence and so forth,
WASTEFUL AND
RIDICULOUS
EXCESS.
LILY : "My dear Vi, why will you drench yourself with
scent? You know what Shakspeare says about adding perfume to the violet. "
VIOLET: “ Doesn't he say something, too, about painting the t[?]?"
I glanced at his Honor and plunged in.
To such straits was I driven that I set out
on a line of argument in which I endeavored
to show that the table was of such a dilapi-
dated and degraded nature as to be the last
place in which anyone would seek to conceal
anything, and that my client therefore
placed the property in it without any feloni-
ous intent whatever. With this end in view
I enlarged on the shape, general structure,
size, and present state of decay of the table,
and incidentally harangued the 12 sages on
the utterly impossible and ludicrous condi-
tion of the legs thereof. It was at this
stage that the bolt came from the ermine.
Just as I was flattering myself that I was
making something of an impression, his
Honor suddenly and gravely remarked,
“Yes, yes, Mr. G., all this talk about the
legs is very well, but don’t you think we
might now get on to the drawers?” Wild
mustangs won’t drag from me that Judge’s
name. The table turned scarlet.

“Bloore” : It is time to give the system of
appointing area-officers from the ranks of
those who hold day-jobs in the civil service
a kick in the neck. The
overworked sergeants-major
do most of the graft, I know ;
still the area-officer has some
duties, and he neglects his
own departmental work to
do them, and incidentally
draws two salaries. I know
of one khaki-breeched gentle-
man, on the £250 mark in
the State’s service, who, half
his time, is using the
Department’s ’phone lines
and hanging up business
generally to further his
own business as O.C. of the,
say, 8.876 Area. Neither
the Area nor the Department
for Blast Furnaces gets a
fair deal. Others attend to
their regular jobs diligently,
and it is sheer waste for the
Defence people to pay them
£156 a year for the indiffer-
ent and slap-dash work they
do in their spare time. Why
not insist upon area-officers
being detached instead of
semi-detached persons, and
give them, sav. £250 to start
on? There are plenty of fel-
lows —fellows who have seen
the real thing—available and
willing to take it on at this
figure; but they all seem to
get passed over in favor of
Algernon, whose military
training is mostly of the Boy
Scout and malacca-cane
order.
The old-established convention which im-
pelled judges to assume ignorance of every-
day matters appears to be expiring. Any-
how, on the morning of Oaks Day, when
counsel, on both sides, applied for a post-
ponement till the morrow, on the alleged
grounds that it would “be a great conveni-
ence to their clients,” Justice Hood merely
looked over his glasses and remarked: “Yes,
gentlemen ; and which train do you catch ?”
Up at Kempsey (N.S.W.) the other
day, Judge Hamilton had just con-
cluded the swearing-in of the usual drove of
J’s.P., when the astounding discovery was
made that, by error, allegiance had been
sworn to the departed Edward. There were
whisperings and shudderings and visions of
burst marriage-ties and civil war, till the
Judge saved the country by ordering another
round-up. Then the performance was re-
peated, with the name of George substituted
for that of the Good Sport.
“Deeby”: Some of the methods of the
Defence Department seem designed to make
military training as unpopular as possible.
In this area it was intimated on two differ-
ent occasions that pay would be dispersed
on a certain date. The intimations proved
to be false rumors. When the long-due pay
did at last arrive, it transpired that the
local pay-officer had placed it to his credit
at the local bank, probably with a view to
giving a healthy appearance to his account
for business purposes. Anyhow, the lads
were told they must again wait till next
parade. It recalls the time when I lived
in a South American republic, where the
monthly pay of the local troops was dis-
pensed with the utmost irregularity once a
year or so.
There are snobs on the Blue Mountains,
and some of them propose to change the
name of the Berghofer Pass to Victoria, the
old Victoria Pass having been done away
with by a deviation. The feverish loyalists
give as their reason that they “want to
perpetuate the name of the late queen.”
The bathos of it, when you remember that,
in nine cases out of 10, the people who want
to do such things are also the people who
talk loudest about the sturdy pioneer and
the honor that is due to him! When did
Victoria do any pioneering on the Blue
Mountains? And, in any case, there’s a
summit and a fall and a few other things
brandea with her name. Isn’t that enough,
without robbing poor old Berghofer (who-
ever he was) of his little bit of monument?
Archdeacon Boyce .(N.S.W.) recently
issued a diagram depicting the relative ex-
penditures on drink in the Dry and Wet
portions of Maoriland. It showed a very
short column, marked 18s. 6d. per head, for
the Dry, and a very long column, marked
£4 55., for the Wet. No one doubts that
the well-meaning Archdeacon has used any
but official figures; and that is just how
the Archdeacon is dragged in. For, while
the official figures show only the legitimate
consumption of drink inside the Prohibition
districts, the illegitimate drinking is in-
cluded in the £4 ss. of the Wet places ; the
illegitimate drink being smuggled into the
Dry area to be dispensed secretly at
increased price. And not always very
secretly. For instance, a man goes to
Invercargill, and wants to offer refreshment.
So at the pub he yells for Bill, the man.
Bill appears. Then it’s —“Bill, go up to
my room, and in the trunk you’ll find two
bottles of lager, and a bottle of whisky. Fix
us up with sodawater, Bill.” Bill finds
all that is required in that trunk. He
would find brandy, benedictine or port wine
if he were asked. The Archdeacon’s dia-
gram is quite worthless, because it doesn’t
show that mvsterious “trunk,” which is re-
plenished from the nearest Wet district.
“A III.” : The news that the recent Brit-
ish naval manoeuvres have proved the
superiority of the cheap and handy sub-
marine over the big battleship is good hear-
ing for Britons in general and Australians
in particular. The cable goes so far as to
say that the “subs” were so destructive
(theoretically, of course) that they were
called out of action before the end of the
manoeuvres. A couple of dozen submarines
could be built for the price of the Aus-
tralia, and as they can lie, safe and in-
visible, in almost any creek, the “suitable
harbor” trouble is almost eliminated. From
what can be gathered from this latest trial,
the fear of the effective invasion of Britain
has been lifted, in a large measure, from
John Bull’s chest. J. 8., though late to
start, owns by far the biggest fleet of sub-
marines, and the latest designs are admit-
tedly the best going. Since the new sea-
going submarines have a steaming capacity
of 1000 miles, the mosquitoes will not in
future be content with mere coastwise
works; wherefore from this onwards life on
board a big battleship promises to be a jumpy
affair. Men can face a visible danger, but
the dart that may drop in without warning
is calculated to unnerve even a politician.

“Gunnah” among the tombstones: —
“N. or M.,” in his account of old St. David's
cemetery, Hobart, omitted one of the most curious: —
Sacred to the Memory of
GEORGE GWATKIN,
who died Oct. 22nd, 1830,
Aged 32 years.
Beneath this friendly stone is laid
A spirit none could ere subdue,
To spirits ever was inclined,
And spirits ’tis has brought him low.
All you that spirits drink in waste
Consider his untimely end.
They ardently believed in “sermons in stones” in
those days. The authorities usually prefaced their
numerous hangings with remarks anent “the awful
warning” ; and when Nature took matters into her
own hands, the last word was generally spoken in
bad print in the graveyard.
DEAD, BUT NOT GONE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw children pelting stones into an open vault.
They were trying to sink a coffin floating in water.
I also saw human bones protruding from the smashed
wood of a coffin amongst bricks and water in a ruined
vault. Some time ago one of the vaults was entered
and a coffin stripped of its lead sheafing. —From the
story of a neglected Sydney cemetery.
When I at last am dead,
Don’t put me in the ground.
Why should bones lie around
When the poor soul has fled ? •
When I am safely dead,
’Twould make my thin soul howl,
If some unfeeling ghoul
Should loot my coffin-lead.
Don’t box and bury me
With very great respect,
For Time will bring neglect—
Cold, cold as charity.
Burn me to ashes grey,
And let the first wind take
Me hence, for pity’s sake.
That is the quickest way.
Above the dusty earth
On fresh, brave winds I’ll trail;
The hollow of the sail
Shall be my white sea-berth.
Place not a stone upon
My thoughtless, buried head ;
When I, good friend, am dead,
Let me be also gone!
N.S.W.

Some phases of life in the South Sea
Islands were described by Dr. Wood.
Bishop of Melanesia. The Bishop’s Ex-
periences show that the natives in
certain of the missions* having lost the
enthusiasm of the early days of their
conversion to Christianity, are much
inclined to fall into a state of indolence.
To rouse the islanders from this condi-
tion of somnolence to something like
their former alertness, is the great task
of the missionary.—Newt Item. •

C. H. Bertie, the Sydney municipal
librarian, while holidaying in the country of
the Macquaries—the Isle of Mull, in Scot-
land —became possessed of a portrait of
Imchlan Macquarie, the early Governor of
N. S. Wales. Isle of Mull people recognise
it as the correct picture of the old-time Gov-
ernor ; but it differs so much from the
hideous oil painting in Windsor (N.S.W.)
Courthouse that one or the other should be
set on fu*e.

ship of State was on the rocks, with “Socialistic poli-
tical pirates on board. —Electioneering item.
The Great One (watching the distress
signal): “If it wasn't for that scoundrelly

That ferocious warrior, Colonel Ryrie,
M.H.R., recently offered to take on any six
Labor members with the gloves. His chal-
lenge was promptly accepted ; but, lest Ryrie
should still be unsatisfied, the Randwick
Political Labor people have issued a definite
challenge that their candidate, W. Id.
Brown, an old sparring partner of Bob
h itzsimmons, Peter Jackson and others will
box both him and the bellicose Herbert Wood.
More than that, they undertake, if Brown
is defeated, to hand over £lOO to the chari-
ties in the victor s electorate. Of course
nobody expects that there will be any bruis-
ing; still, it is good to see bluff ealied.
Matthew Henry Stephen, ex-Justice and
Acting Chief_Justi.ce of N. S. Wales, hopes
to have an 85th birthday party on December
'? < and among others who expect to be there
is Mrs. \ illeneuve Smith, a daughter, who is
on the way from England with her hus-
Matthew Henry’s father was Alfred,
Chief Justice ; and Mrs. Smith’s other grand-
father was Colonel- Shadforth, one time
Usher of the Black Rod. The lady’s hus-
band, Francis George Villeneuve Smith, is
the only surviving son of Francis Smith,
who, in 1844, was admitted to the Bar at
Hobart Town; was, in 1848, Solicitor-
General, and in 1854 Attorney-General in
Tasmania. He took the latter office condi-
tionally on. being at liberty to oppose the
continued introduction of convicts. Deni-
son, the Governor, was in favor of convict
settlement, and had sacked Chapman, the
Colonial Secretary, because of his opposition.
Later on Francis Smith held many high
offices in the island-State—was member of
the first responsible Ministry, Attorney-
General, Premier, and. subsequently. Puisne
Judge and Chief Justice.
Pen fold’s purchase of Angus’s Minchin-
bury Vineyard was announced last week.
This fine estate, near Rooty Hill, almost a
suburb of Sydney, was a grant of 1000 acres
to Lieutenant William Minchin, the Ad-
jutant of the smellful rum-selling N.S.W.
Corps. This Minchin was a man with a
history. As ensign, he started first for Aus-
tralia in May, 1797, on board the ill-fated
ship Lady Shore, in command of a detach-
ment of recruits who acted as guard of the
convicts on board. On August 1 the sol-
diers seized the ship, and murdered the cap-
tain and first mate. They put Minchin, his
wife, the remaining officers and the famous
Major Semple in a boat, which reached Rio
Janiero, whence they got to England.
Minchin again sailed for Sydney, and, reach-
ing there safely this time, was made adju-
tant of the corps, and remained such till he
left the colony. He was the lieutenant of
the guard sent into Government House on
January 26. 1808, by Major Johnston, to
arrest Governor Bligh, and it was to him
Corporal Michael Marlborough handed over
the Governor, whom he found in an upstairs
room. Major Johnston selected Minchin to
take his despatches to England, informing
the British Government of Bligh’s arrest; he
sailed in Blaxland’s vessel, The Brothers, in
May, 1808, and returned in August, 1809,
only to be sent back again by Lieutenant-
Governor Paterson to give evidence against
Bligh in the Johnston trial. Soon after-
wards he retired from the corps and settled
on the land at Minchinbury. lie died on
March 25, 1821, aged 45 years, and was
buried in the old Devonshire-street cemetery.
From London: Among the undefended
suits set down for hearing in Divorce is
that of the Countess De La Warr, who is
asking for a restitution of rights. The Earl
has a certain Australian interest, inasmuch
as (1) his first wife was a daughter of that
Brassey who, while Governor of Victoria,
fell off so many things, and (2) he is a
brother of Lady Edeline Strickland. He
is one of London’s most festive characters.
The Brassey lady divorced him 10 years
ago amid a social uproar, suggestive of the
Zoo at feeding time. His present wife is
advancing on him from
a new legal angle—
and one, by the way,
that promises to be-
come exceedingly fash-
ionable during the
coming divorce season.
Lady Randolph Chur-
chill set the vogue.
The lady who doesn’t
want her erring spouse
back at any price asks
the Court to order him
to return to her arms.
The Court does so.
The husband sneers at
the Court. The peti-
tioner then gets her
order. There is no
scandal, no trying ex-
posures in court, and
very, little expense;
and all parties are satisfied, barring the
lawyers. ,\\
“T. 0.1.”: A propos the suggestion that
John Madden should be the next Governor of
Victoria, and the old-time appointment of
Fred Weld as Governor of Westralia and
Tasmania (B. 13/13/’l3). There is another
instance in John Bates Thurston, Governor
of Fiji. Weld was a politician in Maori-
land when he got his promotion, and had
strong, powerful backing in England. Thurs-
ton, on the other hand, landed in Fiji an ob-
scure and (possibly) runaway sailor. lie
got employment in the Consulate in 1866,
being then 30 years of age ; became secretary
to Cakobau, and was special representative
of the dusky king at a conference with Her-
cules Robinson re annexation. When an-
nexation had been accomplished, he was suc-
cessively Acting-Governor, Deputy-Governor,
Assistant High Commissioner, Lieutenant-
Governor (1886) and Governor of Fiji; and
High Commissioner and Consul-General for
the Western Pacific (1887). This last post
he held until his death in 1897. Thus he
never had any official job outside the Fiji
whose service he joined on the bottom.rung.
The very serious-minded Judge Murray,
of S. Aus., has made a joke. Inadvertently
Piper, K.C., stood on the tail of Symon’s
gown, and, when objection was raised, his
Honor described it as undoubtedly a
"riparian” trespass.
THE DEAN
OF
SYDNEY.
W. G. Meudell, one of Melbourne’s best-
known and best-liked mining secretaries,
died the other day, before his time —mostly
of too steadfast a devotion to work. He
was a thin man, who felt his responsibilities
keenly, and hastened death by taking his
office home with him. It was all work and
no play, and, though it never succeeded in
making W. G. a dull boy, it helped to make
him a dead man.
The yarn is told of a very important
person whose appointment in connection
with the transcontinental railways was be-
ing considered. Said a Minister of the day
to a man who had worked with this Im-
portant Person: “You know (say) Jones?”
“Yes,” said the other, “I know" him. A
fine fellow, and just about the slowest in
the world.” “Ah,” said the Minister,
/“that's the one we’re looking for.” And
evidently it was, for he got the big job.
Dead in Melbourne Hospital, Geo. Vesey
Allen, journalist, etc. —of late years mostly
etc. Allen was the breeziest, most irre-
snonsible and best-liked person you could
come across; also lie had the bluest eyes
ever seen in a man. After serving on the
Age, he was for some time in one of the
N.S.W. Government offices under the Chief
secretary’s Department. An eruption like
unto Mount Etna at its worst occurred
therein, and in the smoke Allen disappeared.
A couple of years later the then Principal
Under-Secretary was blinded by the appari-
tion of George Yesey on the official mat, top-
hatted and frock-coated. Allen had become
Ambassador-in-ordinary of the Bendigo
CVic.) Exhibition, and he was entrusted
with the job of booming the show in Sydney.
Some people would have been diffident about
bailing up his old official boss, but Allen
was one of the cheerful souls who are not
diffident about anything.
When Benjamin Lee, the old Sydney
S.M., had his 88th birthday lately, the
papers mentioned a stirring Parliamentary
“scene” in which he had participated. This
is the gist of it. Ben was member for West
Maitland at the time, and the row he raised
involved Jack Robertson, Parkes, Dr. Lang,
William Forster, Speaker Arnold, John
Lucas and many others. Parkes was chiefly
to blame, as he insisted on making a speech
on a letter of Archbishop Polding’s without
laying the paper on the table of the House.
McPherson, member for Central Cumber-
land, was supposed to have used the word
“blackguard.” and Lee, taking it to him-
self, walked across the floor. McPherson
was so little conscious of offence that he
stood up to salute Lee in a friendly way •
but the latter shot his right fist into McPher-
son’s face, and down went McPherson.
Motions for suspension and expulsion fol-
lowed. Meantime McPherson left the
Chamber, and returning with a horsewhip,
struck Lee across .the face as *' the latter
entered the library. The men clinched, and
the police were sent for, as the area of
fight had now extended to the “outside.”
Martin, Attorney-General, raised an inter-
esting point at this juncture. Saul Samuel
demanded that the Attorney-General should
give the combatants into the custody of the
police, but Martin refused, holding that the
magisterial powers of members are sus-
pended while they are in the Legislative
Chamber.
“Geebung” on his travels:—
I called at the N.S.W. Agency, intending- to
my respects to the able gentleman who keeps that
end up for us in London. I knew him as Tim Gogh
lan in the old days, one of the smartest and most
unassuming of the natives we have reared. A bright
young English damsel approached me in the down
stairs office. Her nnen was official, yet affable Save
for my venerable appearance, 1 had none of those
things which impress young English damsels— neither
frock coat nor shiny silk hat. I said, “Is Mr (Ch
lan in?” Changing her manner, she said, with gentle
asperity: “You mean Mr. Coal-un.” I said “Who?”
“Mr. Coal-un!” I said I was after my old friend
the Agent-General, Mr. Timothy Coghlan With
expression in which pity and resignation were beau"
fully blended, she said, “You mean Mr. Coal -nr, ”
“Ah, well,” I said, “I expect he still spells it
C-o-g-h-l-a-n.” “Oh yes,” said she; “but you know
the proper pronunciation is C-o-a-l-u-n ” What
man does have to put up with in London, to be sure?
George Davies, who fox' a decade past was
Speaker of the Tasmanian Assembly anil
for more years still had been Chairman of
Committees when “Nick” Browne was
Speaker, died at Hobart last week. The
late Speaker and his brother, Charlie have
been proprietors of the Hobart Mercury
for a generation—since the death of their
father, “Old Jack” Davies, who founded the
journal. The Davieses also own the weekly
Tasmanian Mail. A third brother has
been for long connected with the newspaper-
agency business of Gordon and Gotch
George Davies was not a brilliant man, but
he was a careful one, and that and his good
sense carried him through any office he held
He was in the Tasmanian Parliament for 3U
years. Brother Charlie has usually man-
aged the Mercury, but whenever George
was in charge, everybody concerned was
glad. He didn’t worry the sub-editor. He
saw that potentate just once —before he
caught the last tram for his home in New-
town —and in a confidential whisper asked
“Is everything right?” Bluff old Ted'
Ings, when he had the job. would blurt out:
“Yes, yes, all light. Good night!” and
George’s supervision was over. George was
Mayor of Hobart when the Yorklets visited
Tasmania, and, when they had gone, it was
thought a sure thing that he would be
knighted ; bvit he wasn’t —he had to wait for
the bauble till he became Speaker.
“Oimai” from the North :
The news that Governor Murray, of Papua, was
recently deserted by his native carriers and left, a
long, lean and perturbed Personality, in the dripping
recesses of some of the worst jungle country in the
world, seems to be causing extreme distress to a sec-
tion of the Australian press. The Brisbane Courier
regards the happening as the outward and visible
sign of the passing of the white man’s authority, and
P. McMAHON
GLYNN, Minister for
Foreign Affairs.
forecasts a sort of Got-
terdammerung for Pap-
uan officialdom. This is
all hot air. Government
officials have been left in
the lurch by their car-
riers before to-day, anti
though the crime of
leaving a semi-sacrosanct
Lieutenant-Governor to
the company of the
jungle leeches may be
unspeakable to all up-
holders of law and order,
Papua can supply prece-
dents. No less a “Gov-
ernment” than the
mighty William Macgre-
gor, who nowadays oc-
cupies a sort of demi-
god’s position in Papuan
legend, was left, at least
once, in the same pre-
dicament This was in
the early days of his
reign, when “the Doc-
tor” —now his Ex. of
Queensland —used to in-
sist on humping his own
swag. His carriers, after
consultation, decided that
this action was highly
unbecoming, unprece-
dented and calculated to
disturb industrial circles.
Moreover, it was doubt-
ful if his Ex. was a duly-
qualified member of the
Carriers’ Union. A sub-
committee having re-
ported that the Gover-
nor’s swag was much
heavier than any regular
carrier would carry, all
doubt on this head was
set at rest. A resolution
was passed that the time
was not yet ripe for the
admission of outlanders
on equal terms to the
local labor market, and
the meeting adjourned
sine die, to its respec-
tive homes. It may be
added that, in Murray's
case, his carriers seem
to have been recruited
from the southern litto-
rals. The best and most
trustworthy carriers in
Papua are those from
the north-east and nor-
thern divisions. Occa-
sionally they may have
eaten their boss, as the
Mamba natives did in
Green's case; blit', gencrallv speaking-, on the ques-
tion of desertion, Mrs. Micawber has nothing on
thprn.
I DON’T THINK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Are there ever times, Mr. Keid, when you feel
that you would like to be back here in the fighting
line, as of old?”
He smiled, and said : “That is one of the things 1
do not want to talk about.”
—lnterview in the S. M. Hkrald.
Young Spoopendyke was sent to see
Our George, and ask him questions plain ;
He wonders if he’d choose to be
At Joe Cook’s elbow once again :
“Now would you like,” says Spoopendyke
“To serve the party, and all that ?”
“Ha-ha!” says George;
“Oho!” says George;
“Young man, what are you getting at?”
For Georgie, with his inward eye,
Sees Deakin fail in ruin sad ;
Sees Joseph Cook go blowing by
In “triumph” that is just as bad:
For Joe is made Prime Minister —-
Who would have dreamt it in the past ?
“Aha!” says George;
“Oho!” says George;
“How long will poor old Joseph last?”
“There’s Joseph scrambling in the mud,”
Says with one eyelid down,
“And Deakin tumbled with a thud,
But I am safe in London town!
I’m ft-ted, banqueted, and wined,
And feel contented —wouldn’t you?
Except” (says George)
“Just this” (says George)—
“I’d like a little extra screw.”
When all the Fusion went to smash
Upon an unforgotten day,
George sat serene above the crash
And drew five thousand far away.
Come back to it? Not on your oath!
That needs no X-ray sense to tell!
“Trust me!” says George,
The same old George:
“I left before the rainstorms fell.”
N.S.W.

(8 p.c.)
Sept., 1913.. 80,613 55,000 22,000 3,613 198,425
The unexpected thing there was the 8 p.c. paid on
the pref. shares. Six months ago, in reporting the
accidents and disasters of the half-year, the Board
said: “The assets have been revalued and sundry
reserves absorbed, which enabled the directors to
issue 550,000 £1 cumulative 6 p.c. pref. shares fully
paid.” At the meeting at which the issue was fore-
shadowed a resolution was passed authorising the
directors to issue shares “with a preferential or quali-
fied right to dividends.” When the new lot were
issued as “cumulative 6 p.c. pref. shares” it was
assumed that they would not be entitled to more
than 6 p.c., and they were trafficked in on that
assumption at about 265. Then came the 8 p.c.
announcement, and they leaped nimbly to about
31s. Possibly the argument is that people who get
bonus shares and promptly run them on to the
market don’t deserve any consideration ; still, as the
shareholder may have wanted the money to buy
beer or do some other virtuous act of which this co.
would approve, it was a pity the rights of these new
shares were not made quite clear when they were
issued. It seems an extraordinary thing, anyhow,
that bonus shares, besides carrying a cumulative prefer-
ence, should be entitled to soar up to any heights in
the matter of dividends. It isn’t usual to single out
bonus shares for any such special favors as that.
As usual, the co.’s figures have a fine lot of
sparkle in them. Profits, it is true, were better in
the March-September half of the previous year ; but
£lO,OOO of the shortage in the past term is accounted
for by a gift of that amount made to a fund estab-
lished “for the benefit of all the employees, in the
event of death or disablement while in the co.’s ser-
vice.” Still, even after excluding this £lO,OOO, the
half-year’s profits are better than the average of the
wintry periods. The financial position of the co. is,
of course, very fine: —
Sept., ’O3. Sept., ’l3.
Liabilities . . . . . . £30,687 £43,589
Assets.
Breweries and properties, etc. .. 644,913 1,229,444
Book debts 242,375 213,358
Stock 88 959 171,428
Liquid assets . . . . . . 143,969 369,784
A small part of the very big jump in the “breweries
and properties” item may be due to the re-valuation
made last year. Still, here are the facts. Without,
getting in one penny of capital from the sharehold-
ers, and after paying a dividend of from 8 to 10
p.c., the co.’s net assets increased from £1,089,529
in 1903 to £1,940,425 in 1913 —which means an
average of £85,089 per annum for the whole period.
Possibly, as remarked just now, some of it was due
to the increment in values, ascertained by re-valua-
tion ; but the bulk of it —say two-thirds of it, at
least —consisted of undivided profits Trom trading.
In fact, the position may be better even than that,
because in 1903 “breweries and properties, etc.,”
included goodwill, whereas goodwill has now been
cut out of the assets altogether. It is true that very
little visible profit is left after the past half-year’s
dividends have been paid ; but (1) the March-Sep-
tember half-vear invariably gives poorer results than
the other, and (2) various internal reserves were
disclosed a while ago, and there is no reason to sup-
pose that the practice of stuffing undisclosed profits
into inner contingency reserves has been dropped.
What effect legislation may have on this co. and
its profits The Bulletin doesn’t know. The legis-
lator is always liable to break out in quite unex-
pected places ; who, for instance, would have believed
that the N.S.W. Labor party, which stood for sane
finance, would have dropped into the wildest borrow-
ing orgy and the maddest finance ever known in the
State ? “ That orgy has, incidentally, played into the
hands of this and everv other landlord. And, curiously
enough, the anti-liquor party is also playing
into the hands of this and the other big brewers, in-
asmuch as, besides choking off all competition, it is
driving the more timorous and weaker ones into the
arms of the big fellows. Thus during the last month
or two Tooth’s have purchased the Maitland brewery.
The anti-liquor people curse “the brewery mononoly.”
and all the time are helping to make a monopoly
of what certainly wasn’t a monopoly when the pre-
sent crusade began.

Marcus Clark and Co., Ltd., who carry
on a furnishing and drapery business in Sydney and
elsewhere, and do much of it on a time-payment
basis: —

1909.. 35,473 6542 8 „ „ 12,712 2660 3,401
1910.. 39.640 7500 8 „ „ 14,000 3167 18,374
Insurance Fund dropped in, £1920. . 20,294
1911.. 43,482 8250 8 „ „ 15,000 2788 37,738
1912.. 51,430 9000 8 „ „ 16,000 2946 61,222
1913.. 36,121 9000 8 „ „ 16,000 3379 68,964
The public came into this co. in 1905-6, three or
four years after registration ; before that the business
had been carried on by partners who retained the
bulk of the control. There were three Clarks then
in it, two of them being on the Board. Both those
two, though comparatively young, have died in the
brief seven years that have since passed. These good
men left, of course, the results of their organising
work behind ; and, though there may be just as capabl
men to take their places, the premature loss of
men who had been mainly responsible for making
the business shows how necessary it is, in any such
concern, to quickly write down goodwill out of pro-
fits. This co. attended to that matter splendidly.
The price paid for goodwill was £72.377 ; all that
remains of it now is £20,000 —in other words, ex-
cept for that amount, the paper has all been replaced
out of profits by tangible assets. But the co. has
done more than that. Getting its pref. capital when
money was comparatively cheap, it pays only 6 p.c.
on £150,000 ; and on its ordinary capital (now
£200,000) it has resisted all temptations to pay
more than 8 p.c. The result is that, starting just
over 10 years ago, with tangible assets £72,377
less than its capital, it has now tangible assets
£68,964 in excess of its capital; which means that
in these few years its profits have exceeded the divi-
dends by no less than £141,341. In the six years
since the public first came into the co. the cut-up has
been as follows: —
Paid away in dividends. . . . . . £132,004
Written off leaseholds, plant and fixtures 17,911
Put to reserves .. .. .. .. 91,754
Total profit (six years) .. ..£241,669
It is a most creditable performance.
Compared with the previous year, the past year s
profits don’t look well. Part of the big fall is,
however, due to the previous year's figures having
been inflated by a sale of freehold at a profit of
£8026. Even so, there is a considerable’ shrinkage.
Calculated on the capital in the business and the
undivided profits, the return is, indeed, smaller than
in any year of the past seven. In 1906, when the
co. made £.30,656, the capital was £250,000; now,
with £350,000 of capital and over £lOO,OOO more
of undivided profit, the co. has made only £36,121.
'ibis, of course, isn’t pleasant; nevertheless two
things can be said: (1) the co.’s freeholds and lease-
holds are now considerably greater, and when you
have the security of city property you can’t expect
to get a high interest-return for the money put into
it; (2) the surplus profit, after providing for the
cumulative pref. dividend and the depreciation of
leaseholds, etc., was equal to very nearly 12 p.c. on
the ordinary capital. Put in another way, profits
might shrink £7742 before the co. would have in-
sufficient to pay the present 8 p.c. on ordinaries. They
probably will shrink. No doubt this co. has been
spending a good deal of money on which it has got
little or no return during the past year ; but that is
not likely to be a full set-off against the shrinkage
of trade and profit which will follow the collapse of
the borrowing boom and the largely artificial prosperity
which every borrowing boom produces. Last year
(to June 30, 1913) the Government of N. S. Wales
spent £3,053,362 more loan money than was ever
previously spent in a year by the wildest boomster
among State Governments. The total State expendi-
ture was £25,889,811, or £4,005,000 more than in
the previous year ; and not only was a vast amount
of loan money spent on loan works, but over
£1,000,000 was spent on ordinary services in excess
of the revenue. It is certain that the borrowing
cannot continue; but that won’t end the trouble —
we shall not only have to make good last year’s
million deficit, but we shall have to get £1,000,000
more of revenue from somewhere or cut £1,000,000
off the ordinary services somewhere. Good seasons
may relieve us of some of the effects of this mad
financing, but a certain amount of depression is posi-
tively inevitable; and Marcus Clark’s, being to a
considerable extent a furnishing business (largely on
time-payment), looks as though it might suffer
rather beyond the average from the slump.
Though, as mentioned above, the capital has been
increased by £lOO,OOO in the past few years, and
though large amounts of profit have been put aside,
the co. has launched out so vigorously that its lia-
bilities to the public have grown, too: —
1906. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Mortgages .. £40,800 £50,000 £42,000 £40,000
Other debts.. 65,262 81,017 111,189 107,910
Total . . £106,062 £131,017 £153,189 £147,910
Until the total is much smaller than it is at pre-
sent, the management will do the right thing in
sitting hard on the dividend. If business eases off,
it will, no doubt, be fairly simple to reduce the
figure still further than has been done during the
past year. This shows where the money is: —
1906 1911. 1912. 1913.
Stock. . . . £96,965 £209,271 £214,587 £211,175
Book debts .. 124,289 175,791 193,116 182,477
Freeholds . . 77,093 92,451 82,455 82,159
Leaseholds . . 14,093 26,751 42,827 64,220
Plant, etc. . . 17.354 18.428 19,433 19,033
Cash, etc. . . 3,943 8,564 24,492 20,310
So it will be seen that, though over £22,000 has been
put into leaseholds during the year, the liabilities
have actually been reduced. It has been done by
shortening stock and book-debts ; and, in view of the
liabilities which are still big and the outlook which
is still thundery, the movement ought to continue.

Farmer and Co., Ltd., with its office in

1907.. 22,223 12,000 7 „ 10,936 226
1908.. 28,702 12,000 8 „ ~ 12,498 4,430
1909.. 32,706 12,000 8 „„ 12,498 12,638
1910.. 35,206 12,000 8 „„ 12,498 23,346
1911.. 38,945 12,000 10 „„ 15,623 34,668
1912.. 43,027 12,000 12£ „ „ 19,529 46,166
1913.. 47,223 12,000 12! „ „ 19,529 61,860
In one shape or another Farmer and Co. date back
to the infancy of the Oldest Inhabitant; but the pre-
sent co. only arrived in 1897, and Australia had very
little to do with financing it. When it was formed,
the vendors took ordinary shares, and John Bull sub-
scribed £150,000 in 6 p.c. cumulative pref. shares.
Subsequently other issues were made, and the capital
is now £200,000 in £lO prefs., and £156,230 in
£lO ordinaries. There are 4377 ordinaries unissued.
Five or six years ago, when Australia was handling
big wool and wheat cheques, shares, both pref. and
ordinary, began to pass out to this country in consid-
erable numbers; and in several ways besides that,
Farmer and Co., Ltd., has been growing more Aus-
tralian ever since. Its position is exceptionally
solid. Down to 1907 it had practically no reserves,
and didn’t bother about such things; it was prac-
tically a private concern, and the owners cut up
profits as they were made. That left the co. appar-
ently weak; yet it was doubtless stronger than it
looked, because the rule seems to have been, when
building improvements were made, to pay for them
out of earnings, and charge the cost up along with
working exes. And this rule seems to have been
followed for some time afterwards, so that, though
many thousands of pounds were certainly spent
between 1908 and 1911, the premises account actu-
ally stood at rather less in the latter year than in
the former. Since then, however, big purchases and
additions have been made, and to-day freehold pre-
mises and plant stand in the books at £328,121, or
£102.000 more than in 1911. The remarkable thine
is that there has been no increase of capital and
none of fixed debt or mortgage in making these pur-
chases-, ‘etc. Cash at bankers has been drawn upon to
some extent; sundry creditors (apparently trade
creditors) have increased by £27,000 ; for the rest
the undivided profit has seen the co. through. Even
with the increase noted in trade debts, the liabilities
(including mortgage) amount to only £135,583.
Against capital, reserves and liabilities there are
£ 585,204 of tangible assets. (For some unknown
reason goodwill is still allowed to lumber the
balance-sheet —£30,000.) Of the £585,204 of tan-
gible assets, no less than £328,121 is in freehold
property and plant. Book debts are only £28.653
The balance is nearly all stock. Put it this way.
On July 31 the co. had enough liquid assets (includ-
ing book debts) to pay off £35,929 of its liabilities,
leaving £99,654 to pay. To represent that and the
capital (£356,230) there were: Stock, £188,050;
freeholds, £328,121; and a few comparative trifles
in the way of leaseholds. After providing for all its
liabilities, this co. can show 64 p.c. of its capital in
city freehold premises; and a co. which can have that
proportion of its capital in such gilt-edged securities
and still pay p.c. on its ordinary shares, may
reckon itself in luck. But this CO. can do more: it
can pay 124 P-e. and put by enough to pay another
10 p.c. The Bulletin doesn’t for a moment sup-
pose such profits will last for ever. Sydney has been
passing through a few years when it hasn’t mat-
tered much what the drapery cost : and no doubt the
goods have been marked accordingly. It will matter
some day. When that time comes. Farmer's share-
holders will at any rate have the blessed satisfaction
of knowing that the splendid property asset is at
the back of the capital, anyway.

This is the Herald’s turn to be sorrowful. “It will
be observed,” the directors say, “that the profit is
a reduction on the amount earned in the preceding
period. This is accounted for partly by the cost of
the new cable service, and still more by the largely
increased cost of producing newspapers generally” ;
and then, without a thought, of course, for the
threatening Mirror., the directors proceed into
details, explaining, among other things, that “the
wages and salary increases of the last 34 years
amount to upwards of £lO,OOO per annum.” This
reviewer doesn’t question the figures. Nevertheless,
he notes that the co. never before showed anything
like the profits it did in the 12 months ended
March, 1913. From that it would seem as though
the co. flourished on higher expenses. And no doubt
it has done so ; like a thousand other concerns, it has
got threepence for every extra tuppence it has had
to pay out. Now, this would be all very splendid
if things would continue like that. But the chances
are they won’t. The extra tuppence, or the greater
part of the extra tuppence, will continue to be called
for long after the extra threepence has left off coming
in. It is easy to put up wages and salaries and in-
crease other exes, on account of boom business and
receipts; it is the Devil’s own job to null them
down again. So, while The Bulletin isn’t as deeply
impressed as it ought to be with the sudden slump in
profits and the incidental lamentations, it is quite
prepared to admit that profits may presently shrink
as much below normal as lately they have been above
normal.
However, let the Herald thank its lucky stars
that, though it started with a terrific loading of
goodwill, and though nearly all Melbourne has said
unkind things of it at some time or other, it has
blown off nearly £BO.OOO of the froth in the past
eight years. Or take this view over seven years
