The capital is now £202,900 in ordinary shares of
255. each, and £50,000 in 6 p.c. pref. shares. Half
the ordinary capital was distributed in free shares
three years ago; and the pref. capital has lately
been doubled at par. The co., which was started in
Melbourne during the Victorian boom, did poorly for
some years, but was on hand when the Coolgardie
rush led on to the great things of Kalgoorlie and
other fields. It was thirsty work, that; and this co.
was soon in clover. As money was made, the surplus,
after paying a fat dividend, was mostly put into
pub property; and to-day that asset stands for
£250,354 in the books. This is cost price. Nothing
has been written off for depreciation, which is a
mistake. It is, no doubt, quite true that some of
the properties are worth a heap more than they were
when the co. bought them ; but that is no reason why
the wear and tear of the buildings shouldn’t be paid
for out of the rents those buildings earn. They will
have to be replaced some day, and the replacement
ought to be provided for out of profits—to the ex-
tent, anyway, of the value of the old buildings when
the co. “bought them. Still, though the co. doesn’t
write off anything for depreciation of buildings, this
paper isn’t going to accuse it of inflating its profits.
On the contrary, it has all along written down its
plant severely, until what cost £144,789 stands in
the books at £73,310. Also, repairs and mainten-
ance of properties run into an average of close on
£7UOO a ycm\ Apart from the freehold brewery
premises, and the plant, and the quarter-million or
hotel properties, the co.’s chief assets are advances
( £41,973), book debts ( £29,943) hnd stock
( £32,042) ; but as against these are mortgages,
etc., for £57,462. Freehold rents, apparently aftet
providing for interest on the mortgages, brought in
£9892 last half-year—enough to pay the pref. divi-
dend and 5 p.c. on the ordinaries, after allowing for
repairs and maintenance. The trading part of the
business did the rest.
As for the future—well, in the first place, this
is a co. whose business is to sell beer and, inci-
dentally, extract rent for pub properties; and beer
and pubs have many enemies. Westralia doesn’t look
like a place where the Wowser would be a power
in the land ; yet, no doubt for that very reason,
more has been done in that State than in any other
towards making the liquor business a State-owned
monopoly. Progress along those lines is, however,
very slow ; and there doesn't seem to be much danger
to this co. from that source for many
years to come. There is much more danger
of a slump in profits resulting from a general
slump in the State’s prosperity. In the past 14
years £91,000,000 worth of gold has been dug up
out of Westralia, and well over £50,000,000 of that
and subscribed money has been spent locally; and.
meantime, another £15,000,000 has been borrowed
and spent by the State. No small State could fail
to boom under such circumstances. But the gold
output is easing off; borrowing is maintained onlv
with difficulty and at high rates of interest; and
many agricultural failures seem inevitable. These
things point to ona conclusion. Of course, Westralia
isn't going to crumple up. But it will have to settle
down to corned beef and carrots after its gorgeous
time of turkev and truffles. Some businesses may be
affected thereby hardly at all ; but the liquor busi-
ness certainly will be.

Big warehouses in Melbourne are very largely inter-
ested in this co. ; in fact, it is to a great extent a
co-operative manufacturing concern. Hence a market
is more or less assured. In the past year fashion
favored the co. through “an exceptional demand for
ladies’ felts” ; also, the co. secured a contract for
military hats. Thus, one way and another, it had
a second remarkably fine 12 months. Having, as
before remarked, a close connection with various big
warehouses, it doesn’t have to spend very large sums
in roping in customers ; also, “no provision for bad
or doubtful debts is considered necessary.” Capital
(watered out of reserves, as shown above) is now
£54,006; and though that is a small amount for a
business of this size, the co. manages to get along
without any mortgages or other borrowed money, and
its liabilities on all accounts run to only £8162.
The assets include the factory (freehold) at Abbots-
ford, plant, stock ( £22,166), book-debts ( £19,694)
and sundry cash and other credits amounting to
about £7OOO. Altogether the co. is on a very fine
wicket, mainly the result of the trade connection
before-mentioned. Still, a trade connection won t
make custom if the public hasn’t the money to buy
a hat; and the large capacity of the public to buy
hats during the past few years is reflected in the
first column of the table.

You are not permitted to know more about this than
most coal-mining cos. Curiously enough, coal shares
are regarded as “investments” by the Stock Exchange,
and not as mining speculations; yet the big copper or
silver mining concern which didn’t give, or at any
rate profess to give, far more information than the
most talkative coal co., would be howled at till
every dog in the city was awake. About all that is
known in this case is that the co. owns the Last.
Greta and Stanford Merthyr coal-mines and the Last
Greta railway, valuable properties all of them.
Some years ago the co. plainly wrote down its min-
ing properties out of profits; and probably it is
doing the same now —if there is anything more t 0
write down. Six years ago the mines had f been
written down to under £20,000. Nowadays
ing and house properties, railway and _ sidings are
lumped, and the value put upon them is £102,021).
There is 244 miles of substantial 4ft. BAin. railway,
and the co. has the right to carry passengers as we
as freight over it. When 24} miles of substantial line is
cut out of £102,026, there isn’t much left to represeni
the mining properties and houses. The co. s capita

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

pleasure in advising you that I have now used the

Engineers will be interested to know that in the
year 1889 the Victorian Water Supply Department
laid a line of 24in. diameter W.I. Pipes, to supply
the city of South Melbourne with water. These
pipes were coated with asphaltum composition in
the usual way, and were made by Mr. Mephan Fergu-
son. The ground in which they" were laid was parti-
cularly unfavorable to the preservation of any unpro-
tected metals, being originally swampy land contain-
ing much vegetable matter, and filled with tidal
dredgings. Recently the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works effected alterations, and installed a
new main. The old pipes were, therefore, taken up,
and on examination were found to be in an excellent
state of preservation, and the Board’s engineers were
so satisfied with their condition that they decided to
use them again. They were therefore removed to the
Works of Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary
Limited, at Footscray, Victoria, where these 24-year-
old pipes were cleaned, re-tested and re-coated with
asphaltum composition in a new and improved pro-
cess. They are now being re-laid in a position where
they will have to withstand a working pressure of
1351 b. to the square inch. That the asphaltum pro-
cess for coating pipes is rust-proof and thoroughly
efficacious is proved by the foregoing.*

Again an unusual amount is shown as written off,
£20.859 of it being due to encroachments on the
Reserve Fund to square things up over the North
Lyell fire. The total direct cost of that disaster, in-
cluding compensation, was £42,141, all of which
has now been met. The indirect cost was probably
greater; for, though ore which isn’t taken out to-
day mostly waits to be called for to-morrow, there
is the loss of interest on capital to be taken into
account. The co. has had a pretty long struggle to
get things back to the old state again ; but it
arrived there recently. The figures for the current
half-year should therefore be normal. Those for the
period under review are not.
The figures of most practical consequence in the
present report relate to reserves. A re-estimation has
been made, and the calculations work out as follows:
Quantity. Copper. Silver. Gold,
tons. p.c. oz. oz.
Mount Lyell . . 2,202,335 053 1.96 .027
North Lyell . . 1,086,112 6.00 1.33 .005
Now take the ore treated since the present co. was
formed in 1903:
Quantity. Copper. Silver. Gold,
tons. p.c. oz. oz.
Mount Lyell . . 2,475,956 0.81 1.94 .060
North Lyell . . 1,108,237 6.07 1.52 .004
The shrinkage in the copper value of Lyell ore is
serious; it takes three tons of ore to give' the return
which two tons formerly gave, and’ the cost of
recovery is much greater. Still the reserves are verv
fine: there is nearly as much metal proved in the
mine as the co. has taken out of it in 10 years,
during which it has paid 295. per share in ‘ divs.
( £1,770,000). The copper contents of the ore
treated since 1903 were 87,300 tons ; in the ore now
proved, 76,700 tons. The gold contents of ore await-
ing treatment are about 8500 oz. less. It is not
possible, though, to make a simple proportion sum
of it, and say “If 3,500,000 tons of ore have given
295. in dividends, how much will 3,200,000 tons give?”
A dozen things may creep in to cause a disturbance.
Costs are thus a most important factor. These are higher
now ; on the other hand, the co. is no doubt making
a lot of money out of its chemical works, which
are a comparatively modern installation.
Last half-year, as mentioned just now, production
and costs were not normal. Still, take that term
as a basis. The input to the treatment plants from
the Lyell and North Lyell mines was 129,725 tons.
The co.’s profit (after writing off depreciation) was
e42,l4l=about 6s. Bd. per ton, assuming that all
the profit was from these ores. At that rate, the
reserves would be worth £1,096,000. To that add
the surplus of liquid assets, £486,498. Total,
£1,582,498. There are 1,286,786 shares=2ss.
per share. That valuation is based on last half-
vear’s work, and should, under the exceptional cir-
cumstances, considerably understate the position. No
credit is taken directly for the ore reserves of the
Comstock and Tharsis; and, so far as the former is
concerned, it is very probable that good profits will
be made by the flotation process. Last half-year a
few thousand tons of ore from these mines were
smelted, and whatever profits they yielded have been
credited to Lvell and North Lyell. This is some set-
off against the non-inclusion of estimates for the
Comstock and Tharsis.
And then there is the superphosphate business. The
profits from this are thrown into the heap. The co.
has never given the smallest indication of what this
business is worth ; profits have always been tossed in
with those from mining and smelting. What profit
the copper and precious metals yield can thus only be
guessed at. The 6s. Bd. per ton above-mentioned in-
cludes the profits from the fertiliser business; and
the maintenance of that profit is (given similar con-
ditions) dependent on the maintenance of profits from
the side-line. It is a fairlv safe assumption that
things with this business will get no worse. Agri-
culture is growing and will certainly go on growing,
and with it will grow the demand for fertilisers.
The future of the business is, therefore, very bright
indeed—and the future, so far as profits are con-
cerned, isn’t likely to be any the less bright because
of the arrival of a “pup” co. to take over the in-
terests of this and the other principal artificial manure
business is S. Aus. This “pup,” as mentioned last
week, is to have a capital of £400,000 —a pretty
good indication of the size to which this industry
has already grown, seeing that the new co. is con-
cerned only with the business in one State.
* * * »
Broken Hill Junction North reports a
nrofit of £36,619 for the past half-year, which is
highly respectable. The mine is the co.’s weak
point; reserves are poor, and the purchase and treat-
ment of other cos.’ by-products is an uncertain reed
to lean on. There have been big profits from it of
late, certainly; but everything has been favoring
them. This co. lost a valuable asset lately when
Owen, the young Australian who has been doing fine
work with the selective (flotation) process, was
snapped up for an American job.
* * * *
In its early days Broken Hill used only
Australian hardwoood for underground timbering;
but American managers came in on a flood, and
thereafter, for many years, only Oregon was looked
at. Lately the pendulum has started to swing the
other way, and Block 14, among others, is finding
that the change pays. The old softwood is still
most used, but the proportion in Block 14 has got
down to three of Oregon to one of hardwood.
* * * *
Golden Ridge (W.A.) denies the soft
impeachment that it is about to acquire the Yerilla
King group. So far, the Board has not made up its
mind one way or the other, and it may be weeks
or months before it does so. Meantime the pro-
perties are locally stated to be opening up fairly
well, and a winze started at 230 ft. level is reported
to be in fair values.
At the Lake V iew and Oroya meeting, in
London, chairman F. A. Govett said, in regard to
the Burma mines, in which the co. holds a large
and hitherto unprofitable interest, that a big lode
had been opened up which justified the expectations
of the past. But, according to Hoover, it is ex-
tremely doubtful if this lode is amenable to any of
the processes now in commercial use for treating
lead and zinc ores. However, there is also a copper
lode. Its width is irregular, and its average size at
present unknown ; but 14 p.c. assays have been got
over a width of 36ft. More money has been put up,
and this year ought to show the real value of the
property. When Govett had finished this story,
nobody asked what had happened to all the high
values and brilliant prospects referred to at previous
meetings, or what had become of all the promised
divs. that the prehistoric Chinamen were alleged to
have left behind. Everyone seemed content to swal-
low the chairman’s parting remark that the specu-
lative value was great aud the possibilities varied
and extensive—just as previous meetings had done.
One thing that leaked out at the Lake
v iew and Oroya meeting was that the ore in the
Yuanmi (W.A.) had developed more arsenic in depth,
treatment had consequently become difficult, but
hopes were entertained of obtaining a satisfactory
extraction with the new sulphide plant. So far, re-
sults were declared to be promising. Though the
mine was shaping well, it was candidly admitted
that ore reserves were not sufficiently blocked out to
allow them to regard it as a permanent concern.
Altogether 128.415 tons of ore were ex-
tracted from the Kalgurli (W.A.) during its past
year. This was 4615 tons more than in the pre-
vious year, and slightly more than in any other
12 months. In addition to the ore taken out,
15,170 tons were broken and left in the stopes.
Breaking costs averaged 45., and inclusive of all
local expenditure excepting development (2s. 9d.),
total costs were 19s. 104 d.
Adelaide Westralia Syndicate had the
mournful ending predicted in sorrow by this paper.
It tried to get hold of something at Ora Banda
while the Victorious leases were booming, but before
it had time to attract public attention the field’s
cake was dough. Most of the money subscribed was
expended in fares, telegrams, prospecting, etcetera,
and as a last resort the co. bought 25 Chaffinch
Option shares with the balance of the cash in hand.
A drill is now about to bore to 500 ft. in the old
Chaffinch lease, and on the result of its operations
all the hopes of the Adelaide-Westralia crowd de-
pends. One consolation is that it won’t be kept
long in suspense.
Great Boulder (W.A.) has got a further
extension of its option on the Big Bell leases near
Cue (W.A.). Almost every other day reports are
coming to hand about the good value of the reefs
and lodes in this show, and the splendid crushings
that have been taken out of it. In fact, there is so
much boosting that one feels suspicious.
Edna May Central, which recently
acquired the Myrtle leases at Westons’ (W.A.). has
appointed a manager and proposes to start work
right away. The co. is in 60,000 10s. shares, of
which 24,000 (distributed among the public) are
paid up to 7s. 6d., 24,000 fully paid are in the
possession of the vendors, 2000 ditto were annexed
by promoters, and 10,000 are in reserve. The show
is considered to have prospects, provided sufficient
hard capital is available to show what it is made of.
Mararoa (W.A.) isn’t looking any better,
and winzing below No. 9 level has been suspended
in order to crosscut 40ft. From there it is proposed
to let the diamond drill test the reef 300 ft. deeper
down, and also bore east and west to see if any ore
channels exist. A shaft is being put down on the
co.’s newly acquired Sydney Norseman leases, and
at 200 ft. the reef is of fair size but poor grade.
Values may increase when the sulphides are reached.
* ■* * *
Tindals (W.A.) has turned over a new
leaf, and is now making an apparently honest effort
to keep shareholders posted in regard to develop-
ments. Faul, the new attorney, is mainly responsible
for this change. In a recent report to the Board he
adopts a cautious attitude, which is in marked con-
trast to the old way of doing things. Some good
assays have lately been obtained, and all the work-
ings are reported to be in ore. At last the show is
apparently going to be given a chance to prove its
real capacity—if it has any. This paper fears it
hasn’t much.
* * * *
Payne’s Find (W.A.) is turning out as
The Bulletin feared it would. Development work
has shown the gold to be in narrow shoots, the stone
between the shoots being mostly worthless, and the
country rock much harder than the boomsters repre-
sented. Only one co. —Payne’s Development—is
operating on the field at present, and doubtless it
wishes it had never seen the place. The last six
months’ work resulted in a loss of some hundreds of
pounds, and, to make matters worse, someone stole
the proceeds of the last crushing from the manager’s
dwelling. The thief, the directors say, has not been
traced. Funds are very low, and apparently the end
is not far off.
E. A. Weinberg, who went to Germany
and London to raise eanital for the Hercules- cum-
Primrose-cwm-Zeehan smelter fusion, is not bringing
back a deal. Financial stringency is the reason
given for the failure. The Bulletin isn’t sorry.
There never was much sense in linking up the
Zeehan smelters with the Rosebery mines, especially
as Morse’s renort on the Oker and other processes
showed that the de-zincing of slags isn’t the sure
game some people talked. Meantime there hasn’t
been any stop to the experimenting with the crude
Rosebery ore by flotation So successful has been
the work that Barrier chemists think pretty well
the last word has been said on the subject. If so, a
Primrose-Hercules amalgamation without the smelter
is ahead. That will keep out the European gold-bug
and give Australian brains and cash a chance of
properly handling two decent mines.
“XX”; The Tasmanian Smelting Co., at
Zeehan, has again suspended operations. Last time it
was allegedly for want of ore; this time it is allegedly
for want of coke. The real reason is probably some-
thing else altogether. There certainly seems to be
little prospect of a resumption of work till the com-
pletion of the Hercules, Primrose and Smelting Co.
amalgamation. This amalgamation scheme has been
in the air for some time, and it has been said
vaguely that the necessary capital is assured, but
nothing definite is known. Even should things be
finalised, a process for the treatment of the zincifer-
ous ores has yet to be proved satisfactory. If the process
of zinc-extraction by the Metals-Extraction Co. at
Rosebery is successful (which should be demonstrated
within the next few weeks), it may alter the whole
position. This co., which has the right to the
whole of the product of the Tasmanian Conper Co.,
proposes to extract the zinc, leaving the residues, with
silver, lead, gold and copper contents, for further
treatment. With the zinc extracted, the treatment of
the residues is simple. If this co. succeeds, it may
pay the Hercules and Primrose better to extract the
zinc contents of their ore by this process, and after-
wards arrange for treatment of the residues at the
Zeehan or some other smelters. Meanwhile, the
Zeehan Smelting Co. is in other trouble. The Govern-
ment loaned it £20,000 four years ago, and has
subsidised it to the extent of nearly £7OOO per year
in addition. The co. was supposed to commence re-
payment of the loan in October last, but it
doesn’t appear to be in a position to do any repaying
just yet.
The fall in the price of tin means a lot to
Biscboff Extended (Tas.), but with a nest egg of
£4OOO and £lOOO of mine product on hand it can
go slow till better times put up the price of the
metal again. The co. has about 40,000 tons of ore
proved, averaging close on to a unit of tin. With
that grade it would do well enough, provided there
was cheap power, efficient labor and a decent climate.
Now that the summer is on, a bolder policy of test-
ing the lode formations is to be adopted. If results
add at all substantially to ore reserves, capital will
be available for more plant. This is the only way to
get the Extended out of the “stringer” stage in
which it landed after five years of hard toil.
* * *
For a mine, that has won over £lOl,OOO
worth of gold Nuggetty Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), is in
the poor position of having to shoulder a loan of £lOOO
and to carry on with the aid of regular calls. Luck
simmered out when the church withdrew its patron-
age and flitted to bazaars for excitement. Taken as
a whole, work at the No. 7 level is almost the
limit; but as some few prospects have been got in
the north drive, the shaft is going down to learn
what is below. Seeing the way things have gone
with every other mine at Daylesford at depth, there
isn’t likely to be anything very cheerful in follow-
ing the fortunes of the Nuggettv deeper into the
bowels of the earth. That doesn’t mean that a final
ragtime will not come from Ballarat to enable folk
there to dance out the block of shares they are
holding. Still it won’t be easy to get rid of them ;
a bit more than the two p’s—“promising” and “pay-
able”—is required to bring in buyers for Daylesford
goods nowadays.
Victoria for the first 10 months of the
year produced 382,7510 z. fine gold, or 28,6230 z. less
than for the same term in 1912. This fall accounts
for the dreary state of the Stock Exchange. The
total is sure to dwindle if dredging in the Ovens
Valley is blocked. About the only local mining
tip heard is the Langi gutter, where half a dozen
shows are doddering along until the New Langi
rises into the wash. Mines Minister Billson ought to
compel sharper work in shaft-sinking, or biff loafers
out of their leases.
Williams United, Bendigo, with the aid of
several members of the Lansell family, has been
climbing up the hill, while Catherine Reef, next
door, has been sliding down the slippery slope. In
all 63 calls have had to be paid, leaving the leeway
of £45,000 to be made up to the faithful few who
have stuck to the mine all along. The co. is work-
ing at about 2100 ft. in the big ore channel operated
on north by Catherine Reef. Its last tally of
26-184 oz. from 9544 loads put it in credit, and the
chance is that higher stopes at 2100 ft. will enter
a richer run of spurs. So the co. is tipped to have
a spell of good times. Anyway, to make profits on
s£dwt. stone lifted from 2100 ft. while steady
development is being carried on indicates that Ben-
digo has adjusted its work to the poorer conditions
prevailing.
* * * *
Victoria, which owes more to mining than
any other State, bar, perhaps, Westralia, treats its
State geological staff like this. The average salary
in N. S. Wales is £430, in Westralia £414, in
Queensland £331, in Victoria only £258. Of the
laboratory staffs: N.S.W., £309; Westralia, £293;
Victoria, £195. Kitson got £325 a year in Vic-
toria. He has gone to Africa at £750. Thiele,
who also gets £750 in Africa, was paid £l3O in
Victoria. And Lewis, with £l5O, was transferred
to the Commonwealth, which judged him to be worth
£7OO.
“Rangan”: The Mount Morgan brigalow
question seems threatened with a violent end. The
evidence of various miners was so strongly against
the timber that it was only the influence of the co.
that obtained for it a six-months’ trial in a special
place in the mine. The miners’ unions have now
decided that after the six months are up, they will
not work with brigalow timber, anyhow; and, after
all, it is the miner who takes the risk, so his say
should he loudest.
Mount Elliott (Q.) in the past three years
has produced 16,760 tons copper from 136,175 tons
ore; but you can’t feed a furnace with the metal
that has passed. Last month Elliott, Hampden Con-
sols and Selwyn supplied 4461 tons of ore, which re-
turned 480 tons blister (6370 z. gold and 10560 z.
silver). This smelting of a ton of copper from less
than 10 tons of ore looks all right; but what of re-
serves? Development has been painfully slow, and
not productive of any big discovery below No. 5
level. And we hear nothing about sinking the main
shaft or opening deeper levels.
Blair Athol is the one Queensland colliery
that has caught on with the share market. Its paid
up capital is £37,896 and its market value £85,000.
Rather a stiff premium; but the quarterly divi-
dends are calculated to give 124 p.c. per annum
with shares at 16s. The net profit last half-year
was £5094, and the coal output 45,438 tons. Other
collieries are springing up, and Mount Morgan,
which has been taking some coal from Blair Athol, is
out prospecting for a colliery of its own ; nevertheless,
the Northern demand is big and increasing, and
there are few local mines in any sort of a position
to supply it.
* * * *
Brilliant Extended. Charters Towers,
finished its half-year in fairly good heart. From a
market point of view it ranks only fourth' on the
field. Mills United, Freeholds and Brilliant Deeps
being nominally, at any rate, of greater value; yet
the Extended is leading with regard to tonnage and
profits. Also, its 3000 ft. on the underlay is the
deepest hole at Charters Towers. The Freeholds holds
its superior position by reason of the fact that it is
safe for some good crushings from the new reef ; its
latest return was £1196 from 280 tons. Mills
United crushed a trial lot from No. 12—which gets
an extension of the Freeholds reef —for an average
of £4 6s. lid. The boom in Deeps has been fol-
lowed by a very dull time. The month’s crushing
(717 tons for £3657) was better; but costs are
heavy, and will continue so for some time to come.
The underlay shaft is making progress towards No. 7
level, but the reef met with in sinking is lightly
mineralised. No. 6 east level is in 161 ft., and
excent for one blank the reef has been payable, with
a thickness of from 2ft. to 6ft. Next year may see
a stir in Deeps, when the ventilation is improved.
“W.W.H.”: The last returns of the banks
carrying on business in Victoria show that on Sept.
30, 1913, they had their own notes still in circula-
tion to the value of £120,780, although the Com-
monwealth Note Act came into force in Nov., 1910.
This recalls that one of the boom banks which went
into liquidation over 20 years ago still has over
£3OOO i in hand to pay unpresented notes. It looks,
then, as though the banks are going to make a scoop
on other people’s money represented by notes that
have been lost in fire, flood, wreck, etc. The money
paid in exchange for the paper being the people’s
money, it seems only just that the Federal Parlia-
ment should provide that all money held for payment
of these notes, and not claimed after (say) 10 years
from the commencement of the Australian Notes Act,
should be paid into the Commonwealth Treasury.
The States (or most of them) have taken that action
with regard to unclaimed dividends, so if precedent
is wanted there is plenty of it available.
Following the present fashion, the Apple
Isle's oldest-established timber business, Henry Clark
and Co., has been converted into a limited liability
eo., with a capital of £20,000. The energetic A. J.
Nettlefold is one of the managing directors, so things
should shift. The new co. is keen on manufacture,
but when it bumps against Australia’s hybrid tariff
and Japanese-made goods, the keenness may lose a
bit of its edge.

A WOMAN'S LETTER
Sydney, November 17, 1913.
My Dear Arini, — :
The Mayoral banquet to welcome home
G. H. Reid was held in the handsome vesti-
bule of the Town Hall, under the blaze
of the £506 chandelier. This huge cut-giass
ornament now emits electric lignt, though
it was bought in the wayback ’seventies to
shed gas rays on municipal revels. At 5.30
on the afternoon of the Reid dinner, the
invaluable Layton, who attends to all the
details of these civic junketings, was dis-
covered (as they say in the penny novelette)
in his shirt sleeves. He refused to be inter-
viewed without his coat, and merely waved
his hand (the hand that wasn’t adjusting
green ropes of smilax) towards the glittering
blobs of pendant light that winked through
amber glass. This experiment—the tinting
of the lower tier of the glass globules in the
chandelier —was an enormous success; it
shed the same soft glow on politicians of
every tint. Yet, as the night wore on the
watchful Layton probably eyed his handi-
work with some anxiety. Its color wasn’t
guaranteed to last more than a few hours,
and the good diner might wonder, late in
the evening, whether the cut-glass or-
nament was a chameleon, or a gas jet, or
a white decanter-stopper. Water lilies and
roses in baskets were set in front of Georeie
Reid’s patch of tablecloth. Upstairs, the
corridor windows were opened to allow Mrs.
High Commissioner and a few official dames
the priceless privilege of watching the Lion
feed.
Citizens (at one shilling a head for booked
seats) swarmed into the Town Hall on
November 14, to see G. H. Reid on the plat-
form, and hear his remarks on things in
general. _ Bunting took the cold chill off the
dead white walls. The thunder of the organ
drowned the public boot, which began to
rap its heel on the floor when 8 p.m came
and the entertainment was still waiting for
the leading gentleman and the official under-
studies. The latter stamped in soon after,
while the National Anthem bellowed from the
organ loft. Governor Strickland, looking
much better for his recent snack of country
life, had Mrs. George Reid on his arm.
“Georgie” mfhde a majestic attack on the
platform steps in company with Mayoress
Cocks, but he had to tread the narrow track
that ied to the front chairs alone. His
circumference is smaller than it used to be,
but it still requires quite a yard and a half
of space. Mayor Cocks, clad in the flowing
skirts of office, and ballasted with the muni-
cipal gold plate round his neck, had fair-
haired Thelma Reid at his elbow. Town
Clerk Nesbitt, who had been hovering round
the front to stage-manage the entry, was
swallowed up in a male procession of recep-
tion committee, which climbed into its plat-
form chairs with much shoe-creaking.
Archbishop Wright sank into a seat between
little Miss Reid and her mamma, who looks
about five minutes older than her only girl,
and has the same pale, canary-colored' locks.
General Finn and A.D.C. Campbell and the
Reid boys—nice-looking youths with their
front hair scraoed back off their foreheads
in the present style—bustled into their
places, while J. C. Watson clambered into
a choir seat behind. After a few prelimin-
ary crackers the feu de foie or G. H. Reid
sky-rockets went off to loud applause.
Mrs. Mayoress Cocks opened a new septic
block in the Royal Hospital for Women
the other afternoon. This building is more
or less presided over by the ancient Benevo-
lent Association of N.S.W., so President
Neville Mayman, of the latter agency for
good works, had the official 'right
to plunge into statistics. He dropped a
deficit of about £lO,OOO on the platform as
a cue to Prof. Andy Stuart to appeal
for More Cash. When the company
had recovered from the shock of finding that
still another needy hospital was shouting for
help it trailed off to fortify itself with tea
and buns. The stately MacCallum dame,
little Mrs. George Earp in red-and-blue
plumage, Mrs. Trefle under the brim of her
best hat, Mrs. Philip Morton (a surprise-
packet at a charity meeting) in a light,
summer frock that was up to the last
fashionable gasp in V front and floral
decorations, and Mrs. Neville Mayman in
gobelin blue, surrounded the official teapot.
H.M.A.S. Australia is to be tethered in
Spencer’s Gulf for a while. The cheerful
Patey hopes to snatch a holiday, in the
intervals of the gun-banging at targets
thereabouts, with friends who camp on the
banks of the Murray River. He had a
notion of strapping his barge to the rest of
his shore kit, and carrying it overland as
part of the baggage, but the barge is too
big a burden. Our Chief Mariner will,
therefore, paddle up the Murray per
tripper’s ordinary ferry-boat.
Archbishop Kelly, whose portrait by
Julian Ashton will be an ecclesiastical turn
in the forthcoming .Society of Artists’ Show,
was the model “sitter” every artist is look-
ing for. lie patiently stood up some of the
time, gave no trouble, and was as innocent
of vanity as a baby. After climbing the
aggressive little staircase that curls upward
to the studios like a steep corkscrew, the
eminent subject was as cheery as a school-
boy, and as biddable as a lamb. He gave a
full-length standing, and allowed the train
of his best Sunday dress to be pulled round
to the front in a beautiful tail, to please the
artist’s eye for color.
The Quinlan Symphony concert in the
Town Hall the other afternoon was another
proof of the magnificent material out of
which Conductors Eckhold and Voghera
carve so much fine music. Voghera, who
cultivates a decided waist to his frock coat,
waved the baton for Mendelssohn’s over-
ture to the “Isles of Fingal.” This per-
formance was possibly the finest work the
orchestra _ achieved in an afternoon stuffed
with musical plums. The swish of the tide
and the boom of the seventh wave (it is
the seventh wave that always makes such
a fuss about its arrival) in Fingal’s Cave
gave the fiddlers some wonderful moments.
Eckhold steered his team through a re-
markably fine attack on Beethoven’s Sym-
phony (C Minor). “Faster than a horse
could gallop,” as they say of the incoming
tide at St. Malo, swept the fiddles in
the final allegro movement. The soloists
were less sensational. Edna Thornton
stormed her way through a good fighting
edition of a Verdi aria (“The Masked
Ball”), Francis Costa gave the Sword Song
(Siegfried) a hearty hammering, and Per-
ceval Allen was handed a chair while the
orchestra had its preliminary innings in a
long excerpt from “Tristan and Isolde.”
John McCormack’s concert in the Town
Hall on Saturday night found the encore
fiends in their old haunts. The famous “II
Mio_ Tesoro,” from Mozart’s “II Don Giov-
anni,” was a gem of vocal art, with the
McCormack tenor gliding over the wonderful
legato _ passages like a streak of silk velvet.
John is going to lift up his voice in Salz-
burg, where Mozart first opened his eyes in
Jan., 1756, at the Mozart revival
there next year. May Huxley’s best contri-
bution to the programme was “Robin
Adair,” in which her mechanical trills are
not wanted. This was an encore number.
Donald Mcßeath fiddled with his usual fer-
vor, and dragged a double encore for Han-
del’s “Arioso” and Kreisler’s arrangement
of “La Precieuse.” Vincent O’Brien spent
the evening at the piano. Mrs. John McCor-
mack was in her accustomed front chair.
An opal, about the size of a pigeon’s egg,
set in diamonds, hung about her neck ; and
by that and the worshipful smile she turns
on her favorite tenor, I imagine John takes
every care of his pretty colleen. The able-
bodied McQuades arrived in sections, dark-
eyed Thelma rather pale after her recent
appendicitis argument. Mrs. Oliver Osborne
contributed a dash of rose color, a relief to
the garish Town Hall lights that blaze on
the powdered and unpowdered nose with no
respect of persons. The David Cohens (she
used to be Dorothy Isaacs, of Melbourne)
were also in the throng.
If, by chance, John McCormack has over-
looked any spare cash in this community,
the Butt-Rumford team will cheerfully col-
lect the same in December. They’re medi-
tating another farewell concert about
Christmas or sooner.
. At a meeting of the Seaside Camps Asso-
ciation, held in the muffled seclusion of the
Town Hall cloak-room, last week, the look-
out committee reported snags ahead. The
notion of bringing batches of women and
children from the Outback to the edge of
the blue Pacific is good ; and some day the
camp, which is to be pegged out near Narra-
been, should be self-supporting. But the
preliminary expenses are giving the hon.
treasurer a very sore head. For instance,
gas lighting: gas ring to each camp, general
cooking and, say, 200 lights, are estimated
at £ooo. _ After this comes sanitary drain-
age (insisted on by the local municipal
council), which begins at £7O. Where it
will end no one presumes to say. The War-
nngah Shire insists next that the bungalows
shall be of nothing less substantial than
wood, and each such humpy is likely to run
into £lOO apiece, at the lowest estimate.
Two kitchens and two dining-rooms for
general use are invoiced at £5OO. These are
a few facts which threaten to push the Sea-
side Gamp into the middle of next summer
or the beginning of next century. Honorary
Architect Stowe submitted this'fearsome list
of exes, to such incurable optimists as Mrs
Bob MacKinnon, organising sec. Grace Bur-
rows. Mrs. James Ashton, R. McMillan and
H. Russell Crane.
The Twilight Home committee, which has
been hiding a few pence in an old stocking
and beating the air for an extra half-crown
or so to go on with, is trying to accustom
itself to a sudden burst of affluence. When
non. sec. Mrs. Innes Noad informed a meet-
mg the other day that Mrs. Alan Carroll
had offered £lOOO towards the fund, the com-
mittee nearly fainted dead away on the floor.
Mrs. Carroll is the widow of the doctor who
founded the Child Study Association—an
institution in which for various reasons she
is no longer able to take her old interest.
I rob aid v it is on this account she is handing
the £lOOO to the hostel for old ladies, as a
memorial of her husband’s battles for suffer-
ing humanity.
In the intervals between taking rest and
refreshment and getting into its best clothes
for the opera, a section of this community
spent its hours last week in committee. The
most notable was the meeting to arrange a
public recognition of the services of Concert-
Manager H. N. Southwell in the cause of
charity. This congregation made a new
record. Instead of interminable speeches
and dreary iteration of a great desire to
collect cash, chairman J. D. Fitzgerald (in
place of Mayor Cocks, who was inadvert-
ently late) rushed the meeting through at
express speed. With a live wire in the
chair, and a crowd of “Southie’s” friends
bubbling over with enthusiasm, over £BO
was collected before you could say, “The
ayes have it.” John Lemmone and Claude
McKay are the hon. secs. The silvery
thatch of George Portus will also be fore-
most in the fray. Mrs. J. L. Trefle was
pounced on as whipper-in of a woman’s
committee, with Mrs. G. L. Goodman as
hon. treasurer. Ernest Broughton handed
in his sympathy and a cheque. Mavor
Cocks flung his weight (about 16 stone)
behind the golden tide. George Titheradge
sent a line to ask the committee to com-
mand his services. The popularity of H. N.
Southwell, who has been laid aside by seri-
ous illness for the last six months, may be
gauged by the fact that every person in the
room looked as pleased to be able to do
something for the stricken favorite as if he
or she had just been given a suburban villa,
with a water-frontage and no taxes.
The rich Miss Ducker, whose father left
her a mere £70,000 a week or so ago, has a
big nest-egg of her own to add to the par-
ental legacy ; so her income is likely to tip
the scales at about £7OOO a year. She is a
charming woman with a heap of girl friends
who love the lady partly for herself, and
partly for her irreproachable taste in
motors. The Edgecliff heiress used to petrol
these damsels about with an unalterable
desire, quite unusual in the motored class,
to give them as good a time as she could.
They will, in consequence, wave very damp
handkerchiefs over her departure in Feb-
ruary. Miss Ducker goes off on a world
tour with Architect Tom Cosh and his
wife, via Japan and China. Cards will be
left with Cousin Jonathan and John Bull
during the scamper. The furniture, etc., of
the Ducker home, Winbah, will be sold to
avoid the perilous adventure which opens
with the notice: “Furnished house to let.”
“Penny Post” Heaton and his wife and
daughter have left this port for the Wet
Country in the Medina.
Dr. Scot Skirving, who once wrote a novel
with the alluring title “Love and Longi-
tude,” has joined the gyeat majority. He
has had an operation for appendicitis, the
most fashionable complaint of the century.
The mighty surgeon MacCormick was in
charge of the knife, and the patient is mak-
ing the usual recovery.
Mrs. Charles Todd, of these parts, leaves
here on December 31, for a scamper to Eng-
land, via Java, Singapore and other coffee-
colored settlements. She is bound for Bir-
mingham. Near that city of tall factories
there’s a famous Training College, where
one Professor Hemming, who is the lady’s
father, has made a record as a stayer. At
the age of 75, this veteran art-teacher
recently retired after 47 years’ service. Prof.
Hemming kept in touch with modern art in
all its wayward moods, and stood in line with
men who were young enough to be his grand-
sons, until _ rheumatism forced him to an
easy chair in the chimney corner.
Architect Griffin, the Chicago man who is
going to lay out the Federal corpse at
Canberra, left for ’Frisco in the Ven-
tura. He is bustling home to pack up his
wife and other valuables and bring them
back to Australia for the term —three years
—of his laying-out contract with' the
Government. Before going, he sent a fare-
well greeting to Miss Rose Scott, with whom
he had a Friday evening corroboree during
his dash through this town.
Artist E. W. Christmas, who played
around the Moonta copper mines of S. Aus.
when he was a boy, and mostly wandered in
Maoriland when he was learning how to
paint, sends a line from London. He has
once more camped on the shores of the
Thames after breathless adventures in South
America, and is showing the results of his
climb over the Andes and adjacent scenery
at various British exhibitions.
Mr. B. F. A. Fawcett, one of Colombo’s
best-looking young tea-men, is meditating a
descent —possibly a permanent one—on
these shores, early in 1914. With one or
nossibly two exceptions, this athletic Apollo
is reckoned the most dashing all-round
cricketer in Ceylon.
Surveyor Cowley, who passed out at
Petersham in his 89th year on November 11,
was one of this State’s strongest links- with
the past. He saw the bush grow into towns,
and watched towns stretch out into cities.
The city of it is that these fine old pilgrims
rarely leave any written records of their
long journey.
The Charles Marsdens are amongst the
homing pigeons who fly this wav early in
the New Year. Mrs. Marsden hasn’t found
the British rainfall good for her health and
son Cecil is in the same plight. The Ene
hsh medicoes order large doses of Australian
sunshine, and the boy may swallow this
Physic on a Queensland station.
The Percy Hunters will leave for the other
side sooner or later—probably sooner. Mrs
1 ercy has _ been thoroughly overhauled and
renovated in a private hospital, after lone
standing bouts of asthma, and is pacing ih P
road to Better Health.
Singer Dolores is packing up her music for
these shores. She leaves the F o g in January
and steps ashore in Sydney before Easter’
Boyd Wells will be at his place before the
piano in this tour.
Singer Mary Conly is to warble here at
the Phil, concert on November 27.
Clever Irene Brown, of the Julius Knight
‘ Mil est°nes” Co., tripped into the stalls of
Her Majesty’s one evening in the middle of
last week. Her intelligent forehead was
buried under a decorative mat of auburn
locks in the most fashionable shade, and the
film of emerald green gossamer that floated
round her shoulders was the envy of every
Titian-haired woman on the premises. After
a look-in at the opera and other sights in
this towm, the Knight team packed up its
grease paints for Brisbane.
The departure of the “Milestones” Co
from strike-infested Maoriland was almost
tragic. Lumpers and porters were all taking
a holiday, and the fearful spectacle was wit-
nessed of Julius Superbus Knight humping
his own bluey.
Soprano Mabel Batchelor has been en-
gaged as Principal Girl for the Adelphi ver-
sion of “Aladdin,” and Carrie Moore is to
appear as Principal Boy. The George
Willoughby co. opens here with the glittering
show on Boxing Night. The “book” is by
young A. E. Martimwho made the Gadfly
buzz very briskly during its brief life in
Adelaide.
Zeni, the stormy 6ft. 4in. Samson of the
Melba Opera Co., is writing to some of his
friends here re his proposed return in 1914.
Zeni is the kind of telephone subscriber who
would shake up that nodding service; he is
the hasty-tempered person who, after a brief
argument with the Sydney “Central/’ is
alleged to have torn an instrument out by
the socket and hurled it from him, as he
hurled the walls of the temple, a crumbling
ruin.
The wedding of the Bathurst damsel,
Marjorie McPhillamy, and ex-aide Captain
Scarlett, occurred with a flourish on Octo-
ber 15 at Datchet, in Buckinghamshire. The
bridal frock and court train were concealed
under lace of great age and much beauty.
Two maidens —the bride’s sister Alison and
her cousin, Valerie Jay—attended her. They
were in old-rose and black velvet, with a
crown of frilly millinery. Assistance was
rendered to the benedict by two brother-
ornaments of the army, and a reverend
cousin of the Bathurst girl attended to the
prayer-book part of the ceremony.
Mr. and Mrs. W. J. Brooke, who have 25
years’ domicile in Sydney to their credit, are
going off to Ceylon for a holiday trip.
W. D. Bunnell, the forerunner of the com-
ing American baseball team, is taking his
bearings of this city. The team is a mighty
Chicago combination of “Giants” and “White
Sox.” An informative acquaintance de-
scribes baseball as the fir~t cousin of cricket
and the half-brother of rounders. As the
Chicago men play it the game is like a
N.S.W. Parliamentary row. when the leader
of the Opposition is hurling his candid
opinion at the Government. The Chicago
men work so hard with their tongues that
floods of language are poured down the backs
of their adversaries. Still, baseball has some
advantages over Amurkan football, which
needs a cemetery within easy driving dis-
tance of the goals.
Ward. Lock and Co. send along “The
Australasian Cookery Book.” It is pro-
fusely illustrated; and, as the recipes are
in alphabetical order, the young bride who
is searching for the right way to disguise
the chops of yestereve can put her finger on
the place while the gravy browns. On the
other hand, the young husband who culti-
vates a profound ignorance of carving, should
avoid the new treasure, for the anatomy of
fowl and beast is therein laid bare in dia-
grams which leave him no excuse whatever.
The osprey, which has only made a timid
appearance at the opera on first nights, has
a rival in the shape of butterfly bows. The
latter can be heartily commended to any
woman who wants to block the view of the
person who sits (and swears) behind her.
The larger the better, of course.
The gallery of Her Majesty’s, during the
opera season, has occasionally sheltered Phil-
istines who have wanted to accompany the
orchestra w r ith remarks about summer sales.
The other night it had a different experience.
The second act of “The Flying Dutchman”
was hurrying to a close, when an annoyed
galleryite suddenly rose to his feet and
stamped towards the exit. Before going he
Anna: his scorn at the Wagner maniacs, who
received it with clenched teeth. “If that's
your ‘Tales of Hoffnumr,’ ” said he, “you can
jolly well keep it; but I'd like to have my
money back.”
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

With six illustrations by Harry Julius,

Mr. Bathgate is new to Australian readers, which is unfortunate for both author and readers,
for Sodger Sandy s Bairn displays such an amount of ability that it seems a pitv he did not ven-
ture sooner. He gives us some splendid pen-pictures of Scotch and colonial types, describes the
early gold rushes and their attendant excitement with wonderful vividity, charms us with his insight
into human nature, possesses a command of the Scotch vernacular that reminds one of J M Barrie
or lan Maclaren, whilst his fund of pawky humor is rare and refreshing. Scotch people especially
will welcome this book and want to know more of its author. ,

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 17, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Denman lady invited the National
Council of Women to a party at Federal
Government House the other Wednesday,
then developed a feverish cold, and was un-
able to receive her guests. Cady Barttelot
and Mrs. Pearson filled the breach,
with the assistance of the august Madden
dame, who joined the reception group. Cham-
berlain Nevill hovered over all, like a well-
tailored guardian angel, and kept up an
endless murmur of apology very soothing to
listen to. Miss Knatchbull-Hugessen gave
her moral support. The National Council
was indulging in its eleventh annual con-
giess, and some International Council identi-
ties were present. A great dock of these
serious women fluttered in the drawing-
rooms, and the plumage looked distressingly
expensive. The talk didn’t seem as good as
the hats ; but that was only to be expected.
Suitable varieties of food were left, about
in tea-meeting style on small tables in the
ballroom, and the chatterers sat down and
attacked it with great skill and energy, while
the house party moved about from point to
point, and urged them on. It was quite
unnecessary; but, then, so was the whole
function. Mrs. Pearson was a vision (a
vision is quite different from a sight) in
ivory lace with a fat sapphire-blue velvet
:sash. The Barttelot dame wore a blue sash
on a white muslin dress, perhaps in memory
■of childhood’s happier picnics. Miss Knatch-
bull-H. put a little lilac coat over her white
chiffon. The Madden dame wore a serious
;air and a brown dress.
Just as the gossip started to sizzle, the
thunder boomed wrathfully, as if Providence
was annoyed about it, and large hailstones
ipelted down from Heaven. The G.-G. had
•escaped the squaw party by going off to
Kooyong for polo practice. But the hail
was unkind, and drove him back just in
ttime to say good-bye to his wife’s visitors.
He blew in breezily, with an attendant
group of his Gentleman Help playmates,
and assisted in bowing the women out like a
polite boy. The big. purple an,d gold foot-
men with the white silk limbs i(I keep
these menials out) are getting quite used to
the invasions of congress women, female
leaguers and so on. They have been made
so free of the house lately that they run in
and out almost unasked.
While the company tea-ed the Women’s
Orchestra accompanied the conversation with
sweet airs. The girl musicians wore big
black hats and sashes round the white
frocks, and generally looked like heroines
out of the latest society novel.
The captain and officers of the Encounter
duly gave their dance on the Monday fol-
lowing race-week. The poor Society squaws
complained that they were tired to their
very bones; but, notwithstanding that, the
young ones danced all night. I also wish to
remark that the same brisk matrons carried
off the partners from their rightful owners,
i.e., the flappers and young girls who are
just full of romance. There were unattached
girls everywhere —most of them pretty
enough to eat; but they could not get dances.
“The girls all turn up — of course!” was the
complaint lodged by one weary Nelson. .The
smart young matron who chaffs and isn’t
stampeded by a little sailorly daring is in-
variably the target of these young men when
programmes are being made up. The poor
flappers who are full of Charles Garvice
notions and polite blither about theatres are
left to languish alone or in bunches, exactly
as preferred.
One or two of these naval lads want kick-
ing—not mere slapping, mind you, but good
hard kicking. The worst example stealthily
mounted the gangway and whispered in a
young dame’s ear, “Is the sainted Agatha
aboard?” “She is!” chuckled the bare
shoulders. “O Lord!” groaned the brass
bounder. “I met her at one port, and she has
chased me right round the continent!” Talk
about she-cats after that! Fancy giving the
poor girl into the hands of the enemy in
that way ! And probably the little boy. was
just spiteful because she had flirted with a
rival buttons.
Ordinary civilian youths with company
manners said “Dash it!” next day, when
they heard that the girls were sitting three
deep, the prettiest just hungry to book three
waltzes with anybody at all. The plain-
clothes lads had refused the dance in large
numbers, because they anticipated being
cold-shouldered by the girls in favor of the
infernal uniforms. Nesta Fitzgerald wore a
gold-rimmed eyeglass; but even that quaint
and piquant touch did not draw. Pretty
Sheila Chisholm, from Sydney, wore black
chiffon and put cherry blossom round her
hair and cherry bobs in her ears. Mrs.
Leslie Stuart danced every dance. Her
cherry-colored ninon had a gold buckle aft
above the waist and a wisp of tulle for’ard ;
and a sleek mermaid train was steered clev-
erly through the raging seas of feet. A few
staid dowagers settled themselves heavily
amongst cushions under an awning in the
bows; but, as frequently happens at dances,
the majority of the guests were young.
They found the sitting-out places very cosy,
for a sailor can do a lot with a 6-inch gun,
a palm and a couple of flags. Fortunately
the night was starry and soft and the moon
on duty. Supper, being a warship spread,
was a splendid interlude in a rather patchy
evening. If the poor flappers couldn’t use
their toes, they at least got some exercise for
their jaws.
A beautiful example of parental affection
was glimpsed at one of the recent race meet-
ings. During the vice-regal procession to-
wards luncheon, young Thomas Denman, the
small boy of the party, seemed to be in
danger of being overwhelmed by the crowd.
Without an instant’s hesitation, his illus-
trious pa caught him up, in much the same
manner as the common laboring person
seizes his young, and bore him, with a do-or-
die air, in the direction of the food. An
agitated male Help fluttered distractedly
after him, waving white and helpless hands
above the heads of the encompassing crowd.
“Ai’ll take him, sir!” he wailed. But
the brave father never faltered or glanced
behind. Panting and perspiring from the
unusual exercise, he carried his infant
through the crush, while the following Help
wrung his hands and exhibited other suit-
able symptoms of intolerable mental an-
guish. Some outsiders who were mechani-
cally elbowing for room and didn’t know
who was who or why, thought the sensation
was over a case of sudden illness.
Little Tommy Denman is trying to be a
good sport, just like Dad. On Cup Day the
youngster sat in the official crate with a
race-card in his little fat fingers, and
gravely recorded the scratchings for each
race. Then he would sit back, push his hat to
the back of his head and thoughtfully
a stub of pencil. He has the two prominent
front teeth of the Best English families.
Mrs. Dyce Murphy collected all the Cup
peers and peeresses for her dance. The
Murphy horse, Andelosia, had followed up
its Guineas win by capturing the Linlithgow
Stakes; consequently, the household felt
blissful and in the right mood to tango.
They knew how to do it, too. For weeks
beforehand the socially saved had been prac-
tising the thing in small parties in the
Woorigoleen ballroom. The Barttelot pair
attended for Federal Government House,
and a good contingent of officers from the
Melbourne and Encounter rolled along in
their official fancy costume. The Governor
of the Falkland Islands and Mrs. Allerdyce
were specially trapped for the function,
which was attended by Millers, Curries,
McKinnons, Paynes and the other people
whose names are signboards of suburban
history. The usual dreams of gowns were
carefully extracted from heavy fur _ wraps,
for the November night was astonishingly
cold and big fires roared opulently in the re-
ception rooms. It takes more than a
modern evening gown to keep you from
pneumonia on a cold night. You dare not
take off furs until you are next a furnace or
ready for the violent exercise of the ball-
room.
The Dyce Murphys are packing up the
race winnings, and after collecting their
daughters will make tracks for England and
the Continent. I understand that Europe is
to be shaken-up till its teeth rattle.
The Norton Grimwades are coming in
quietly with their dance on November 26,
when the splendid race-time jigs and whirls
will be fading memories. By following at a
respectful distance you avoid the com-
parison that is odious. Still the Grimwade
revel will have a high polish.
Shovers generally were wild at the L. C.
Mackinnon garden frivol happening on the
Sabbath, which, of course, blue-pencilled the
weekly day of rest. The young of the Mac-
kinnon family will wait to see the closing
down of 1913 in Scotland before starting
home for Toorak. Australia meanwhile will
have to bear up as best it can.
The marriage of the Harry Ricketsons’
daughter Jean to Harry Ramsay, fixed for
December 4, will be a subdued affair, owipg
to the death of an uncle. The bride-elect is
fresh from an interval of foreign travel.
On October 14 fashionable St. George’s, in
Hanover Square, London, was engaged by
the elder Malcolm McEacharn daughter Eila
for an appointment with Mr. Connop Guth-
rie, of Valparaiso. The bride in brocade and
rose-point was handed for keeps to her bene-
dict by brother Neil McEacharn, and six
maidens saw it done. They wore tunic
frocks of white satin and champagne-tinted
chiffon, and carried baskets of lilies-of-the-
valley and white heather. At the Maddox
Galleries near by there were the requisite
eatables, diamond tiaras, breakfast cruets,
bread forks and cheques.
Mrs. Davidson, a Repertory Theatre en-
thusiast, turned her house inside out on
Saturday of last week, and indulged in pri-
vate theatricals for the financial good of the
Gregan McMahon enterprise. The show
consisted of three one-act plays in the big
billiard room and a rose-decorated supper.
Vere Donovan, daughter of the J. A.
Donovans, of St. Kilda. has enslaved a gold-
laced naval man, R. C. Negus, of our own
Navy. The wedding is programmed for
November 26, at the Carmelite Church.
Arthur Shirley, an actor who is at present
engaged at the Princess’s, is cast. for the
comedy of Matrimony. The leading lady
is Helens Newcomb-llall, a Maoriland girl
with a fine soprano voice.
Dr. Reginald Weigall, president of the
Automobile Club, is superintending the
club’s usual yearly treat to the orphanages.
The outing is listed for this Saturday,
November 22. Youngsters from a hundred
or feo institutions are to be taken for a spin
into the country, and there given a picnic
and presents. The doctor has sent out an
imploring appeal for the loan of another
fleet of cars. Though the trips of many
orphans are safely booked up, there are still
crowds of eager passengers for whom there
is no room. The perfect bliss these kiddies
get out of a mere motor-ride is worth watch-
ing, and anyone with an idle machine may
get some good fun by merely being charit-
able. Nurses and other kindly volunteers
are ready and willing to do the necessary
“looking after,” and the charges are scrubbed
and starched much more carefully than the
average tram passenger.
Apparently Mr. McCrae, of Dalgety’s,
found the elixir of youth during his trip to
England. He looks only a day or so older
than he did when he first propelled an office
pen.
Dr. O’Brien and family are back here in
search of sunshine, after doing seven years
in Maoriland. Daughter Irene has acquired
a voice, and, though it does not hurt her at
present, she is shortly taking it to London.
She will there be under the care and cocked-
hat of Uncle Long, who is a British general
and sleeps regularly in the War Office. Mrs.
Long is a beauty and a suffragette; the
cook is a red-haired Presbyterian ; and the
chauffeur has an uncle a printer, and is
learning German. That’s all the information
I have been able to get hold of so far.
Captain Hardy, an Australian army man
who used to be a Melbourne fixture, drifted
into town in time to see the Cup. He has
been in Bananaland and Westralia looking
after defence matters, and is now off to
Adelaide.
Mrs. “Rubber” Edwards bounced into
Melbourne on Monday, after an exciting
roll round the world. She rolled very care-
fully through America and Germany, and
haa a trip aloft in a Zeppelin. It didn’t ex-
plode that trip, otherwise Mrs. “Rubber”
would still be bouncing between the conti-
nent of Europe and the blue floor of Heaven,
hitting Europe once every 24 hours.
A Melbourne surgeon recently operated
on a well-known business man for appen-
dicitis. The patient reckoned that his
trouble was brought on by a long stay in
the witness-box. “I owe it to that con-
founded barrister,” he grumbled. “Don’t I
wish I had him here, carved and bandaged
like I am!” That night the surgeon
operated on another man for appendicitis.
The second case was laid tenderly in the
next bed. When the pair were well enough
to compare notes, the first patient dis-
covered that his curse had gone home to
roost —his neighbor was the barrister.
Rose Alba is the gentle alias of a little
lady who in the intervals of assisting a
parson-husband in Melbourne occasionally
raises a nice soprano in Charity’s aid. Some
mission movement induced the songstress to
reveal the dimensions of her gift on a recent
evening, and before the entertainment had
expired she had to take shelter from the
bouquets. In fact, she was such a success
that people are now advising her to take
her warble to a bigger market. Miss Alba,
who is beset with good looks, bears an em-
barrassing resemblance to Gaby Deslys, the
hussy who flirted with ex-King Manoel when
that juvenile’s kingdom was bursting.
The daughters of our Best and Attempted
Best families dearly love to patronise. At
Flemington recently, a beaming lass swooped
on a party of theatricals and gushingly in-
vited the star of the bunch to “come along
and be introduced to mai brother.” The
star gave her a brilliant smile of thanks
and went quietly. But, when she returned
alone, it was with quite a different expres-
sion. “Awful nuisance these people—-but
one must be polite to them !”
Ernest Goll, who specialises in Chopin
and hair, is to give another concert next
month. The Toorakians and others who
have to be seen where this sort of thing is
going on, are buying bunches of tickets and
wondering what clothes they will listen in.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The social feature of the week has been a succes-
sion of ■“opening” ceremonies-—mostly it is teas, not
seldom deaths. These social events come not in
single spies, but by the dozen, somehow. Thus on
Saturday Minister Pascoe opened the garden fete in
aid of the Home for Incurables. Rather a grim
object to be festive about, but the company managed
it all right. By the way, it is a pity that the name
of the place cannot be altered. An invalid is not
stimulated much in his last great fight with the
Universal Enemy by hearing the referee give the
decision against him before the battle has started, so
to speak.
Talking of garden parties, Mrs. Harry Bickford
gave one at Mount Lofty on this same Saturday.
The Bickfords’ garden is one of those that don’t
need a party to render it attractive. Amongst the
nourishment-seekers identified, in addition to a horde
of Bickfords, were Mrs. Pendleton, Mrs. James Hen-
derson, Mrs. Peter Wood and her daughter, Mrs.
A. J. Barker, Mrs. T. Duffield and Mrs. W. Hender-
son.
The new building for the Franklin-street Free
Kindergarten was opened by Lady Bosanquet on
Thursday. The proceedings were uneventful—in fact
one might almost say tame. What Thinker was it
who remarked that two things were inevitably dull
complete virtue and the opening of a kindergarten?
Miss Martha Bruggeinan gave a farewell recital at
the Town Hall before leaving us for Sydney and the
perils of matrimony. Miss Kathleen Holder, Miss
Dorothy Mcßride, Mr. Eugene Alderman, Mr
Alexander Cooper and Mr. Pearce abetted her.
The Michael Hawkers and their children left for
England by the Otranto. However, these national
tragedies have a way of balancing themselves. Our
howls of grief had hardly died down when they were
changed to sharp yelps of joy. The village look-out
had discerned the ship that contained Mrs. R. I).
Ryder and her two small children. The local
tenantry hadn’t time to turn out —in any case their
Union rules precluded any action of the sort, except, of
course, at overtime rates —as the home-comers left at
once for Angaston. They will stay at Lindsav
Park till the middle of January.
The S.P.C.A. got a horse-driver fined on Tuesday
for cruelty (loud cheers) ; and what was more, his
“passenger” also had to part up £1 for being an
accessory to the act. This is designed to render the
casual citizen chary about helping to overload
’buses. Also, it is calculated to make him forsake
cabs which drag wearily behind half-starved,
shoulder-galled and worn-out horses.
The exhibition of the Federal Society of Arts was
opened by the Governor at the Institute Rooms,
North Terrace, on Friday evening. Hans Hevsen’s
work was praised (the discernment of these Gover-
nors 1) ; also a portrait of Dorothea Spinney by Violet
Teague.

Billmarion Edwards, the Victorian girl
who roamed around in male attire and pre-
ferred man’s jobs to woman’s, has fallen on
evil days. A broken limb lately came
“Bill’s” way, and friends have been promot-
ing a picture-show night to help pay doc-
tor’s and nursing fees. She runs to fat
nowadays, and isn't the dapper young fellow
she was when she loomed large in the public
eye.
Even Jokook has been overcome by the
ridiculousness of his bluff that the Labor
opposition in the Representatives alone pre-
vents him going to the country. The other
night lie made this belated admission:—
All I have to say is that the Government will go
td the country when it thinks it ought to go, or, on
the other hand, when iny honorable friends opposite
acquire the ability to drive it there.
Truth is mighty and will prevail despite its
illtreatment by the Kookoos.
Another of the old holders of the Maori-
land Cross heard Bugler Gabriel call
last week. He was Color-Sergeant Richard
Shepherd, formerly of the 6Sth Foot, and he
earned the Cross for conspicuous bravery at
.Otauto on March 13, 1869, when our late
cannibal brother Titokowaru got what he
had been calling tenders for. Shepherd was
again specially mentioned for gallantry
against Te Kooti, when that choice savage
was putting up his last stand. The old
soldier was 75 when Time discharged him.
Last week a Sydney gentleman engaged in
the meat trade was fortunate enough to run
into an inspector who had both eyes open and
a nose that was in working order. Later on
the meat merchant appeared at court, and
the inspector described the condition of his
cart. The outside was coated with
mud, which had dried hard; the inside
was freely bespattered with crushed brains
and other decaying matter; and a boy who
was sitting beside the driver had his feet
reposing on a consignment of kidneys. In
reply the meat-merchant simplified the mat-
ter by pleading guilty, but urged in extenua-
tion that (1) he was having a new cart
built, and (2) a brace of inspectors had pre-
viously bailed him up the same morning and
neither had taken any exception to the condi-
tion of the cart. The fine was £2 with 6s.
costs, and there the matter ended. In a
civilised country something else would have
happened. First, the malefactor would have
had a black mark put on his license, and
two more marks would have barred him from
the foodstuffs business for ever; and then an
anxious search would have been made for
the other two alleged inspectors, with a view
to ascertaining if they had any qualms about
collecting their salaries.
“Hamer” : The Millions Club of Sydney,
with an effort to make itself a useful insti-
tution, has come forward with a proposal
for supplying Mary Ann to the missus who
is short of her and has £8 to spare. The
lady is to subscribe the £8 to pay. passage
money, and the Millions Club will then
secure and forward from Britain the genuine
domestic, hall-marked and warranted tame,
and the lucky recipient will proceed to get
back her money by weekly deductions from
Mary’s wages. At least, that is what the
Millions Club proposes. But even suppos-
ing that the “carefully selected domestics
don’t turn out on arrival to be something
altogether different, and the laws with regard
to the importation of contract labor consent
to turn a blind eye that way, and supposing,
further, that enterprising labor agents
don’t get in and secure the _ arriving
treasures for somebody who . isn’t the
consignee, Mrs. Rose Bay will still he
likely to meet all sorts of e
when she starts to make the weekly deduc-
tion, and Mary realises that there are plenty
of “places” available where there are no
weekly deductions. About the time tn
harassed secretary has got through explain-
ing to excited ladies that he isn’t responsible,
the Millions Club will be a discredited insti-
tution.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Billy Blue”: The Sydney daily press has
again been reviving the old tradition that the name
of the ancient suburb of Parramatta was its native
monniker, meaning “where eels sit down.” It makes
a pretty picture, and it is a wonder the papers have
not had an illustration of an old buck eel gracefully
reclining on a mangrove mudbank. But there is
no authority whatever in any of our records
for attaching such a meaning to the name. The real
thing will be found in a letter written by the wife
of John Macarthur on September 1, 1795, in which
she says: “By the date of this letter you will see
that we still reside on our farm at Parramatta, a
native name signifying the head of a river.” This
letter is printed in Vol. 11. of the “Historical Re-
cords of N. S. Wales.”
MORE LIKE A FRIEND.
The evidence indicates that for some years he
has been addicted to drink, and that he has not only
neglected at times to provide properly for the sup-
port of his wife and children, but in addition has
been guilty of acts of personal violence and cruelty
to his wife....But the petitioner could not say that
the respondent wilfully separated himself from her
against her will. Not only did the evidence indi-
cate that she gladly consented to his remaining away
from her, but it also established that she made it
plain to him that she would not live with him again.
— Judge’s summing-up in recent unsuccessful petition
for divorce by a deserted wife.
His Hon. : “For 14. years your husband has
been an habitual drunkard, and has con-
sistently beaten and left you unprovided
for. On such occasions you were no doubt
glad to see the last of him 'I ”
“ You bet I was ! Look at these marks ”
“ Then , under the circumstances , madam , what
you allege to be desertion was merely evi-
dence of his kindly heart and a loving
readiness to oblige and meet your every
wish. Petition dismissed.”
“Bushman”: My first experience of
Binghi’s tracking qualifications was back in the ’6o’s.
Two elderly blacks had been given the heel of a
barrel of beer that had gone off in a Vic. bush pub,
and had departed for their camp, carrying it in a
borrowed bucket. A couple of hours later my father
and I, searching for strayed horses, came across the
abos., sound asleep beneath a bushy-headed, low
tree, with the bucket, now only half full of sour ale,
between them. Dad lifted the brewery, and hung it
in the thick of the tree, and we moved on. We
circled through the bush, over a hill, across some
very scrubby creek country, and reached home from
the direction opposite to that from which we had
departed. About four o’clock that afternoon, two
blacks came out of the bush exactly where we had
emerged, and walked up to the house. They were
the shareholders in the brewery, and they had
tracked us relentlessly for some information con-
cerning it. When Dad gave them its address the
antics of those untutored brothers were something
to remember. The second experience was also in
outback Victoria, on an occasion when one Col-
stone, or Colston, had murdered an old
couple named Davis. The husband was killed by
the roadside, about a mile away from home, after
which the slayer made a detour, through the bush,
entered the house by the front way, killed Mrs.
Davis in bed, departed by the back way into the
bush, across Dom Dom Creek, and through a marsh
to the Black Spur Hotel. Two young binghis, not
long over from Queensland, were with the police,
and when they arrived on the scene they' rapidly
followed Colston’s tracks and described his move-
ments. The local police meanwhile had constructed
a clumsy theory that a neighbor had committed the
crime. Fortunately for this man he had a very
large foot, while the binghis insisted that the
man they tracked had a very small foot. Had they
admitted, as was suggested to them, that it might
be a large foot, the police would have arrested an
innocent man, and woven a web of strong circum-
stantial evidence around him. To the credit of
the trackers, they never faltered? They knew
their business.

“Ding Bat” : Get on to this! I dropped
in to see a cocky in Northern Maoriland the other
day, and saw milking machines in operation for the
first time. They seemed to be bailing out the god-
desses in great style, which made me the more sur-
prised to see one small boy hand-milking a cow. I
asked him why. “This is what we use for ourselves,”
he replied. “The toobs [tubes] stink.” One sniff
satisfied me that restraint in his language was that
boy’s strong point.
* * * *
“Gin”: “Leo Lear” says (B. 23/10/’l3)
that the bushman reads more than the city youth
or bloke. In this part of Monaro (N.S.W.) the
bushman does not read. He was mostly born here,
and lives his blameless life on the spot, without
aspirations and in deep content. He tails sheep,
smokes, eats, sleeps and yarns about “that there
steer o’ Stone’s,” “me ole roan mare,” rabbits,
sheep, lambs and snakes. Lately I met the excep-
tion. He was going fresh and strong on Bill Beach,
and spoke glowingly of Hanlan. Hardly a station
hand in my vicinity has ever heard of Henry Law-
son even. Of course, there are exceptions. But,
although our bushmen don’t read, they take keen
notice of things they see. An
example has just returned to us
after spending 10 days or so in
Sydney. He can describe every
inch of 11.M.A.5. Australia, knows
exactly how many wethers (“20,
be gob!”) could be comfortably
stalled in each gun, the size of
the shells and their carrying and
destructive powers. He knows
how the bluejackets feed, and can
describe the ladies waiting at
Man-o’-war Steps. He also trav-
ersed wool stores and machinery
and motor establishments, and has
a wealth of interesting details
concerning them. He visited the
mixed bathing beaches, was
edified with the behavior at
Manly, and horrified with the
misbehavior somewhere else. He
is eloquent on transparencies, and
describes the fashions faithfully.
So far, he has not reached the
opera or politics, but I expect
them along next week. What’s
the use of reading when you’ve
got a bottomless mine of infor-
mation like that to draw on?

“Camp” : Old Dan Ryan, a local big gun,
was judge. Intervals between events were long, the
day was hot, and annual re-unions between friends
were frequent and moist. By the time the Langi-
Yaloak Cup was run Dan had buried enough
whisky to go on with. There was a field of five
horses, and the finish was a ding-dong go between
a black mare and a grey gelding. Up the straight
they hurtled and passed the posts with the grey
about half a nose to the good. Josh Hill, the
clerk of the course, promptly convoyed the grey
up to the box. “That’s not the winner,” said Dan.
“Why don’t yez keep yer eyes open?” “The old
chap marked the black mare,” thought Josh. “Any-
how, it was pretty close.” So he straightway
brought along the black, and, in turn, the three
“also-rans,” each being rejected with rising in-
dignation by Dan. The crowd and the bookies by
this time were nearly barmy, while Josh’s temper
was badly frayed. “You old he-goat,” he yelled,
“that’s all the damned horses that ran.” “Y’ im-
pident blaggard!” roared Dan. “Would yez have
me disbelieve me own eyes ? I tell y’ ’twas a
piebald that won!”

“Fitz”: I’ve saved dozens of dogs suffering
from ticks. Treatment: Discover the parasite or
family, and introduce the kerosene bottle. Saturate
the curse well, and count it out. Tie the dog up
in a cool place or a tub of water, and, instead of
giving him food, entertain him with a packet of
Epsom. Add a few drops of liquid ammonia every
hour till there are signs that the sufferer is getting
back his punch or bite. If he is far gone give
him strychnine solution, which can be obtained
with directions at any pill shop. It will then be a
case of kill or cure.

“Condamine”: He was fresh from the
Old Dart, and seemed anxious to make an impres-
sion on his new boss. “First,” said the boss, “we’ll
knock up a hut for you.” When by their united
labors it had reached the stage where all that was
required was to nail on the boards, the farmer left
for the day and instructed his aide-de-camp to com-
plete the job. On returning home that night the
boss was told that the hut was finished, w'hereupon
he suggested that Algie should hump his nap over
and sleep there. A few minutes later the young rnan
returned to say that he couldn’t get in. Sure enough
he couldn’t. He’d made a complete box which could
be opened only with an axe.
