“Jenks” : Bill Wheatear came to the city
for the races, and one morning at his pub he had a
yarn with the barman, who told him a “good thing.”
The barman said if he was going out to the races
that afternoon to be sure and have a bit on Fidget
for the five-furlong flutter. Bill’s friend further con-
fided that the wiser course was to back Fidget “in
the running,” as the mare was very unreliable at
the barrier ; “but if she hops out, the race is all
over, bar collecting,” he concluded. “Just as well
I met that bloke,” William remarked to himself on
the course when the scratcliings were hoisted and
he saw that Fidget was a starter. Everybody else
was busy with his bets, and Bill mooched about
getting more restless every minute. “Wish they’d
’urry up and start the blasted race —Fidget is 10
to 1 now,” he said to himself. At last 1 Here they
come out on the track, Fidget looking well. Bill
chews at his card as the nags face the barrier.
They’re off! Fidget has “hopped out”! Bill
rushes up to the nearest bookie with a £1 note in
his moist hand. The registered man is watching
the race through his field glasses. “What’ll yer give
us Fidget?” Bill roars, but Solomon is interested
elsewhere. Off Bill rushes to the next. “What’ll
yer give us Fidget?” he bellows. “Take 5 to 1,”
said Abraham. “No good to me,” gasps Bill flying
to the next. “What’ll yer give us Fidget?” “Take
10 to 1,” answered Mossy. “Gawd blimey!” On
once more, perspiring and excited. “What’ll yer
give” Too late! No. 7 (Fidget’s number) is
hoisted on the board, and William came to life in
time to hear someone say that the horse had won
by a short head, but the time was rather slow.
Bill glared.. “Slow be damned!” he snarled; “/
never even had time to hack the blasted thing!”

INTERIOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I possess a magnificent interior.”
—The High Commissioner.
Wrote the missionary home:
“After crossing of the foam —
No voyage could be dismaller or drearier —
We found a lovely land,
With plenty smiling bland;
It owns a most magnificent interior —
We’re settling in a wonderful interior.”
Said the Chief of Wanki-poo:
“When the Gospel Ship is due,
Put on the pot and make the village cheerier;
Let the missionary see
We shall perfectly agree—
He’s coming to a wonderful interior,
We’ve got a most magnificent interior.”
Said the papers when they heard
Of a Something that occurred:
“The ways of Wanlci-poo-dom somewhat eerie are;
And our hapless brother —well,
Where he is we cannot tell;
We fear he’s lost in Wanki-poo’s interior;
He’s bushed in that magnificent interior.”
Rose a statesman at a spread,
With a portly grace and said:
“I know you folks of speechifying weary are;
But the fish and fruit and wine
Have aromas all divine
To him who has a competent interior,
And I’ve a most magnificent interior! ”
Said the worms who came to prey
On a politician’s clay:
“Now this is what we call a dish superior,
So round, and full and fat,
You couldn’t growl at that —
So large and rich, so very much interior,
A cut and carve and come-again interior!”
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER FLAM.

“The Vagabond” : T can put “Camp” right
as to the origin of the phrase “mad as a hatter”
(B. 23/10/’l3), which he says is Australian. The
first “mad hatter” came to earth with “Alice in
Wonderland.” When the book was published, the
English newspapers enjoyed it so much that every
bright sentence was quoted over and over, until some
passed into proverbs, and thus the “mad hatter” has
lived.
* » * »
“N.M.”: Talking about eccentric em-
ployers (“Silas Snell” was, a week or two ago), old
Maurice Weston, who owned the Shene estate, Cluny,
Morrison and other valuable property in the
Brighton and Bothwell districts, used to be our Tas-
manian champion. He was known far and wide as
“Blossom.” He called every horse “Blossom,” and
in the end got called by the same name. A kindly
old chap was Weston, and one who never liked to
turn away men looking for work, though at times
he was hard put to know what to give them to do.
“See that gate down there?” he said to one appli-
cant. “I’ve got a man swinging it. You can go
and sit on it while it swings.” The job ended in a
fight, and Weston, coming along in the thick of it,
sent for a trooper and had both locked up for the
night. Another time, when work was slack, he set
a man to dig a hole, so many feet by so many, and
of such and such a depth. The new hand set to work,
picked and shovelled, put his back into the work,
trimmed and patted the orifice into shape and went to
the house to report. “Yes,” said Maurice, inspecting,
“that’s what I wanted. Now fill it up again.” As
the old man aged and his eccentricities increased,
cadgers frequently omitted the formality of soiling
their hands, and turned up on pay-day claiming
wages for as long as seemed prudent. One bearded
agriculturist who had been sacked, had a clean
shave, came back and was re-engaged to fill the
vacancy left by “that scoundrel.” The boss often
congratulated himself on having got so good a man
in exchange. There was a fine spaciousness about
the Weston methods that appealed to the imagina-
tion and flung most of the properties into the hands
of the banks. “Blossom’s” order sent to a horse
sale is still remembered—“ All the horses you can
get.” The result was a miscellaneous collection of
120. Horses were up, too. The mob was driven up
to Shene, and the weeds that were not worth bother-
ing over were turned out in the back country. Some
died there; others were removed by casual persons
who felt they required a little riding exercise.
BOARDIN’ HER.
THE PILOT: “No wet togs these days!”
“Pick”: It was at Reevesdale station, near
Bungonia (N.S.W.), that the sweet-briar, a Gold
Country importation, was first planted in Australia.
Charles Reeves Styles, who got a grant of those
broad acres in the glorious days of assigned con-
victs, cheap labor and no strikes, brought three
cuttings with him from England, and carefully nursed
the only one that survived. He lived long enough lo
see the pick of his lands matted with the thorny
reminder of ’Ome, but not long enough to see his
successors and neighbors ruined in their endeavors
to reclaim the lands from the ever-spreading horror.
Nowadays the briar has planted itself far and wide,
and, like other imported curses, has mostly selected
the best lands. It can be stunted if you yoke a
team of bullocks to it in wet seasons, or use forest
devils on it, and afterwards feed down the young
shoots with sheep or goats; but I have never yet
met the bloke, fellow or cove who could wholly eradi-
cate the nark, and I badly want to hear of him.

“Celia Lloyd”: For the defendant in the
case against the black-tracker, I quote from a
diary kept by a cattleman in N. Queensland in 1870.
He tells us that on February 1 in that year, their
horses having been stolen by blacks, he and his
mate, with two black boys, all mounted, tracked the
animals for four days, for the greater part of the
time from the saddle and at a trot. On the morn-
ing of February 5, they found their lost nags feed-
ing by a creek. That afternoon, four miles from
where they found the horses, they picked up the
tracks of two blacks; and as each left foot was
minus the first joint of the middle toe, it appeared
that the thieves were women of
a district where the missus was
marked that way. The “boys” in-
sisted, however, that the tracks
were those of men, and pointed
out that wherever they showed
plainly the right track was heav-
ier than the left, and the latter
heavier at the heel end. It appears
that it was quite necessary to
shoot horse-thieves in those days,
so the chase was continued, over
country where, according to the
writer, it would have been hardly
possible for an ordinary man to
track a shod elephant; and a
brace of black men died from
lead-poisoning (or something to
that effect) on the morning of
February 8. The amputation of
the toe had taken place only a
few days previously, apparently
to deceive the trackers; and the foot had become
tender—hence the light tracks. Said the boys: “This
fellow savee too much—me savee more.”
» * * *
“Potbrook” : A word for that much-abused
bird, the crow. Although 1 have cursed him in and
out of season, and even dropped the “r” out of his
name, I must say he’s a game lot when there is
trouble amongst his own people. While I was riding
through a paddock recently, my dog was set on and
given a wild time by a party of crows for some
unknown reason. A few days later I was in the same
paddock, and again the birds got going on the dog,
who had been hunting around. I was curious, and
investigated. Near the trunk of a fallen tree I
found one of the flock with a broken wing. It was
a bad break, and there was no hope of a recovery.
I think he knew it, for he had a look on him like
Jokook has when the Majority is missing. How long
the gang had stuck to him, seeing him down the steep
places, I don’t know. He reached the steepest of
them that afternoon, for I sent him to the land
that is flowing with sheep’s eyes and tripe, not
because he looked like Jokook, but because I was
sorry for him, and it gave the others a chance to go
to the country.
“Masarcus”: I want to know the name of
the striped green and yellow snake which resides
along the Murray banks. It’s not in any directory
or tide manual that I know, although the beast is as
familiar ashore as afloat. I daresay I could have got
the name of the green part of him at the office of
the Protestant Watchman, and of the yellow at the
Cartiiolic Press, but that’s no good. I want the
simultaneous name, as it were.
“Bloore”: To the weighty tomes of Cow
literature I throw in the worst case I know of. The
Simon Legree to whom I refer has 60 cows, all
milked by four State boys, one of whom is 10 years
of age. Three times has one lad tried to get away,
but his luck seems to be right out and his dream of
stowing away on some ship at Newcastle has yet to
be realised. Between milkings the lads do a little
corn husking, and corn husking is about as pleasant
as husking oakum if you’ve got a pile to do.
“Eddyson” : Inge was sitting at his hat
floor, scraping parsnips, when Ridgeway passed
“Hello, Inge!” cried Ridgeway. “So yer goin’ t’
marry Seward’s widder, after all? Well, it’s the
best thing you can do!” Ridgeway continued big
mild career, and Inge sat, with a parsnip i n his
nerveless hand, and his mouth wide open, for nearly
10 minutes. Then he gulped and resumed the slow
rasping of his parsnips. “Goo’ day, Ben! Congratu-
lations!” It was Leggo. lie had pulled up at the
panels. “Congratulations—what for?” asked Inge
dully. “On yer approacliin’ marriage, o’ course
Milly Seward’ll make yeh a good wife all right”
Leggo rode on, leaving Inge inactive again, his mouth
open, his intellect groping towards a dull apprehen-
sion. Inge was still scraping parsnips when Miss Nichol-
son hailed him. “Good day, Mr. Inge, I’m so glad ”
Inge forgot his parsnips again. Knife in one hand
vegetable in the other, and mouth ajar, he gazed at
Miss Nicholson. “You ought to have been married
long ago, you know. If I were Mrs. Seward i
wouldn’t have been as patient, I can tell you,” said
Miss Nicholson. Inge’s eyes followed Miss Nicholson
till she passed over the hill; then he swallowed hard
and resumed his task, slowly scraping a parsnip
Within half an hour Hughes, Withington, Morton
Mrs. Rivers and Myles, the rabbit inspector, passed
and each one offered Inge neighborly congratulations
and each greeting threw Inge into the same state of
nervous inaction. Never had there been such
a trend of population past Inge’s hut. He was still
scraping parsnips, when a timid eye fell upon a
tall female figure coming down from Cole’s. For
10 seconds Inge gaped; then he fell over himself
into the hut, went out by the back door on all-
fours, and gained the timber in safety. The tall
figure was that of Mrs. Seward. So it happened that
Inge did not have parsnips for tea that evening.
During a trip to the railway at Flyflat next day!
Inge was congratulated 19 times going and coming!
He went like a man in a trance; he returned like
one possessed of devils—silent, tormenting devils.
While Inge was harvesting his two acres of spuds
that afternoon, Egan chanced along. Egan took
Inge’s hand, and wrung it silently but with great
warmth. He seemed overcome with emotion. “I
wanted her meself, Bennie,” he said; “I did that.
But if I can’t have her, ’tisn’t fer me t’ begrudge
him what can. Take her, me boy, an’ Godblesher.”
Egan wiped his eye, and passed on, leaving Inge
leaning on his hoe. Then came Bishop, and Carter,
and Mrs. Carter, and Bill Bracken. In two hours
Inge had turned up 14 spuds. He had just recovered
from the shock of Bracken’s congratulations when
he beheld the Rev. Josiah Nott coming at him from
Gray’s orchard. Inge adopted no subterfuge; he
dropped his hoe and ran. He ran a mile, and
arrived at the office of Hobbs, saw-miller, Flyflat, in a
breathless condition. “You wanter buy me bit iv
land along the crick?” he said. Hobbs admitted it.
“Seven quid an acre, you said?” “I’ll make it
seven,” said Hobbs. “I’ll take it!” Inge almost
screamed the words. “Gimme me deposit, an’ fix
th’ sale up ez quick’s possible. I’m clearin’ out.”
Inge left Flyflat next day. Hobbs and Leggo saw
him off. “It does seem a bit tough,” said Hobbs,
“but he barred my tramway, and I simply had to
have that timber paddock.”
* * * *
“F.H.R.”: Doolan was on his way up the
“crilc” to get a sheep from Casey’s; and mentioned
the fact to Ryan. As soon as he was gone a con-
sultation was held between the boys and old man
Ryan. When Doolan came abreast of Ryan’s on the
return journey, he. noticed a great commotion. Tuck-
ing the cloth round the carcase of his fat wether, he
ran in to see what might be the matter. “For the
love of Heaven, Doolan,” cried Mrs. Ryan, “you’re
just in time to see the last of him.” Ryan reposed
on the bed. His face was flushed and his great, hairy
chest was exposed. He took Doolan’s hand in what
seemed the death-grip and held on. “Don’t lave me,
Doolan.” he whispered in an unearthly voice. “Don’t
lave me—’tis me harrt; ’tis me harrt.” After a
quarter of an hour or so, Doolan tore himself away —
to find his mutton gone. They say that if Doolan
could have recovered his sheep as quickly as Ryan
recovered his health all would have been well.

WHY HE WENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cup race itself is, in essence, nought but an
excuse for making public national holiday. Most of
us will attend the event to indulge our native pre-
dilections for gregarious intercourse.
— Cup-day leader in Melb. Age.
He had a pair of glasses on, he looked quite spick
and span ;
I thought he was a typical Australian racing man ;
But, when I asked him for a tip, he didn’t know a
horse:
“My secret I’ll divulge.
I’m going to indulge
My native predilection for gregarious intercourse.”
He went away to do it, and I haven’t seen him since.
I hope he put his all upon that most unlucky
Prince —
A human jay who talked like that deserved to make
a loss.
Confound the windy clown 1
I hope it got him down—
Him and his “predilection for gregarious inter-
course 1 ”
N.S.W.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
PILLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Are pills “food” ? —-Legal point recently debated
at great expense.
There is a shelf at home
Whereon my bottles stand ;
Old “mixtures” darkly foam
And pills lie close at hand.
These balls in bottles pent
Now catalogue for me
Past ills that came and went —■
Some aching history.
I dare not throw one out,
In case I ache again ;
So I 'ake care about
My diary of pain.
Sometimes when in good key
A pill or two I cram,
If I think I might be
Much better than I am.
The “mixtures” effervesce ;
But all my being thrills,
Whenever I progress
Around my stock of pills.
Pills lull me now to sleep;
For still more pills I pray—
The bullets that I keep
For holding Death at bay.
Along Life’s stony track,
And over Time’s blue hills,
1 11 pelt the Reaper back
With pecks of purple pills.
In cool, judicial mood,
I’ll go to this great length:
Pills are my daily food,
My refuge and my strength 1
N.S.W.

preparation are no less manifold than
amazing.”
So writes a well-known physician in
the Medical Press and Circular one
of sixteen thousand doctors who have
testified to the value of Sanatogen in
various kinds of weakness, loss of nerve-
power, impaired digestion, brain-fag,
sleeplessness, and general weakness.
Sanatogen, the tonic food with lasting
effects, has given a new lease of health
to more than a million sufferers, in-
cluding many of the most distinguished
people in the laud.
Nerve Power Restored.
The reason for Sauatogen’s great

“ Sanatogen is an excellent nerve food,
containing a large amount of organic

it can be easily absorbed.”
But Sanatogen does more than revivify
the nervous system. It also builds up
the muscular tissues and has a beneficial
effect upon every organ of the body.

“T. 0.1.”: It is said you can tell an Eng-
lishman by his constant longing to kill
something. Some Australians are as bad, or
worse. Just now their “something” is a
grove of historic trees on Wardry station,
22 miles from Condobolin (N*S.W.), where
explorer John Oxley camped nearly a cen-
tury ago—to be exact, 1817-19. There he
observed two peculiarly-marked pine trees
adjacent to the grave of an aboriginal chief.
These trees were recently discovered by a
party headed by traffic-superintendent Milne,
of Orange. A number of Condobolin resi-
dents went out the other day to view the
spot and have a chat upon native habits.
It was then stated that the tree with the
native marks, and in a state of perfect
preservation, would be cut down and for-
warded to the Sydney Museum; but some
local people, who don’t see why everything
should be sent to Sydney, want a small
reserve proclaimed to include both the trees
and the grave. Let’s hope the iconoclasts
won’t win this time.
Sydney Government House is a good ad-
vertisement of the patriotism of the men
who were in high office when the place was
built. Governor Bourke led the way and
spoke up for Australian materials whenever
he got the chance. So the cedar in itaircase
and other parts is Australian; the
marble came from the few N.S.W. quarries
then opened, and the stone from handy places
in the Domain and Botanic Gardens. Nearly
everything, in fact, was Australian except
the architectural plan; that was supplied by
an English architect named Blore and pre-
sented by the British Government. More-
over, after a good deal of discussion, the
middleman contractor was cut out, and, by
Bourke’s direct orders, Mortimer Lewis, the
Colonial Architect, was given a free hand
to employ masons, carpenters and others at
current rates. Altogether, the building is a
monument of the best ideals of 1837, when
the first £lO,OOO was placed on the Estimates
towards the cost of the work.
“The Puritan has always been the expert
politician. He has so little in his own life
that is amusing that he is forced to meddle
with the affairs of other people.” —Sidney
Dark, in his new novel, “The Man Who
Would Not Be King.”
‘Teace at Any Price”: Will someone in
Sydney (the Gaud Mayor, for preference)
start movement to suppress unnecessary
street, noises? Such modern tortures as the
snarling motor-lorry cannot be suppressed;
but the barrel-organ can be banished from
the inner city, and the speeding motor-hog’s
dying-pig scream can be taken from him and
some sort of milder alarm substituted. Every
civilised city should have a Censor of Noises,
and he should not allow one more yell or
thump than is absolutely necessary. As a
rule, a loud and discordant noise, however
produced, is an indication that there is an
incompetent about. The competent man or
woman contrives to work with a minimum
of clatter ; it is only the loud and slapdash
fool who bangs the broom against the skirt-
ing board, dumps the valuable packing-case
violently on one corner, drives headlong over
a rough patch of road, or howls when a con-
versational tone would be sufficient. A little
judicious regulating would reduce the street
noises of Sydney 25 per cent., and a set of
formidable by-laws would probably get it
down a good deal further.
From another member of the shark-toothed
profession:—
If the time is mature for the law to compel the
filing of wills before death (B. 18/9/’l3), it is
equally mature for looking into a dozen other mat-
ters relating to wills. The existing Common Law
and Statute Law leave much to be desired in the
way of suppressing very many brands of fraud. Con-
sider, for instance, the position of the attesting wit-
nesses. Under the law there is much complicated
detail concerning how, where, and in whose presence
the witnesses are to sign; but absolutely no restric-
tion is put on the qualifications for an adult witness,
beyond that he or she must not be dead drunk, or
imbecile, or possessed of an interest under the will.
A witness may be a notorious blackguard or an
ignorant clod, but those little matters don’t cut any
ice at all. Testamentary dispositions are supposed
to be as important as most other documents, and yet
their attestation (which, be it remembered, goes to
the very essence of their validity) can be performed
by almost any two individuals who happen to be
hanging about or lying down at the time. In striking
contrast is the system prevailing under the Transfer
of Land Acts, which provide that only a person who
has the qualifications therein specified can witness any
document coming within the scope of those Acts,
however trivial the affair to which such document re-
lates. This special statutory witness has in practice
scarcely ever been found to stray from the paths of
virtue in this respect. Why not bring wills into
line? Surely one person who is commonly presumed
to possess a fair amount of commercial morality and
good sense is more fit to perform such a task than
two possible fools or two possible rogues. Indispu-
tably the present law allows of an enormous amount
of fraud, and undue influence of almost uncountable
kinds. Moreover, it is generally admitted by the
profession and others in a position to know that the
corruption or Arcadian simplicity of one or both of
the attesting witnesses is directly or indirectly re-
sponsible for about 25 per cent, of the cases relating
to wills which come before the Courts. The innova-
tion would, of course, do awuy with many “deathbed
wills,” because a special witness would not always
be handy. Consequently, for a short time it would
give rise to a few extra cases of intestacy; but even
that result would be far preferable to the present
method, by which the designing would-be beneficiary
may take an unfair advantage of the mental inertia
of the moribund testator and the easily gulled or
easily bribed attesting witness.

policy comes from unexpected quarters. At
Hongkong there is the
established by the Government, and used
chiefly, if not exclusively, by Chinese. Lt
publishes in English and Chinese a very pre-
sentable monthly magazine, the bellow

The Bulletin “Den’s” long Son or a

possessions. “We draw attention, says the
Yellow Dragon, “to the powerful lines,
‘Son of a Fool,’ taken from the Sydney
Bulletin —lines that surely can only be
addressed to the small _ and uninfluential
minority of those who object to the working
of the military system of home defence m
the Commonwealth of Australia.” Ihe idea
of not preparing to defend your home evi-
dently strikes the Yellow Dragon as too
ridiculous to appeal to anybody but a few
cranks who don’t count.
“Celia Lloyd”: Thousands of Japs look
upon Australia as the Promised Land, and
it would probably thrill us if we knew how
many of them are getting in early. They
manage it in all sorts of ways, and this is
one. There is a large memorial stone at
Broome (Nor’-West Australia) bearing a
long list of the names of Japs lost in a
willy-willy some five years ago. Now, I
knew many of those Japs, and only lately
I’ll swear I have seen two of them in N. S.
Wales. It is pretty well known up in that
smudged corner that Japs supposed to have
been lost had run their luggers up on the
beach, after making as much of a wreck of
them as possible, and worked their way down
the coast to scatter through Australia.
The supposed loss of the vessels cleared the
owner of all responsibility, the crews were
written off as drowned, and everybody w T as
satisfied, including the energetic pioneers
from Nippon who were not dead but cheat-
ing.
The shipping cos. can’t, with their present
equipment, keep pace with the rush from
this Labor-strangled country, so they go on
building bigger and bigger ships, and chasing
more and more of them out to load up with
escaping Capital. The 18,500 ton Ceramic
has hardly got her pick down on Austra-
lian mud before someone signals that a
20,000-tonner is being built for the Aus-
tralian trade —we mean for fleeing Austra-
lian Capital. The Suez Canal is evidently
blocked with ships loaded dowm to Plimsoll
with Capital, so the Cape route has been
putting on fine fleets to relieve the conges-
tion. Now the Clan Line has decided to
build further tonnage. The owners say this
is for the Australian trade, but, of course,
there is no Australian trade. Labor legis-
lation killed it, and the Sosh tiger ate the
corpse. The Clan Line is really building to
provide Capital with more deep-sea escapes.
The new 15,000-ton Orient liner, of which
the plans have just been approved, is also
to swim out on the same mission of rescue.
It almost looks as if Australian trade —
But, rubbish, there is no Australian trade!
“Billy T.”: They take it all ways. He
was standing near me at the start of the
1913 Cup, a tallish man, thin, grey-faced,
possibly 45, in a brave but battered bell-
topper, and trousers frayed slightly at the
heels. My attention was attracted to him
by his eloquence. He was talking to him-
self audibly and fluently, while seeming to be
doing his utmost to eat a large, white, cotton
handkerchief. I heard: “If he should! If
he should! My God, if he should! They’re
off! No, not yet, not yet! Now they arc.
No. They’re off! Oh, my God!” Biting
wildly at his pocket handkerchief till it was
torn to shreds, he followed the race, his
large, blue, watery eyes seeming unpleasantly
prominent, and every stride of the horses
drew an ejaculation. “Where is he? My
God, I can’t see him! If he hasn’t started!
He’s left at the post.” Here followed a
sweep of blasphemy which for sheer inten-
sity, for cold, concentrated vehemence, I have
never heard equalled. Then he thought he
had found the colors again, and thanked God
with the same ardor. As they were
coming home down the straight, I saw the
sweat flowing in streams from under the
man’s hat. It was bitterly cold there in the
wind, but four rivuftts of perspiration were
trickling down my neighbor’s taut, grey
face. “I can’t see,” he was saying. “My
God, I can’t see! Yes. there he is! God
in Heaven, that’s he!” Then he simply ejacu-
lated, “My God! my God!” with a rising
inflection and many changes of feeling, end-
ing in a shrill scream as the race finished.
All being over, he broke down and cried—
collapsed on the sward, weeping noisily. Two
men helped him up, but he crumpled in their
hands in a faint. “Poor devil! Lost every
penny, I suppose,” was the most frequent
comment in the sympathetic crowd. I
thought that a reasonable explanation, too;
but as a matter of fact the man had Posin-
atus in a sweep, and had won something
over £6OOO.
There have been some good stayers on
Maoriland’s Supreme Court Bench. Joshua
Williams, who has just been appointed to the
judicial committee of the Privy Council, has
38 years’ service to his credit. C. W. Rich-
mond died in his 33-year-old harness; ditto,
A. J. Johnston, 30 years; Prendergast re-
signed after 24 years as Chief; and several
others sat on for 16. 17 and 18 years. The
shortest stay on the Bench was that of “Pat”
Buckley. Appointed in December, 1895, by
his political chief. Richard John Seddon, he
died five months later.
Following on the big increase in the cost
of running the N. S. Wales railways comes
a chorus from the morning press, inferring
that the wage increases are ruinous and are
imposing an intolerable burden on the Back-
bone. The poidl is carefully overlooked that
the increases which are causing such alleged
trouble have been made by independent
boards, after careful investigation, and in
those boards’ opinion represent only a fair
and reasonable thing. And in most cases
those making the investigations—Justice
lleydon, for instance —could scarcely _be
credited with an unduly wild and luxurious
notion of what are reasonable wages for a
worker. It would be more profitable, before
yelling wildly at increased wages and con-
sequent increased freights, to howl at a few
other factors which are making the Ma
State railways frantically expensive. _ For
instance, the increase in wages is a mighty
small circumstance compared with the ex-
pense involved day after day in dragging
produce unnecessary miles merely in order
to let Sydney have a cut at it.
HOT STUFF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A striking: frock the color of Dijon mustard com-
bined with the darker shade of Bordeaux mustard
aroused much attention. —Cup fashion note.
The subtle, condimental tints
in Clementina’s Cup confection,
Accentuate, with saucy hints,
The creaminess of her complexion.
She wears a vinegary gown,
A belt of Blank and Jorkins’ leather,
Her hat, a vivid cayenne brown,
With mushroom-ketchup-colored feather.
Her .gloves, of Bordeaux mayonnaise,
A mustard stole her curves caressing
With vegetable greens and greys,
The latest thing in salad dressing.
Her coat, of Bombay curry shade,
Is draped with desiccated suet,
And in her laces are displayed
The varied colors of the cruet.
Her dainty shoes of gravy hue,
Her hose, the jaded fancy tickles,
The shade is tender Irish stew,
Relieved with kraut and Indian pickles.
N.S.W.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

Why docs the
Square Tread
possess this shape ?
A long series of practical tests hare demonstrated that only a cover of
this particular shape can give the best results as regards wear.
The tread being tightly compressed to begin with, is still further com-
pressed when the cover is inflated, and therefore offers resistance to cuts.
When, however, a cut does occur—which is sometimes inevitable —it
can be effectually repaired with Michelin Mastic.
N.B.—Remember, it is essential not only to know the best shape of cover, but also to knew
how to preserve the tyre when it is on your car. The secret is to keep it always well inflated, and
frequently verify the pressure with a Michelin Pressure Tester.
Michelin Tyres and Accessories
can be obtained from all the Leading Garages.

HOW TO NEUTRALISE DANGEROUS
STOMACH ACIDS.
Few people besides physicians realise the
importance of keeping the food contents of
the stomach free from acid fermentation.
Healthy normal digestion cannot take place
while the delicate lining of the stomach is
being inflamed and distended by acid and
wind —the results of fermenting food in the
stomach. To secure perfect digestion, fer-
mentation must be stopped or prevented, and
the acid neutralised. For this purpose phy-
sicians usually recommend getting a little
bisurated magnesia from the chemist and
taking half a teaspoonful in a little hot or
cold water immediately after eating. They
recommend bisurated magnesia because it is
pleasant to take, has no disagreeable after
effects, and instantly stops fermentation,
neutralises the acid and makes the sour, acid
food bland, sweet and easily digested.
The regular use of bisurated magnesia—
be sure you get the bisurated, as other kinds
of magnesia are of little value —is an abso-
lute guarantee of healthy, normal digestion,
for it overcomes and prevents that acid con-
dition which alone is the cause of trouble.*

Premier Peake (S.A.) has promised to
favorably consider the proposal to give an
extra salary of £lOO a year or so to the
Leader of the Opposition—another attempt
to prop up the rickety party-government
system.
Three members have been returned to the
N.S.W. Assembly without a fight: Treasurer
Cann, Solemn Lee, and Cohen of Petersham.
Cann and Lee are not opposed, as a matter
of habit; and the idea of doing anything
without Cohen is of course unthinkable to
the Holman Government.
Sample of party politics from the Tory
S.A. Register :
Mr. Andrew Fisher was a working miner before
he entered Parliament Mr. Joseph Cook was also
connected with the mining industry.
Still, real toil being the most respectable
thing in the world, Fisher has nothing to
complain of in the varied description.
Melbourne church congresses have of
late been pouring much melted butter over
Chief Policeman Sainsbury, a raid or two on
“betting shops” having called forth the drip-
ping. What one-eyed people these well-
meaning saints are ! They go delirious over
the “suppression” of one Ikey Moses, and
yet fight like the Devil against the Tote —
which would suppress a hundred Ikeys.
Wood, M.L.A., who is battling for the St.
George (N.S.W.) seat, put it this way to
Sydney Sun last Friday, after drawing the
salary that had been accruing to his credit
since the increase of payment to members :
I have determined not to use any portion of it
[the money] for my own private or personal benefit.
I propose to distribute the whole amount among
certain public charities. Naturally, should / have
the good fortune to represent St. George, the institu-
tions in that district will of necessity have first claim.
Some people would call it political bribery
and corruption, but such a thing is as far
from The Bulletin’s mind as it was from
Mr. Wood’s when he published the informa-
tion.
The right to occupy the Corio cushion in
the House of Yelps is presently to be con-
tested once more in the Labor interest by
A. T. Ozanne, who was M.11.R. for the three
years preceding last election. A ballot was
held the other day, and the figures were
extraordinary. Three candidates nominated
—Ozanne, sometime bookkeeper in the em-
ploy of the Board of Shirks, sacked when he
got the previous nomination, and afterwards
an orchardist; Joe Lyons, a State-school
teacher; and G. J. Mead, a teacher at Wes-
ley College. Ozanne polled well over 2000
votes, while Lyons got no more than four,
and Mead (who is president of the Political
Labor Council of Yic.) but two.
Joe Cook’s large capacity for making blun-
ders has got him into trouble in S. Aus.
That State has two (or more) Newland
families. One Newland, Simpson, belongs
to the woolly aristocracy ; the other was a
sleeping-car conductor before he got into
politics on the Labor side, a while ago. So
when a S. Australian Newland made hostile
remarks about the Government’s administra-
tion of the Northern Territory, Cook jumped
to the conclusion that, of course, it was the
Laborite, and snarled at him, as usual. Now
the Liberal wire-pullers are having a very
busy time smoothing the ruffled fleece of the
woolly Newland who actually made the re-
marks, and telling him and his friends that
Cook is really a much pleasanter man than
you would think.
What sort of a Speaker Johnson is—
Johnson is the gentleman who sat on a log
and woke up to find it an alligator—may be
judged by his treatment of Thomas during
the McGrath rumpus. This member, as
ladylike a one as there is on the Labor side
of the Reps., said: “We are agreed over here
that the Speaker has lost our confidence.”
For this “distinct reflection on the Chair”
Johnson called on him to “withdraw the
insult” ; and Thomas, having in effect told
Johnson that if he would stop cackling he
(Thomas) would apologise, was “named”
and passed out. But where was the “in-
sult” in the first place? Thomas is so firmly
satisfied Johnson has lost confidence that
he had already tabled a motion to that
effect, and this motion had been accepted,
and if it isn’t an insult to the Chair to move
that the Chair has lost confidence, how can
it be an insult to say the same thing?
“The Old Soldier” : According to a recent
Melbourne message :
Representations having been made to the Federal
Government that the erection of stables near Cen-
tennial Park and Victoria Barracks would tend to
depreciate the value of neighboring residences, the
Minister has ordered that no further steps be taken
until a thorough investigation has been made into
the expediency of building the stables somewhere
else.
What is there to investigate? The “neigh-
boring residences” are newly built on allot-
ments bought with the full knowledge that the
barracks were there, and that the site included
a great deal of vacant land, on which new
military buildings on a pretty large scale
might De put up at any time to accommo-
date the daily increasing military forces of
the Federation. But the local residents are
electors who vote in the constituency which
is represented by Willy Kelly. And in order,
apparently, that Kelly may not lose sup-
port, there is a likelihood of a useful de-
fence proposal being turned down. It is
the sort of thing that makes the unbiassed
onlooker very tired of the Party system.
The recent rioting in Wellington (M.L.),
which culminated in a more or less pitched
battle between strikers and mounted con-
stables, is likely to produce unexpected
effects. The special constables were mostly
cocky-farmers. At their first charge they
were repulsed by a shower of blue metal and
boards thrown between the horses’ legs.
Then, the supply of blue metal having given
out, the specials charged again. That sec-
ond charge has produced the revolution. The
most bitter anti-militarist who was knocked
endways in that rush of kicking horses and
swearing men realised that, with just a
little organisation, a little knowledge of how
to fight, the charge could have been turned
into a police rout. Hitherto the “Red
Federation” has been bitterly opposed to
compulsory military training. To-day it
realises that when every man is a trained
soldier, and the strike leaders are men who
understand the handling of soldiers, a mob
of mounted cocky-farmers will think twice
before they charge.

A fortnight ago President Yuan Shai Ivai
of China calmly dissolved the Opposition and
declared several hundred seats vacant. Last
week Joe Cook of Australia took a leaf out
of the old Chinaman’s book. He was anxious
to have a majority of one; and, by the cast-
ing vote of the Speaker, McGrath of Ballarat
was heaved out of the House for the
rest of the session for daring to “re-
flect” on the Speaker. So, unless a couple
of Liberals fall sick, Cook is safe to reach
recess without being forced to go to the
country he pretends he is struggling to face.
An Adelaide case shows the need of a
liberal and uniform divorce law. A husband
became insane in 1893, leaving his wife and
six young children unprovided for. The wife
had no redress whatever—probably wanted
none so long as she had any hope. At any
rate, she battled along till 1904, and then,
the husband’s case looking po better, she.
married again, and took the children to a
new home. By S.A. law, the first husband
is the aggrieved party; and, being at last
released from the asylum, .he has had no
difficulty in getting the divorce which was
all along denied to the wife. The Judge had to
rule that the wedding in 1904 was illegal,
but went out of his way to remark that it
was quite moral. Long confinement as a
lunatic is an accepted ground for divorce in
N.S.W., and why shouldn’t it be all
over Australia? In particular, why
should the children of that second marriage
be branded as illegitimate? Certainly they
can be promptly legitimatised by a re-
marriage of their parents, this being one of
the points on which South Australia is pro-
gressive.
Willy Watt has done the right thing in
bringing forward a bill to deal with steam-
ship cos. which dodge responsibility in re-
gard to injuries to passengers by covering
their tickets with 1379 “conditions,” setting
forth that the co. is not responsible for in-
juries to a passenger caused by the collapse
of the main deck, or the flooding of the
saloon bar, or the clumsiness, stupidity or
incompetence of the co.’s servants, the gene-
ral rottenness of the co.’s property,
any act of the Almighty’s (even though such
act is plainly a manifestation of Divine dis-
approval of the co.’s methods), or from dis-
comfort occasioned by the quality of the
company’s whisky, or the style of hairdress-
ing of its officers, servants or bosses, or
attacks by the company’s elephants, spots
before the eyes, or any other old thing which
may not happen to he mentioned, specified
or darkly adumbrated in the conditions here-
with set forth. The bill, to which refer-
ence was made before the foregoing sentence
got loose and bolted, purposes to put ship-
pins' cos. on the same footing as the railway
department. It sets forth that any contract
by which the co. is relieved from liability for
loss or damage caused by any brand of
negligence shall be illegal, and a penalty of
£lOO is mentioned in connection with any
attempt on the part of a co. to dodge it’s
natural obligations in the old way.

Since taking over the job of Attorney-
General in Westralia, two years ago, Tommy
Walker, formerly of N. S. Wales, has released
74 prisoners serving long sentences"
Probably Tommy knows what a saint
of 50 the sinner of 30 may be; all the
same, there is something wrong with a
system under which the community
insists on the public trial of a man
and then permits the results of that
trial to be secretly reviewed and upset
by a party politician whom it wouldn’t
perhaps trust with even a police magis-
trate’s job.
“William Street’s” note of concern
The poll for the N.S.W. State elections has been
fixed for a Saturday, and Dan Levy is worrving about
it. His Darlinghurst seat is being contested by
Frank Foster, who was formerly Labor M.P. for
Armidale. The Darlinghurst constituency is divided
into two parts. On the top of the hill is Darling-
hurst proper. Levy’s stronghold ; down the hill is the
top end of Woolloomooloo, where the Labor vote pre-
dominates. Potts Point is in Darlinghurst, and is
known as the New Jerusalem, owing to the fact that
Moses and Rebecca there do congregate—to the
number, it .is said, of. about 2000. And as
Saturday is-the Jewish Sabbath, to make Daniel safe
the Jewish community will have to break the Mosaic
law. And Dan is not too sure that they will, particu-
larly as there is nothing tremendous to be gained by
the fracture.
The Holman Government (N.S.W.) is
still busy chasing over the country in motor-
cars and promising railways at the rate of
about a million a day. If Ministers are
sane, they know quite well that nine-tenths
of the promises are futile; but as long as
the hook will land the fish on December 6, it
is all right. On June 30, the date of the
Public Works annual report, 1233 miles of
new railway had been authorised. Of that
lot lines totalling 553 miles had not been so
much as started ; and the sections covering
the other 680 miles were mostly just dawd-
ling along. On the Forbes-Stockinbingal
line, for instance, the work had been played
with for 18 months ; and at the same rate of
going the job wouldn’t be finished for
years. Yet, on top of all that, the Govern-
ment fired up a score more or less of railway
bills last session, and is baiting the country
voter with dozens of other promised lines;
and meantime there is a hollow sound in the
Treasury. Apart from the demoralisation of
the country through this form of political
bribery, the people suffer because, so long
as works are not completed, they bring in
no revenue, and the policy of dawdling over
a host of works means a shameful waste of
interest- —which, of course, the public has to
make good by taxation.

OUR GREATEST FOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Argus, anxiously discussing the idea of its
good friend John Madden being, made Governor of
the State, points out some dreadful possibilities.
“The advocacy of the appointment of colonial Gover-
nors has behind it the same shoddy, demagogic crowd
as urged the hauling down of the Union Jack in the
’6o’s. It is this fact that makes the movement sus-
pect and significant. It is a cut-the-painter policy
in instalments.”
We see him walking in the street,
The Governor by Bull provided.
His linen’s fine, his boots are neat,
His taste in hats is quite decided.
But who would think to see him thus,
Or hear him talking platitudes
When lauding Jones, or laying stones,
That he a bulwark is of thrones—-
That on such harmless elder dudes
The Empire’s unity depends ?
But certainly it does, dear friends.
With cocked hat on his head he goes
To join in banquets and harangues.
He’s melancholy, touched with “Joes,”
Or torn by fierce dyspeptic pangs;
But bravely there he airs his jokes,
Nor drops a hint of fate’s decrees —
A simple wight whose wondrous might
Saves empires dying in the night;
A man of potent mysteries!
The Argus says that this is so,
And Argus editors must know.
If we agree to part with him,
And set up in his lofty place
An image of our making grim,
Our fate is fixed, and short our grace.
John Madden’s an imposing man,
A marvel, too, at talking guff;
But he won’t do. Australians who
Have got the truer, keener view
Insist upon imported stuff.
Our sons are knaves and fools ; we pray
“God save us from ourselves for aye ! ”
VICTORIA.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
“Snaffle” : In support of the Totalisator, a
calculation has been made as to how the books came
out over Posinatus. There were 233 horses entered,
dwindling down to 20. Betting started before the
acceptances were in; Ikey’s harvest began with the
weights. Duke Foote was backed heavily as low as
4 to 1. He was scratched. Ike received at least
£5O on scratched horses for every £lOO he would,
under any circumstances, have finally to pay out.
Take a £5OO book laid all round the field at market
odds. Deduct the £33 staked on Posinatus, and
the £5OO paid out over him, and it will be found
that the bookie got £944 for his £5OO. The profit
of a man with a £5OOO book would be £4440.
Then you have to consider the likely contingency
that Posinatus was not laid at all. Now take a ride
out to Sol Green’s magnificent Stud Farm at Shipley,
Warrnambool, and remember how he nearly secured
the “Leviathan” corner in Bourlce-street. That's
where the public’s money goes—when Ike doesn’t put
it into far worse things.
• Somebody who backed some other horse
has discovered that P-o-s-i-n-a-t-u-s spells “Pains to
us.”
* * * *
“C. O’H.” : Another blow at the good old
superstition that the purpose of racing is to “im-
prove the breed of horses.” The Melbourne Cup
winner this year is a gelding. If the V.R.C., and
the A.J.C., and similar inflated bodies really be-
lieved in the yarn about improving the breed, they
would never allow equine eunuchs to compete for the
prizes.
* * * *
“0.K.” : There’s a story of how Posinatus
for the Melbourne Cup was tipped to the local
police before the geegee was foaled, or soon after-
wards. It happened at the time of the raids on the
fortune-tellers. The police, chaffing the sorceress
whom they had just taken into custody, asked her to
tip some winners, and she replied that no turf
events of the near future had been revealed to her,
but she had foreseen the winner of the Cup for
1913. Its name would be Posinatus. This story
was told before the gee in question got home. I
heard the yarn and can swear to its previousness.
The probable explanation is that at the time of the
raid Poseidon and Positano were names of daily
note, and the sorceress made a blind dash at some
similar word. The detective on the job then kept
a careless recollection of the incident, and was
recently struck by the idea that Posinatus must
have been the verj r name the “clairvoyante” had told
him. But whether the lady said Posinatus, or
merely something like it, others in the “futurity”
line of business can reckon on a lively demand for
information re future Melbourne Cups.
A bygone coup that went wrong—The
Highborn one—was recalled by Belove’s Cup per-
formance. Decent prads both, one was set to carry
fist. 81b., the other 6st. 131 b. Both were ignored
by the public and overlooked by press touts, but so
steadily backed by a select few that they started at
“medium” prices. One had the bad luck to en-
counter the mighty Carbine, the other, ran against
the lightly-weighted and eccentric but capable Pos-
inatus, favored by the weather, and handled by a
lad who made the race to suit his mount. And
both missed the big prize by just three-quarters of a
length. # #
The Westralian Turf Club’s November
meeting at Perth on Saturday saw a couple of nice
dividends paid by the Tote. When Lord Flavus won
the November Handicap his backers drew £l6 135.,
while those who planked their worldly wealth on
Lucky Beggar for the Stewards’ Handicap went home
delighted with £29 10s. This writer has not heard
of the bookie laying odds like these; but on the
testimony of the bookmaker (who ought to know)
and the parsons (who know everything) the book-
maker is far more moral than the Tote. He doesn’t
encourage people to risk their money by paying
tong odds.
J. E. O’Brien is a mighty purchaser of
neddies before the Lord. Consider some of his buy-
ings. Beragoon, winner of both Derbvs, bought as a
yearling for 700 guineas, has already returned
£11,720 in prize money. Malt King was grabbed
as a yearling for 310 guineas, and the handsome
brute has brought in £12,613 in prizes. Great is
the art of successful buying.
The bone of contention pt the Prince
Alfred (Svdnev) Yacht Club's handicap last Satur-
day was tfip Rawhiti Cup. Sever s' t "rted > . Rawhiti
being the scratch boat and Sunbeam th" limit, with
25min. allowance. Hardly had the scratch boat got
away when the wind swung round, and blew abso-
lutely in favor of the long-start lighter yachts Sun-
beam’s handicap thus enabled her to lead over most
of the course. At the Pile Light, however, Aoma,

The Sydney Flying Squadron blew over
its affair in two beats and a final, and the second
one and the final were exciting. The first was a
dead-easy win for Australian, which ran right
through the fleet and beat everything. The only boat
to stand up to the flier was Quibree, which
filled second place. Arline led the second heat
until off Taylor Bay, where Nimrod, invisible under
its desert of canvas, went to the front and won by
30sec. The first five of each heat competed in the
final. The wind had become very fluky, and the positions
changed so rapidly it was hard to keen pace with
them. At one time Australian bolted to the front;
then Golding got ahead, to give place very quickly
to Nimrod, which was being forced along in great
stvle. Every rag that could be crowded on was
shaken out, and the excitement became intense. But
Nimrod maintained the lead, chased by Golding
(32sec.), with Australian and Kismet leading the
rest. In the rush for ho->e Golding was passed by
Kismet and Australian, w lich went after Nimrod —•
a hopeless task, and they finished in that order.

After a glorious defeat of brother Jim at
Ulmarra Regatta (N.S.W.), the whole Paddon family
came to light at the Coraki turn-out. James himself
was just beaten by Charles Towns, to whom he con-
ceded lOsec. in two miles. G. Paddon raked in the
Gladstone Skiffs, and F. Paddon and W. of that ilk
rowed gamely but vainly in the other events of the
day. Looks’ as though there are possibilities of a
Paddon dynasty.
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen is in a very
explosive state and liable to blow up at any moment.
The rank and file charge the council with apathy,
ignorance and utter carelessness as to the welfare
of professional cycling, which it declares is almost
dead. The council woke up, rubbed its eves, ant J
with a flourish of trumpets announced it would put
on a carnival, With a prize list worth £35. The
riders were too pained to speak at first, but. when
they recovered the power of speech they spoke bitterly
and to the point. The council is now clawing wildly
at Yankee sports promoter J. M. Chapman with a
view to a visit to see if he can resurrect the appar-
ently dying sport. Chapman is said to be vvilhng
and to be fluting eloquently about a six-days race
and a fine string of international wheelmen. Mean-
while the League is still liable to blow up.
* * * *
That United States athletic team left
'Frisco last Tuesday in thoroughly characteristic
Yankee style. Templeton, one of the stars of the
bundle, was “smuggled” aboard to prevent him
being kidnapped by the Stanford Leland University
sporting chiefs, who felt they could not spare him.
It’s an awful reputation to have to live up to, and
if he gets knocked over he will have a terrible dis-
tance to fall. These bean-eaters will riot away three
weeks in the Commonwealth, and then flee on, seek-
ing other worlds to conquer.
* * * *
The opening of the fifth round of Sydney
First-Grade Cricket was a mighty triumph for the
batsmen, headed by Bardsley, of Glebe, who carried
his bat for 201 in spite of the bowlers of Central
Cumberland. Moore, of North Sydney, lifted 136
heavenward before he fell l.b.w to Wall. Tozer, of
University, smacked 126, and his clubmate Gregg
backed him up with 101 ; and Lowe, of Middle
Harbor, left 132 on the board when he started for
the pavilion. In spite of Bardsley’s double century,
Glebe did not make the biggest score of the day.
North Svdnev accomplished that by smiting the
Balmain "bowling for 419 at the expense of seven
wickets. Glebe came next with 404 for seven
sticks. Sydney staggered humanity by bashing the
bowlers of Gordon for 318 for eight wickets.
University rushed up 307, and then took
four Petersham wickets for 40. . Middle Harbor
accumulated 296 off the Waverley bowling, and then
got a couple of men out for 53. Western Suburbs
was dismissed by Paddington for 163, which then
lifted 124 for the loss of four wickets. The best of
the trundlers were Newton, of Waverley, who took
four Middle Harbor sticks for 29 ; and Dive, of
Western Suburbs, who laid out four Paddington bats-
men for 34—that is to say, he took all the wickets
that fell during the innings for that insignificant
total. From Dive to Sedgers, of Petersham, with
his four University wickets for 70, is a mighty drop,
equalled only by that from Sedgers to O’Connor, of
Waverley, who retired four Middle Harbor men for
100. It was a funereal day for bowlers, even the
reliable and polished Folkard, of Balmain, taking
only two for 112.
Jerry Jerome, in the last rounds of his fight
with Les O' Donnell, tries to convince “fight
fans ” that he loves astronomy.
There was a sound of excitement in
Parramatta (N.S.W.) on Friday night about nine
o’clock when the starter despatched 20 motor-cyclists
on a 24-hours’ Reliability Test. The course was
from Parramatta via Hartley to Bathurst, thence
to Sofala and Hill End, and back to Parra-
matta via Mudgee, Capertee and Wallera-
wang, a total distance of 373 miles. The moon
was full and the sky absolutely clear, and the
whole crowd scaled the mountains and had an excel-
lent run right through to the old goldfield, where
the first division arrived at 7.54 Saturday morning.
Although a few points had been lost, the field was
on time; and it was not till Wallerawang was
reached on the return journey that five riders were
found to be behind. Two machines had smashed
up between Mudgee and ’Wang, one with a broken
wheel and the other with a fractured fork. The first
rider to reach Parramatta was Readford, at 8.44
Saturday night. Six others were on schedule, but
up to the time of writing the points had apparently
not been calculated, as no results had been issued as
regards the reliability.
The Australian Board of Cricket Control
made itself a gun last week, when the delegates
decided that there shall be a manager and a
treasurer with every Australian team visiting Eng-
land and elsewhere; also that they shall be ap-
pointed by and responsible to the Board only. It
confirms the attitude taken up by the Board last
year, and anticipates any kicking over the traces by
descendants of the Sorry Six. The Six stated that
the manager must be appointed by the “players” ;
the Board rises to affirm that he shall not, and the
players can like it or lump it. The constitution was
also altered to prohibit the touring of Australian
teams without the Board’s hall-mark —which in the
light of recent developments was a very necessary
step. The unpleasant conduct of some of the last
XI in England has moved the Board to reserve unto
itself the right to dispense with any player for other
reasons than those connected with cricket. The selec-
tion committee now consists of Clem Hill (S.A.), E.
E. Bean (Vic.) and Dr. Poidevin (N.S.W.).
» * * •
Clem Hill is on the warpath again. Re-
cently he was appointed a stipendiary steward to
keep racing in South Aus. wholesome and straight.
When a match started Hill batted for his side, but
the following Saturday he had to attend to the
morals of the geegee, and he wrote asking that a
substitute be sent into the field for him. His club
ignored the request, and fielded one man short. This
sent the peppery Hill into a blue passion, and he
has demanded an apology from the club under threat
of resignation. The club is tying low. It is hard to
see how Hill is going to play cricket and watch
horse-racing too ; and as cricket is bound to be the
one to be dropped overboard, perhaps the club would
sooner get it over and done with.
At its monthly meeting this week N.S.W.
Cricket Association was hit between the eyes by an
application from the Baseball Association that the
grade cricket matches should be postponed on Janu-
ary 3 to allow the cricketers to watch the inter-
national game of baseball between N.S.W. and the
U.S A. Although several members spoke strongly in
favor of obliging the baseballers, a motion to the
effect that the request be turned down was carried
by 12 votes to eight.
* * * *
This coming Saturday will see Melbourne
make an original burst by holding a motor-car race
on Richmond racecourse. There will be some motor-
bike events also, but the great attraction is to be a
three-heat struggle for a purse of £lOOO between
R. Jeff kins, who has raced in America and Europe,
and 1). Campbell, an Australian, who has given
splendid demonstrations at home. Jeffkins will urge
a 120-h.p. car which arrived from the U.S.A. only
last week, and has drawn an interested crowd when-
ever it has stopped for a drink. Campbell will use
the 40-h.p. Vinot with which he has favored Mel-
bourne with excellent demonstrations over the same
track. The question of whether the Richmond course
is suitable for car-racing seems to depend upon the
verdict of Jeffkins ; and as he is going to race over
it, it is presumably, in his opinion, good enough.
Saturday was an afternoon of kick-offs in
Melbourne. The combined yachting clubs’ opening
demonstration drew a big crowd to St. Kilda pier.
Half an hour before the fun started, a grey cloud of
rain descended softly on the city and the waters;
but just as people were starting homeward the sun-
shine burst out once more. Unfortunately, with the
rain went the wind, so the yachts floated lazily on
the glassy water, their sails occasionally flapping.
Only the auxiliary yachts could move ; the rest were
becalmed far and near, some off Williamstown, some
off St. Kilda, others all over the place. The motor
launches had a busy time towing them home. Down
at Port Melbourne the swimmers were also opening
the season. The male and female clubs had joined
hands for the occasion, and there was much bunting
hanging listless in the stagnant air. The feature of
the show was “the Dreadnought,” a large punt got
up to represent a warship, with guns and funnels
complete. Duck-egg shells were fired to the accom-
paniment of hungers and other fireworks; when the
shells floated ashore they were found to be in-
scribed with compliments to the local residents who
had in the past assisted the clubs. There was music,
swimming for the youngsters, footraces for men and
hobble-skirt steeplechases for ladies along the sands,
toys for the children, drinks for the men, and re-
freshments for everybody.
#*, * *
There was an awful rumor round Sydney
that Colonel Ryrie, M.ILR., the gentleman who eats
a Labor member every morning before breakfast and.
fights six of them at a time when he is feeling fresh,
had been appointed referee at Sydney’s new Stadium
at Newtown. However, it turns out to be a base
falsehood. The Colonel will not officiate. The mantle
has fallen on the shoulders of Harry Beckett, who
beat him in the ring in November, 1890. Although
Ryrie is said to have been the terror of his district,
there were men who could make mincemeat of him.
In 1891, or thereabouts, when he was well over
12st. in weight, he met a little lOst. 81b. chap
named Burke; and although the fox-terrier couldn’t
knock out the bull, the next time Ryrie talks fight
in the House, the hon. the Speaker might ask him
for a truthful account of how he felt after that dis-
turbance.
* * * ♦
There have before now been sensational
skedaddles by boxers out of matches they were not
confident about; but Willie Ritchie's sudden “bunk”
out of the fight with Freddie Welsh at Vancouver
seems to have been a record. Ritchie was not anxious
to fight for his championship, and after the match
was made tried repeatedly to get it altered to a
15-round no-decision affair. But Welsh wanted the
championship, and stuck to the terms of the agree-
ment. Ritchie was to receive $15,000, win, lose or
draw. As the fight had to be postponed owing to
Welsh having sprained an ankle, Ritchie got $lOOO
compensation for the delay. He held half the
moving-picture rights, and his choice of referee had
been conceded. Later on, he is said to have de-
manded half the profits made by selling cigars, ad-
vertising space and drinks in the stadium —worth,
roughly, another $lOOO. This the management re-
fused. Whether this refusal got on his nerves no
one knows; all that is certain is that he and his
manager stole quietly away the same, night on the
Seattle boat en route for ’Frisco. He was inter-
cepted at Seattle by one of the promoters, who had
gone by train, but he declined to carry out his
battle. His excuse was that the arranging of the
terms had worried him so much he could not settle
down to training, but he would come back later
on. The promoter opined he wouldn’t get the chance.
The laste straw was when he cancelled his cheque
for £2500 put up as a guarantee of good faith. The
promoters in future will take only cheques certified
to by the bank on which they are drawn. Appar-
ently the chief end of Ritchie is to dodge fighting
Freddie Welsh.
* * * »
In Paris they are calling Jack Johnson
“the pious young man.” The troublous darkie is
now threatening to hold a series of elimination con-
tests to decide who is worthy of the honor of being
punched to mush by himself. What does not seem
to occur to Johnson is that he is not getting any
younger, neither is he leading altogether the simple
life, and that it might be as well if he did some-
thing to ascertain he is still the mighty bruiser he
was in the days arear. Another nigger that fate has
not been over-kind to of late is Samuel Langford.
The decision of the New York Boxing Commission
to allow no bouts between black and white boxers
in its territory has made it impossible for him to
earn a living in Yankeeland’s greatest city. The
only negro fighter that has any reasonable show
against him is Joe Jeannette —and he can’t fight
Jeannette always. Their last battle was the ninth.
And when he carved up a hopeless giant named John
Lester Johnson, the papers howled “butchery.” It
does seem to have been a miserable affair. John
Lester Johnson deliberately sprinted round the ring,
with Langford in full pursuit. He had lapped it
three times when Langford hit him in the middle
of the back, right on the spine, and down went
Johnson for nine. Then he scrambled up and bolted
again, but Langford was ready and smashed him on
exactly the same spot and sent him down again.
Then the referee snorted that it was a fight, not a
footrace, and gave Langford the decision. Langford
struck only two blows, and for that received £5OO,
or, roughly, £lOO. How would the same hard-
fighting gentleman have roared with fury at the in-
sult had he been asked to fight for £lOO in Aus-
tralia? The chances are that he will move on to
Paris, where they seem to be very partial to the
negro fighter. He will probably be rejoined there
by Samuel McVea, the Home Department having
notified this ebony Hercules that he will be granted
no further extension (he has already had three) of
his permit to stay in Australia.
At Brisbane on Saturday night fortune
smiled on Waldemar Holberg for the first time since
he landed in Australia, and he overthrew Ted Porter
a recent arrival from Maoriland. Porter’s corner skied
the towel of surrender in the eighth round, when it
was obvious the Maorilander had no possible hope
At the Baker Stadium in Melbourne Eddie Newhouse
was given the verdict over Alf Spenceley after 20
very mixed rounds. Fullalove, the referee, came in
for a hostile demonstration. He has had so much of
this sort of thing lately that he is said to contem-
plate changing his name to Fullup. Next Saturday
week will see the opening of the new Olympia
Stadium at Newtown, Sydney, when there will be a
disturbance between Dave Smith and Les O’Donnell.
The riot at the Baker temple in Sydney will be
between Herbert McCoy and Pal Brown. The popu-
lar impression is that McCoy is in for a hiding.
* * * *
Eddie McGoorty, the Yankee middleweight
boxer who polished Smith off in half a round when
they met in New York, left ’Frisco for Sydney on
Tuesday last. He and Dave Smith were to have
supplied an international battle at Sydney Stadium
for Boxing Day. But Smith, flushed with his recent
success apparently, made his price so high that
Baker abandoned the idea. Smith did zhe same
thing when the Mclnstoush matched him with
Clabby, and the scrap fell through. Another battle,
preceded by quite a Yankee amount of haggling, was
the Stone-Wells disturbance for November 29. Stone
demanded that Wells should make 9st. 91b. at 2
p.m. the day of the fight. Wells refused to sink
below 9st. 111 b., and after a fearsome argument
Baker remarked coldly he had taken a sudden dislike
to the match, and called a halt. Then both parties
hurriedly compromised on 9st. 101 b. at 1 a.m. the
day of the dancing match. The Jew has been having
a great innings of late, and on that night the
oppressed Gentile will be able to sit back and sigh
with satisfaction at the sight of two of the Chosen
knocking Gehenna out of each other. Quite apart from
that, it should be a wildly interesting scrap. Neither
are hard punchers, but in that respect Wells has some-
what the advantage. On the other hand, there is
more than a suspicion that neither thrive much on
punishment, but there is also a superstition that
Stone can digest more of that food than Wells. It'
Stone wins he will have a dazzling career opened up
to him. If Wells goes under, it will be an unmiti-
gated disaster to him, following on the defeat by
Mehegan.
* * * *
The fact that he had already been de-
feated twice by Les O’Donnell did not inspire Jerry
Jerome to deeds of derring-do at Sydney Stadium
on Saturday night. Jerry, in addition to his usual
ring garb, wore a distinctly anxious expression. He
indulged in no humorous interludes. He settled
down to business from the sound of the gong, and
tried hard for a knock-out in the shortest possible
time. His swings were terrific; but O’Donnell was
seldom there, and when he was he caught them on
his gloves and arms. , But, - all the same, for the
first seven rounds Jeremiah piled up points and was
distinctly leading. Then O’Donnell stood up straight,
dropped' his crouch for good, and began to spear
the prophet with a quick straight left. It landed
again and again seventy-times-seven on the mouth
and chin of Jeremiah, and the aperture became a
wreck. It spoilt Jerome’s aim and caused his blows
to go wild. Then his eyes began to swell. Never-
theless, Jeremiah once sent the adversary reeling to
the ropes, and but for the sounding of the gong the
fight had ended right there. Thereafter Jerry became
so dazpd that twice he made for his rival's corner.
His strength seemed to leave him, and he had diffi-
culty in towing his feet round after him. In the
16th he suddenly flashed out and made O’Donnell
hang on for safety ; but it was only a flash in the
pan, and after it he faded more than ever. He was
almost gone in the 19th, but got to his corner, and
when he came out for the final it was obvious that
a very slight one would finish him. That sustained
storm of wallops on the mouth and chin had done
their work. A straight left sent him down for six,
and then he came up again. O’Donnell charged.
Jerry went down on all fours. The referee declared
he had fallen without being hit, and disqualified him
on the spot. The truth was Jerry didn’t know
whether he was hit or not. His tail was right down,
and he was done to the last turn. He knew that if
he had not been hit he was just going to be, and
to his aboriginal simplicity the difference was un-
distinguishable.

There hardly seems to have been a time
when the N.S.W. Parliament wasn’t scan-
dalising someone. In his day Henry Kendall
wrote: —
House with high, august traditions —Chamber where
the voice of Lowe,
And the lordly words of Wentworth sounded thirty
years ago—
Halls familiar to our fathers, where, in days exalted
rang
All the tones of all the feeling which ennobled Bland
and Lang—
We in ashes—we in sackcloth, sorrow for the insult
cast
By a crowd of bitter boobies on the grandeur of
your past!
Take again your penny whistle—boy, it is no good
to me ;
Last invention is a bladder with the title of M.P.
Ninian Melville was the victim of those
verses; and, part of them being very warm,
they were withdrawn at his instance.
Quite a lot of -people might just as well
not have White Australia principles for all
the use they put them to. For instance,
in a N.S.W. country town, which has been
sending good White Australians to Parlia-
ment for years, tenders were lately invited
by the committee of the School of alleged
Arts for the lease of its hall. The two
highest tenders were for the same amount;
one came from a Chinaman, the other from
an Australian. Yet it was decided to give
the preference to the Chow. And only one
member of the committee resigned in pro-
test ! But, then, thanks to the consistent
support of people who say they believe in a
White Australia, this Chinaman is affluent,
and when from business motives lie invites
a customer to have a drink, that man feels
quite honored.

Previous to liet Fatal Wedding, as she
called it, Mat-jorie Heinbockel dashed off a
few rem&l-ks to a Melbourne friend re the
imminent event. Wedding presents already
totalled 300, and all the Moneyed Set had
taken the merry little Melbourne girl to its
bejewelled bosom. Pier gorgeous trousseau,
with 20 dresses as the pieces de resistance ,
had been selected for her by Lady
Raphael; and that lady’s husband, the
M.P. for Derby, had given the happy pail-
some choice Chippendale furniture, a full
equipment of household silver, and five won-
derful prints. Sir Herbert Raphael is bride-
groom Norman Raphael’s fond and particu-
lar unde. Father-in-law-elect was the
donor, seemingly, of a pearl and diamond
pendant that cost £BOO, and Marjorie’s gifts
from the bridegroom were a diamond
brooch, gold bag and a fur coat. Mortimer
Menpes presented the fair Marjorie with a
Whistler etching, as a suitable expression
of his admiration for one so unconvention-
ally artistic as she. Her wedding dress
was d la Victoria, with white tulle flounces
and a train loaned by Lady Rap-
hael aforesaid, who got it as a present from
the late good Queen Vic. Three brides-
maid children were engaged for the cere-
mony, and Capt. Muirhead Collins, Acting
High Commissioner for Australia, was to
give the bride away. The letter tells also
of her home-to-be (a flat in Rutland Court,
with windows overlooking Rotten Row),
and goes into ecstasies concerning furniture
bought from the Oppenheimer collection,
and a carpet of a make so wonderful that
its very name is undecipherable in Mrs.
Marjorie Norman Raphael’s unrivalled
scrawl. The moral of it all is Advance
Australia.
The bitter cold caused one dazzling Cup
dress to be obscured by wraps. The Rupert
Clarke lady, carefully muffled in furs, was
robed in cloth-of-gold with a tunic of bar-
baric colors held together by a line of seal
fur. Beneath the hem of the furs she showed
stockings inlet with lace down to the toes,
and satin shoes that were really sandals with
three-inch heels. A black turban with an
exclamatory feather was the last item of the
outfit.
While the rain was falling on the Cup I
heard one blue-lipped, red-nosed vision, in
shadow lace and ninon shot with hailstones,
chatter to a damp and purple-faced com-
panion: “Have you got much on, dear?”
“For this race, do you mean?” “No! Have
you got much on — underneath ?”
Dr. Friedmann drifted round at race-time
and persistently quoted the classics to women
who only wanted to learn what chance their
sweep horse had, or if the barometer was
rising. The trouble about a good classinoi
education is that very few ever get over it

Girls are developing a habit of holding up
skimpy trains while dancing; it is rather
suggestive of tails. A still later item of
fashionable interest is that ears are to be
hidden and the hair lowered to the eyes all
round the forehead.
From “Rosna,” in Britain:—
Miss Asquith Baker, cf Melbourne, who is working
with her brush on this side, has an exhibition at
the Baillie Gallery. It is a slender little show-
one or two subdued but interesting portraits, a
few flower studies, and some English landscapes—
notably “Dead Timber” and “The Road to the
Farm,” all full of feeling. The Baillie is a
gallery to which most Australian daubers gravitate
