Business, Robbery, etc.
“Harrison 0.”: There is a coffee palace in Mel-
bourne which, like most places of the sort, is run
on strictly Wowser lines, so that couples who arrive
late at night and are suspected of being either more
or less to one another than they should be, are liable
to be told that the place is full, or that the sheets
belonging to the spare bed are in the wash, or some-
thing of the kind. However, the proprietor of a
tobacco emporium near by recently got in a stock of
secondhand bags, and caused it to be known among
the “nuts” that travelling gear might be hired by the
night at a reasonable rate. The scheme, 1 am told,
has proved deplorably successful. With a coffee-
palace booking-clerk a worn “Gladstone” or port-
manteau is considered as strong a guarantee of re-
spectability as elastic-sided boots.
* * * *
Sherlock Holmes couldn’t have managed it better.
It happened in a South Aus. out-port. A s boots
were taken while he lay dead-to-the-world on the
wharf, the taker thoughtfully leaving him some old
slippers in their place. A fortnight later he had
another carouse—or it may have been a later chapter
of the same one —and this time got locked up. In
the same cell was B, and on his feet were the lost
boots. B’s story that he had bought them from 0,
a party unknown, was considered thin ; so A got the
boots, and B got a month.
* * * *
“Ividman’s Man” : Beef cattle in the United States
have decreased 30 per cent, while the population has
increased by 11 per cent. There is a parallel decrease
of sheep in Europe. These matters point to the in-
calculable future of Australian meat. At the annual
convention of American packers, lately held, it was
argued that every small farmer should raise at least
two beef steers. But they won’t do it. Said the
president: “As things are going, steak will be a
dollar a lb. in 10 years!” No fear! Australia can
put it in for under a quarter dollar. The first ship-
ment of chilled beef direct to New York from Argen-
tina, 200,0001 b., arrived last month. It was promptly
taken and distributed. The retail price was from one
to 14 cents per lb. under that of American beef. Yet
a duty of 14 cents per lb. had been paid, and that
duty is under sentence of removal. A little Aigen-
tina beef has been going to New York by way of Eng-
land. Now it will be sent straight. The total U.b.
beef imports of late have been: June, 567,2051 b. .
July, 642,3331 b. ; August, 824,3421 b. San Francisco
took, mostlv Australian, in June, 143,0131 b. ; in
July, 410,2321 b. ; and in August, 397,3551 b. A second
cargo, 1,000,0001 b., is reported to be on the way
from Buenos Ayres to New York ; so, plainly, others
besides ourselves are smelling out the new market.
* * * *
“Sapphire”: The Sheet and Plate Glass Workers
asked the other day for an all-round increase of
18s. per week. The employers swooned as one man,
and on being brought to, referred the matter back to
the union. Their anguish was comprehensible. The
trade in silvered and bevelled goods is purely local.
There is no inter-State, and very little Island, trade.
Fully 50 per cent, of the whole is in stock sizes,
the imported dutv on which is a paltry 20 pei
cent. Now Pinkington’s, probably the largest fac-
tory in England, have only two men engaged m
their largest department. The rest are girls who
start at ss. a week, and may, if expert, rise to Cl.
This house alone could swamp the local market
witli stock sizes, leaving the local employers and
employees to struggle for the remaining 50 per cent,
of trade. In other words, the Australian operative
would be slain dead commercially by the im-
porter. What is wrong with such a duty as would
enable the local employer to live, while paying a
bit extra to the local employee?
* * * *
Australia, having got the load of imported calico
off its back and begun to stand up straight, begins
to find it can make and do quite a lot of things.
As far back as 1891 Tanunda (S.A.) was heterodox
enough to believe that even brandy could be pro-
duced in these sunny parts; and, though it was a
hard struggle to get other people to believe that the
liquor could be anything but rubbish, it managed to
carry its point in the end. Now one co. alone
squeezes ho less than 3500 tons of grapes a year.
That means 600,000 gallons of wine, of which 500,000
are distilled for brandy; and as the law demands
that all Australian brandy shall be kept in wood for
at least two years, these Tanunda people now keep
in No. 1 bond alone enough to fill 1,800,000 bottles.
There is another-new store with 100,000 gallons;
and in various smaller bonds are casks and vats, with
a total capacity of 1,600,000 gallons. At the outset,
though Australian vines were cracked up to be all
that vines should be, it wasn’t thought possible
that anything but imported oak could be good enough
to hold the juice; but nowadays they know better,
and vats as good as any in the world are made of
Australian jarrah.
“Sapphire”: King Edward YI. authorised the use
of a Book of Private Prayer. Evidently the land-
lords of that time were hardy sinners, for the
following was included in the Primer: “The earth
is Thine, O Lord, and all that is contained therein;
notwithstanding Thou hast given the possession
thereof unto the children of men, to pass over the
time of their short pilgrimage in this vale of
misery. We heartily beseech Thee to send Thy Holy
Spirit .into the hearts of them that possess the
grounds, pastures and dwelling places of the earth,
that they, remembering themselves to be Thy tenants,
mav not rack and stretch out the rents of their
houses and lands, nor yet take unreasonable fines
and incomes after the manner of covetous worldlings,
but so let them out to others that the inhabitants
thereof may both be able to pay the rents, and also
honestly to live, to nourish their families, and to
relieve the poor. Give them grace also to consider
that they are but strangers and pilgrims in this
world, having here no dwelling place, but seeking
one to come; that they, remembering the short con-
tinuance of their life, may be content with that
which is sufficient, and not join house to house, nor
couple land to land, to the impoverishment of others,
but so behave in letting out their tenements, lands
and pastures, that after this life they may be received
into everlasting dwelling places. Amen.” In the
absence of a Fair Rents Bill, I move that the Reli-
gious Editor add a curse to the above. It will do
for Australian use then.
“Bad Australian” (from the Argentine) : Australia
is beginning to take notice of this territory. A few
days ago the Remuera dropped her mudhook inside
the breakwater at Monte Video, and dumped many
thousand cases of Tasmanian apples for transhipment
to Buenos Ayres. As a general thing, the M.L.
Shipping Co.’s boats hang off about seven kilometres
while they hoist a few passengers aboard for the
Cold Country. People interested in the business tell
me that they pronose to keep on importing Tasmanian
pippins. I think they ought to, seeing that the
pirates charge 25 cents, which means sd. in good
Mount Lvell copper, for each sample of the fruit that
wrecked humanity.
HE HADN’T THE HEART TO SHOOT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The burglar came while Levi snored,
And gathered up a swag' of loot.
He fell upon Abe’s little hoard
Of ready cash, took socks and suit,
And turned to leave the window by,
When Rachel fetched an awful cry.
“Oh, Abe, awake!” She shook the boss.
“A bur-gu-lar come by der place.
Ve suffer terrible der loss
Kef you don’d shoot him by der face.”
Then Abe he scrambled for his gun,
And Mike the burglar made his run.
Abe took good aim, but did not fire ;
He let his gun drop by the bed.
“Vy don'd you shood?” in frantic ire
Exclaimed his spouse; and Levy said.
“I can’d. If dot gun I should shoot
I lose der cartriches. to boot!”
VICTORIA.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them
the strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What
a relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like g trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt
the ear. It fits the ori-
fice of the ear with pre-
cision and comfort. The
division that runs across
the mouth is to allow the
removal and insertion of
the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sour'd.
Of course, if
you have lost
the he a r ing-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
if you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without
the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor's single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send’ them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order Form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
ORDER FORM.
STAR NOVELTY CO ,
103 Arlington Chambers,
229 Collins Street, Melbourne.

Wilson Common-sense Ear-Drums, an Inserter and a
Remover, for which I enclose One Pound.

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
V
It has a hogskin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of The
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J. P. Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

BANANA LAND
Once again it is rumored that Kidston is
to re-enter politics. Those who know him
best are the most sceptical. As Land Court
Commissioner Wullie gets a salary of four
figures-—and he comes from Falkirk.
A Cremation Bill, lately passed by the
Leg. Council, threatened to be shelved in-
definitely, but the Assembly was urged last
week to treat it as a burning question, and.
recognising that there was something in this
plea, parties sank their differences and put
the desirable thing through.
The shoe is pinching in some of those dis-
tricts in Bananaland where there is a short-
age in railway earnings, and the deficit has
to be met by the land-owners of the benefited
area. A man told the Railways Minister
sorrowfully the other day that his share of
the contribution to a shortage line would be
30s. per week—“and,” he added, “land is
going down in value.” It is good that some-
thing stops the use of the air pump.
A Brisbane speculator happened into the
Lands Office on a recent sale day. An allot-
ment on the left bank of the Caboolture River
was under the hammer. He had interests
there, so he handed over £5l for it. When he
got home and had time to digest the surveys,
he found that in May last he had bought
the same piece of land from the local coun-
cil at an arrears-of-rates sale for £2. He
is now hankering after his £5l, and has
thoughts of sueing the Government for false
pretences, or whatever the law calls it.
There is so much spare land in the Northern
State that half the time nobody knows who
owns what.
The Walter Russell Hall Trust donations
to the Bananaland hospitals have proved a
godsend, as most of the institutions were on
their uppers. But a beetle has appeared
even in this welcome windfall, the Govern-
ment having refused to subsidise the moneys
on the ground that they are bequests. A
few of the absolutely stony, like Roekhamp
ton, told such a pitiable tale to Denham
that he gave them £ for £, and now he is
facing the music on a charge of discrimina-
tion. Dalby. for its part, got legal advice
on the matter, and has been advised that
the moneys are donations, and not bequests,
and are thus due for subsidy, so Denham
may have to pay up all round, after all.
Incidentally the beer fines have been taken
from the hospitals, and to counterbalance
the loss the hospitals have asked for a tax
on picture shows and such things. Truly the
scramble for funds to pay for keeping the
State’s sick people alive is a humiliating
business altogether.
Robert Philp, the alleged Protectionist, is
up against the absentee tax; it “created
more prejudice against the State than it
was worth.” Customs duties also create a
prejudice against the State, for the foreign
manufacturer hates them. Does Robert,
the Protectionist, think that sufficient reason
for abolishing Customs duties? Instead of
doing anything so foolish Robert and other
Protectionists say: “Come here and make
your goods, and you’ll escape taxation.” The
absentee tax says exactly the same thing:
“Come here and invest your money, and so
far as I am concerned you’ll pay nothing.”
Queensland Savings Bank publishes in
the Gazette a list of the credit accounts of
over £3 which have been so long “dead”
that they have been passed to a special
“unclaimed deposits” ledger. The list runs
to the length of many newspaper columns,
and gives a pretty good idea of the pickings
which “unclaimed deposits” may mean to a
bank. Tommy Tanna seems to have been a
particularly casual customer —or it may be
that Tommy died with extraordinary
rapidity in the land where the discolored
gentleman was supposed to thrive. Any-
way, there are dozens of Tommy Tannas on
the list; for instance. Charlie, of Meteor
Downs, £214; Bob Gulling, of Rockhamp-
ton, £347; Lona, of Nebo. £79. Still there
are half a dozen “white” names for every
Tommy Tanna —some of them opposite
amounts running close up to £5OO. Heaven
only knows what tragedies are buried in
such a catalogue.

“Politicos”: Why does the Government
trouble the Railway Commissioner for a
report on every proposed extension? The
late Commissioner utterly condemned the
Port Alma line and damned the Tara exten-
sion, and the present Commissioner now
damns the Via Recta. Yet Port Alma was
built; so was Tara; and the Via Recta, de-
spite its recent rejection, looks as though it
will win through in a year or two. Why
worry about official reports? Count up log-
rollers and go ahead !
The Brisbane sewerage scheme is being
treated like several other important schemes
—the Sydney bridge, for instance. It is so
necessary that the Government recognises
that on no account must a mess be made of
it; wherefore successive Ministries do the
next worst thing and leave it alone, and thus
have the money to spend on something else
which doesn’t offer the same excuses for
delay. However, as the Treasurer reports
that “the scheme is still receiving much con-
sideration,” it is quite liable to come within
the life of the youngest inhabitant.
“Rangan”: Denham’s Minister for Agri-
culture White is a shining example of what
a Minister ought not to be. He reports that
“the conditions in Queensland are not so
favorable to the development of agriculture
as in the other States.” This is merely
shirking the responsibility for poor work.
Queensland has only half a million acres
under crops, while N. S. Wales, Victoria and
South Aus. have each over three millions.
Even Westralia has over a million. In
sugarcane, almost Queensland’s monopoly,
N. S. Wales oroduces 28 tons per acre to
Queensland’s 16 —which is disgraceful. It’s
time White shook up the department, or
somebody shook off White.
A genial Judge in the early days of
Quensland used to “go circuit” to an out-
west township, where there was no church
and no parson. The Judge, who arrived on
Friday evening, was asked by deputation to
read the Anglican prayers at the court-house
on the following Sunday, and on no account
to omit the prayer for rain, as there had been
a 12-months’ drought. The Judge promised,
and duly officiated on the Sunday ; but, some-
how, in place of reading the prayer for
rain, he turned over the wrong leaf, and
substituted the “thanksgiving for rain.” The
subject was mentioned to him after church,
and his mistake pointed out. “Look here,
boys,” said he. “It’s never a good plan to
open a fresh account before you’ve squared
off the old debt. I’ll be bound ye niver
thanked Providence for the last batch of rain
ye got, and owe for it still. Now I’ve
squared that bill for ye, and ye can ask for
more with a clean conscience.”
.Tudve Rutledge, who looks all his 73,
uearly hurst with indignation at the Goondi-
windi sessions. He had summed up against
an alleged cattle-stealer, but the jury found
accused Not Guilty; whereupon the packed
courthouse rang for two minutes with
cheers. The .Judge directed the orderlies
to locate .some of the offenders, and the
“Johns” duly made an invidious selection of
three: Then for 48 minutes by the clock his
Honor lectured the trio on the enormity of
their offence. It gave the old Judge a rare
opportunity to attest his wonderful mental
capacity by quoting off-hand English and
Australian cases galore, Regina versus a
dozen or so, and Rex versus even more,
mostly with something boiling at the end
of them. The batch expressed profound
contrition when the lesson was ended—and
that, of course, was the finish of it.
The Palace of Peace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw a stately palace on a hill,
Of marble pale and pure;
It shimmered whitely in the glance of morn,
x\lone, serene, secure;
And I, a stranger, wondered who lived there
Secluded thus in such a mansion fair.
I neared at last a shining portico,
Intent to push my way ;
But of a sudden came a lady forth,
Her brow as bright as day ;
And I grew bold at sight of that dear face
And, begging pardon for my want of grace,
Inquired : “O lady, wouldst thou kindly tell
Whose palace this may be?”
Straight from her smiling lips the answer
fell:
“’Twas builded, friend, for me —
Built for my refuge in a parlous age
When brutal men and brutal passions rage.
“My name is Peace, and long without a
home
I wandered far and w r ide,
By fierce barbarians daily driven forth,
Unsure of time and tide;
Until men builded me this mansion dear
And fenced it round, and bade me linger
here.
“I am, thou seest, the tenant of the place;
No other home I own ;
And from its open windows oft I hear
The blasts of battle blown.
But if I dare to pass the threshold o’er
They drive me back with gun-butts to the
door.
“Aye, well they say the Palace is of Peace,
For if I seek a bed
In any land but this, the warring clans
Heap curses on my head ;
This is. and has been since my lowly birth,
The only place I occupy on Earth.”
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.

Considering the season, there have been
lately, a few social entertainings above the
average. On Monday night Lieut.-Col God!
dard and the officers of the 7th Infantry
earned the murmured thanks of the Orna-
mental Classes, for taking them on a river
trip aboard the Emerald. The regiment is
noted for its hospitality, and that evening
in its aim to entertain, it showed the old'
deadly precision.
Honorable mention is also made of the
Musical Union’s concert last Tuesday. Mrs
Gilbert Wilson —beautifully frocked, as
usual —was singer-in-chief, and deserved all
the nice things said about her. She is un-
loading her voice to-night at the Continental
in Dutton Park, which place of entertain-
ment has once more been taken over by
enterprising Hugh Black.
The marriage drought, which has been
causing anxiety, appears to be breaking up.
Next week is to witness at least two wed-
dings. On Wednesday (25th) the elder
O’Hagan daughter will take for better, or as
much of it as possible, one Campbell Ram-
say, from Victoria. Ivo Brennan and H. C.
Mustard will take their plunge two days
later. Besides getting a new name, the
bride will have a new home in Malaysia, so
she may be said to be in for a warm time.
Our ’Varsity students’ energy is as limit-
less when the thermometer stands at 90 deg.
as when it is at 50 deg. In fact, I’m begin-
ning to think the Northern climate is a
swindle and a Fiji uncle. This afternoon
the annual cricket match between St. John’s
and King’s College took place at the Exhibi-
tion Oval, in the vicinity of a large number
of more or less admiring fellow scholars.
All present were tea-ed by some of the heads
of the University. To-night a number of
the students, who have styled themselves
“The Khayyamites,” held their initial bean-
feast at Eschenhagen’s. The old tent-
maker’s banquet of a loaf of bread and a
flask of wine was a mere circumstance to it.
Of course O. Khayyam had “Thou” (he
didn’t mention her front name) as a frill
to the meal, which probably made a differ-
ence. On Thursday next the Students
will have another rush into the limelight,
when a dramatic entertainment of some sort
will be given. The coins derived therefrom
are going to a fund for the proposed Resi-
dential College for Women.
It’s not all ice-cream and strawberries
being honorary pianiste to a musical society,
but Miss Essie Lloyd (who has for the past
six years been the good Samaritan to the
Musical Union) last week reaped a share
of the just reward for her labors, when the
conductor and members loaded her down with
a silver hot-water kettle, a set of entree
dishes, other metal ware, and enough com-
pliments to float a wreck. The presentation
was in honor of the fast-approaching date
when the popular miss will be a missus.
The Overseas Club shivooed on board the
Lucinda this afternoon. The star feature
of the entertainment was the exhibition of
a handsome cabinet, made of Queensland
woods, which is destined to adorn the War-
rego when she pokes into this port. Two
large silk flags are also to form part of the
Overseas Club’s presentation to the gun-
boat.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

According to a correspondent, who calls himself
“Sunny Jim,” “the White Cross League takes the
place of Arthurian knighthood, and aims at curing
the male of his f propensity for smutty yarns, educat-
ing him out of his indulgence in the glad eye, and
securing his co-operation in the safe incarceration of
the old Adam. The prevalence of self-abuse among
bovs, the solicitation of street women, the traffic
of brothel, and the threatened prominence of the
White Slave Traffic compose the cause of its exist-
ence. It is actively engaged in a system of educa-
tion in sexual physiology. In some of the State
schools of Victoria and New South Wales addresses
are being given in the functioning of the reproduc-
tive organs. These lectures are graded to suit vary-
ing ages, yet much room for improvement is needed.
So far, they have not been recognised officially.”....
TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Eric D.: Go away and play and work for seven
years. Then, if you still think you can draw, let’s
hear from y0u.... Pompon: There isn’t a picture in
it.
“Silas Snell” gives the whole show away:
My friend Rose is an artist,, and an excellent artist
too, * although it was a long time before his native
country consented to put his talent to good use. A
few years back, in one of his bad periods, when the
rent was more overdue than usual, and the bailiff
had deserted, him because there was nothing in the
house to sit on, Rose secured a commission from a
neighbor, who was a temperance fanatic and lecturer,
to paint a drunkard’s liver. Rose had never seen a
drunkard’s liver, and none of his drunken friends
would submit to a surgical operation in the interests
of art. So the poor painter, in the absence of the
living model, painted the goods from the lay figure—-
a colored plaster cast then being displayed in the
window of Melbourne Temperance Hall. When an
excellent life-size portrait was completed, the tem-
perance orator-went back on his order. He had been
converted to bowls, or something. To soften the blow,
however, he undertook to give the price agreed upon
£3—for a painting suitable for a drawing-room.
Rose went home, turned the liver upside down, dashed
in a lurid sun over the top, stuck a dead tree in the
right-hand corner, and delivered the framed result to
our art patron, labelled “Sunset on Mount Ararat.”
The story does not end here. More recently, when
Rose had developed into a fairly successful brush, he
held a one-man show in Melbourne, and a good
buyer turned up. “Do you know,” he said, “I have
already got a little . painting of yours ? I picked it
up cheap; but it’s a remarkably clever thing. I
doubt if you’ve done anything better. It is called
‘Sunset on Mount Ararat.’ ”
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Apropos the Cheeke-Callaghan anecdote (B.
23/10/’l3). Alfred Cheeke was called to the Eng-
lish Bar in 1835, came to Sydney 1837, and got into
practice at once. In 1811 he was appointed Crown
Prosecutor, and so moved onward to the Supreme
Court Bench. Tom Callaghan was called to the
Irish Bar in 1839, came to Sydney in 1810, and was
appointed a Crown Prosecutor in 1811, the same year
as Cheeke. In December, 1814, he became Chairman
of Sessions, and in 1859 was appointed one
of “the first District Court Judges. Like Cheeke, he
was fond of a good horse, and in 1863, being at
Braidwood, purchased a colt in the saleyards
there. Two men were drafting the animals from one
yard to another when the one the Judge had pur-
chased charged the sliprails, against which its new
owner was standing. One bar, torn from the mortise
hole, struck his Honor on the jaw, smashing it and
doing other damage. Callaghan was soon able to
get about his room, and insisted on taking a walk
in the hotel garden. Dr. Beer endeavored to dissuade
him, without avail. That night he was worse, and
the family medical attendant, Dr. Nathan, was wired
for. But Nathan could not leave Sydney, so Mrs.
Callaghan’s brother, Dr. Milford, went inst«ad. ( The
latter decided that the case was hopeless— aud so it
proved. The remains were interred at 1> -> idwood
(in St. Bede’s churchyard), and a handsome monu-
ipent was placed over the grave by public subscrip-
tion. Mrs. Callaghan still lives at her home, Avoca,
Randwick, with her only daughter, Mrs. Auber-Jones.
Her father was Justice Milford, of an old Devonshire
family; he died in 1865, and there is a fine window
in St. Andrew's Cathedral provided by the Bar of
N. S. Wales in memory of him and Justice Wise,
Bernhard’s father.

H ouse, Sydney ; and
Commonwealth Works Registrars, Bris-
bane, Adelaide, Perth and Hobart.

which must be paid before the documents
can be issued.
Tenders are to be endorsed “Tender for

and are to be accompanied by the deposit
mentioned in the Form of Tender.

The Inky Way.
“Locust” : The press-censorship-in-war discussion,
now on in London, recalls recent experiences in
the Balkan scraps. Turkey, under German tactics,
refused the correspondents access to the “front.”
Bulgaria let the press go loose, encouraged it,
and abundantly supplied it with full news
of all the things the Bulgarian com-
manders wanted known. The European press
rang with news of Bulgarian victories and of
Turkish retreats. These were telegraphed to Con-
stantinople, and though the local press there was
censored, the “news” leaked out. Turkish soldiers
in the field, who had been really advancing, thus
heard that they and the whole army were retreating.
So they stampeded. The worried commanders there-
upon communicated with Constantinople, and said, in
effect: “In Allah’s name, send us some war corres-
pondents ! We are not being donkey-licked, as
alleged.” The few correspondents in Constantinople
who were left—most of them had gone, tired out of
waiting round on the Turkish end of the business —
were frantically appealed to by the Government and
the military authorities. “Go to the front. Go any-
where. See anything. Transport will be provided.”
Two of them were specially franked through, like a
couple of commanders-in-chief. Martin Donohoe, of
Sydney, was one of the two. He had lingered in
Constantinople, having an unconquerable faith in the
eventual success of the Turks under the Napoleonic
Enver Bey, who was returning post-haste from the
Italian war to retrieve the disasters. Donohoe reached
the front in time to describe, with thrill-
ing effect, the horror of the Lule Burgas defeats and
retreats ; and to show the world what a fine, patient,
brave, enduring chap the Turk soldier is, and what
a rascal and a botch his superior is—the allegd superior
who starved him on the battlefield while food was
lying in profusion half a mile away.
“T. 0.1.”: An ancient playbill was recently un-
covered in Melbourne after being hidden for 60 years.
The bill is of the year 1851, and was found by a
contractor engaged in dismantling a Little Lonsdale-
street building at one time licensed as the Queens-
town Hotel. In tearing away the old cedar facing
boards in the front of the building, the bill was dis-
closed pasted on a solid red-gum post, which formed
a portion of the doorway of the old hotel. Evidently
it had got there while the building was under erec-
tion. The name of the printer is still visible: “John
Ferres, Printer, Morning Herald office, Melbourne.”
This John Ferres was the son of a printer and short-
hand reporter of -Bath. Coming to Melbourne in
1848, within a weekvl*ehad got a job as a composi-
tor on the Herald —whose office was in Bourke-
st.reet, near the G.P.O. —and in the course of a few
months he became manager. It was by his advice
that the first steam printing press in this part of
the world was introduced and installed in the Herald
office, and under his superintendence the first 8-page
newspaper was produced. Two months after the
printing of the playbill just discovered Mr. Ferres
was appointed Government Printer of Victoria, and
held the office for many years. The Government
Printing Office in those days was in Little Bourke-
street West, close to old Government House; the
latter was in William-street, where the Law Courts
now are.
Adelaide Register (1/11/’l3) had a neat uninten-
tional joke at Josiah Symon’s expense. The literary
K.C. had been addressing the Poetry Recital Society
in Adelaide, on “Poetry and the Busy Man,” and he
quoted Wordsworth :
“Will no one tell me what she sings?
Perhaps the plaintive numbers flow
From old, unhappy, far-off things,
And battles long ago.”
The linotype, with due regard for professional cour-
tesy, made it “plaintiff numbers.”
“O’Phimerty”: lie the statue of Mercury, men-
tioned by “Melb ” (B. 23/8/’l3) as adorning the
Age office. Its purchase and erection were one of
old David Syme’s dour jests, at the expense of the
Argus, which pitches its tent near by. In the
ancient myth, Argus, having 98 spare eyes, was set to
spy on a pal of Mercury’s. That speedy gentleman
happened along, just in the nick of time, with a
lyre. His dreamy music closed the hundred peepers
with which Argus viewed the world, so that not even
one of them remained open. Thereupon Mercury set
upon him and brought his career to a miserable end.
Hey, mon, it was all right; yet I don’t think any-
body ever heard the old warrior David chuckle over
an idea that was really well worth a chuckle.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: An old landmark dis-
appears from one site in Sydney to re-appear in
another. This is Cunninghame’s printing office, which
for 50 years has stood in Pitt-street, within three
doors of King-street. Francis Cunninghame was an
old colonist, dating back to the first ’4o’s. In the
latter part of that decade he was editing the
People’s Advocate, which was published in King-
street, almost on the site of new Daily Telegraph
offices. At that time there were two other printing
offices in King-street. One was the Hibernian printing
office, on the east corner of what is now Truth’s
lane. This was run by William Baker. The other
was two doors west, and was known as the Albion.
Thomas Trood was the boss. In the early ’so’s Mr.
Cunninghame bought out Mrs. Trood, and the busi-
ness was continued there until it was removed to
the premises just vacated. Cunninghame himself
died many years ago. In King-street Mr. Cunning-
hame had the John Bull Tavern on one side and the
Bible Depot on the other. In Pitt-street in the first
’go’s he was in the midst of assorted businesses. On
the corner was the Elephant and Castle, an old-
fashioned pubbery, kept by one William Lamb, who
was a fire-fighting captain. Bob Horne kept a
“night-house,” or restaurant, on his immediate south,
and” next him, strange as it may seem to-day, was
the shoeing smithy of Jack Edwards. Printers ink
later claimed the spot, as Sam Bennett built here the
T and C. Journal and Evening News office; after-
wards the business was removed to Market-street.
The thirsty comp was well provided for here, as in
those early davs, at any rate, there were seven pubs
within 50 yards of him. There only five now.
“Mr Ocky” • The elderly type-snatcher breathlessly
told us of a close shave from being run over. “My
God!” he said, “it was a narrow squeak. The wheel
missed me by a nonpareil. #
“Y.Z.”: One well known and influential paper at
loner bis the courage to advertise that it is not an
astounding success. The New Age (London) lately
printed its own balance-sheet, setting forth a loss of
P 1037 for the year, and it docs not complain. I
remember a Gippsland tri-weekly that did the same,
W it added a footnote: “It will be seen that we
are losing £7 a week in our efforts to carry en-
lightenment to the soul of this miserable district.
We might have been encouraged to persist, but for
our pathetic discovery that this district hasn t got a
soul.”
HARD LABOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I noticed him the first time at
A screaming comedy.
While those around him rocked and roared
He merely looked extremely bored.
And seemed quite dead to glee.
Judged by his attitude of gloom,
His thoughts were centred on the tomb.
Next, at a stirring play I saw
Him sitting, stern and cold,
While he who played the hero's part
Braved shot and shell to save his tart
From dangers manifold ;
The wretch seemed not to care a jot
If Ruth was saved, or Jack was shot !
“Yon hound,” I murmured to a friend,
“Regrets that to this show
He came, and.seems to long—alack!
To get his hard-earned money back!”
The other answered : “No ;
On plays he never spends a thrum —
The rag he’s on pays him to come!”
VICTORIA.

“ Rexona ” from her
dressing-room. There
was only a little in the

to completely put my

The Survival.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT is given to me to hold the lamp
That shines through the grey dead
years ;
I find my way to the cave tombs damp
With a vanished nation’s tears :
I hear the wings of the drowsy bats
Lift from the woven burial mats
And rustle down the years.
Hopes stir in me that others knew
In a warm Egyptian day.
I walk by a sea of Chaldean blue
In fiat meadows far away.
When I smile it is with alien lips.
1 touch old dreams with my finger tips,
And old gods hear me pray !
If I love ’tis as some Ragan loved
In a temple by the sea ;
And the same quick passion others moved
That this hour lias given to me.
For a bygone life beats in my veins,
Forgotten blisses, remembered pains
Lived once again shall be.
You hold a flower to my mouth,
I know it born to-day,
Yet in a vineyaid of the South,
Slow centuries away.
A maid with grape leaves on her brow
Held the same blossom you bold now
In just such laughing play !
Last night when the sky was gapped by stars
And the dusty wind swung low,
And the moon sank over the sunset’s bars,
The way that we all shall go,
And the river line was a snake of mist,
I kis’t you close as a conqueror kis’t
A captive long ago.
And you throbbed to me as a lover throbs
With a virgin heart to yield ;
And I felt as a thief who an altar robs
As he slinks to his potter’s field ; -
For the kisses were those I stole away
From a Roman girl in a bud-trimmed May
As I laid aside my shield.
And should I die in some darkened room
With a wafer on my tongue,
When the West is petalled in poppy bloom
And the Day’s harp is unstrung,
I shall remember and laugh, I know,
O’er a day I died in my long Ago,
Unshriven and unsung.
On a bloody plain, by a rocking wood,
With the nick of steel on steel,
My armor blackened with sweat and blood
And the round blue world a-reel;
While in the chapel on the bill
A white-robed Nun is trembling still
My girdling arms to feel!
Shall I be born into other lives
To find YOU waiting there,
Grey orbs where the dumb chained spirit
strives
To ask me When and Where?
I looked at you with the same bold eyes
Taught the Sorrow that made you wise,
The Love that bore Despair !
It is given to me to hold the lamp
That shines through the muffled years ;
I know the way to the cave tombs —damp
From the centuries dropping tears ;
And over the rotting burial mats
I hear the wings of the noisome bats
Go flapping down the years !
QUEENSLAND.
M. Forrest.
My Ancestral Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EVEN though it is ten thousand years
or more since we last met, L thought
you would have remembered me. 1
remember you so well. Yet 1 passed you in
the street to-day, our eyes met, but....you
did not know me!
When I had caught the last of them in
my dying grapple, utterly spent myself, and
knowing my time was come, you hurled the
rock that wrecked his face before my eyes.
For which of us was it meant, that rock
—him or me?
Let me recall the happening.
* * * *
The cave had a wide opening at its
mouth, facing the river and the hill slope
beyond. They had rolled some big boulders
to one side of it at an angle, as shelter
against the weather and against us —my
brothers and I. Of course we didn’t matter
now. I, well named the Crafty One, alone
escaped that time your brothers caught us
unawares. 1 passed you in my flight. I
had not time to carry you off, but how I
wished I had killed you when I found
you had set them upon my track, telling
them the way of my going. I escaped in
•spite of you—far too great was my cunning
for such as they to match it.
I suppose the slaying of all iny brothers
had made them grow carelesSi Certainly
they felt safe from attack or they would
ne ver ave the Red One alone with you.
-three days I had watched my oppor-
tunity. At last it came.
The day before they had all gone hunting
the five of them with you and the other
women following after. The cave was de-
serted. That was when I crept from my
hiding-place in the thicket and examined it.
-•-he smoke-hole in the centre —here indeed
was the one place to attack from. I clam-
bered up to the cave top and examined it
trom above. What fools they were to fear
uo danger because only I was left! No sen-
tinels, no watch kept—that I had seen the
bay before— but now 1 saw it must have
been so for many, many days. The great
mund stone that by a slight touch, might be
]pfT d as a cov er to the smoke-hole, had been
ett unrolled for many a moon —a greening
0 i llo * B on its rolling side told me that.
* went back to my hiding-place, glad in
ts knowledge— went back and waited
again.
ou all, came home just before nightfall
_ a °, sentinels again all that night. The
fpp of your feasting and The smell of your
t ast came to me—but I never stirred. I
w * 1 must wait. AH night I waited,
vnnv j ay you a H went off again. When
ton t d Passed out of sight over the hill
nJu 1 Was tempted to creep into the cave
ffnif Secure som e of the remains of your
something to still the hunger gnawing
wumn me.
But here again my cunning saved me.
Hollow gutted as I was, I knew the risk of
doing this. So I waited still.
The sun was scarce overhead when 1
spied movement in the distance. I watched.
Two men only—what was the meaning of
it V No, not two men—a man and a woman !
The man with a young buck slung over his
shoulder —the woman —my eyes were stung
to tears with the intentness of my 100k —-
the woman was....you.
I was no longer hungry now. The
moment was mine. I knew it. Now would
I have the revenge for which my heart
lusted, and now, you who had set them upon
my track, you whom in my fleeing I could
hot carry with me, you would be mine too !
And I waited.
Down you came, the two of you, down the
hill slope, over the river, into the cave —no
more than a rock-hurl from me, crouching
flat in the thicket. I heard the crackling of
fire. I saw the black smoke rising up
through the hole. I saw it blueing as the
flames leaped up. Then there was a lull in
the smoke wreath—he was splitting the
buck then. Soon I smelt the singed flesh,
the savory richness of the roasting beast.
Too hot were the hunger and the hate with-
in me—l could not wait longer. I gripped
the rock I had chosen for the slaying in my
two hands —gripped it as if its weight were
as nought—and ran like the wind, bearing
it to the roof of the cave. Here I crouched
down beside the smoke-hole, through which
1 meant to hurl the murder stone.
I tried to see you. The smoke blinded me
and 1 was forced to withdraw my head. It
was well. Through the moisture spilling
from my eyes I saw you go at his bidding to
the river for water. Down I crouched —the
rock was the further side of the smoke-hole
and I could still watch him without being
seen by you.
With my face just out of the rising smoke
I found I could look without it smarting me.
There he was, my enemy, the lied One
whom I hated, the leader of his tribe. Bend-
ing over the beast he caught the dripping
fat as it fell with a long-handled stone ladle,
basting the belly part with it. His back
was to me —his red, shaggy hair falling over
his shoulders on either side. His club leaned
against the cave wall, full five paces from
him. I knew he could never reach it in
time. I raised my knees up towards me,
gripped the rock above my head so that my
knuckles whitened, the hate in me seeming
to burst my ribs, and poised it for the aim.
There was a warning cry from the river
bank. You, whose eyes were for danger as
the eagles for prey, had spied me! But you
were too late! He hesitated, knowing not
whence to look for danger.
Then I threw....and my rock crashed
into his skull as were it of eggshell. He
fell, kicking, into the fire, bringing down
the spitted beast with him in his fall. His
brains, grey and smattered and bloody,
smoked and sizzled in the flames.
Then it was that a rich joy rioted within
me, and, leaping up with a hoarse shout of
triumph, I tore down to make you my cap-
tive at the river bank. I thought that,
womanlike, you would flee. But no ! Whilst
I ran I watched you give a quick look up
the hill slope as if expecting the other men’s
return. Then you shouted —shouted a loud
call that flung itself against the mountains
and echoed along the valleys till the woods
rang with it. After this you darted down
and picked up a rock that many a strong
man would have been proud to hurl, and
stood waiting me in defiance. Your black
eyes flashed lightnings of angry hate, your
tense muscles stood out in clear relief be-
neath your brown skin as you took your
stand ready for the throw. My heart leapt
wild within me to make you mine, only de-
siring you the more from your hate.
You threw well —but 1 was more agile
than you thought. The rock just grazed my
shoulder as 1 leapt aside and ducked—a
mere scratch, no worse. But even now you
did not fear me. Before I knew it you were
upon me —kicking, lunging, biting with all
a mad thing’s fury. It was not till I dazed
you with a blow that I captured you—and
even stunned as you were then, you
struggled with me all the while I dragged
you by your long black hair to the cave.
It angered me, a woman to make this use-
less resistance; and when I got you inside
the cave I flung you with all my strength to
its' far corner, where you lay, beaten and
abashed. He, the Bed One, was there, just
as I had felled him, his battered head
charred in the fire, and all the red hair of
his body singed off on one side. In my anger
I kicked him over and over till I left him in
front of you, taunting you about him all
the while. • , , _ .
But now, seeing the roasted buck flesh,
hunger moved too strongly within me. It was
so long since I had tasted food. I tore off a
strip along the backbone that was more
roasted than the rest and sat down before
the tire—sitting at an angle so that I could
both watch von and keep my eye on that
part of the hill-top whence your glance at
the river-side told me to expect the brothers
coining. „ , T * ~ j
You scowled at me all the while I bolted
the hot buck meat, tearing off great mouth-
fuls of it with my teeth. What for all your
glowering did I care? You were mine now,
and he, the Red One, lay stiffening in a pool
of congealed blood beside you !
I thought more of you the more my
hunger got appeased —and indeed you were
rich to look upon. There was a roundness
in your limbs that made my eyes glad, a
rude, subtle grace that was well fitted to
make you the mate of such a man as I —-the
strongest as well as the craftiest of my tribe.
So I flung you a strip of meat that I had
but half eaten and bade you eat. You
scowled at me the more and spurned it from
you with your foot. I only laughed at this,
knowing how well I would tame you when I
had the wish !
I caught your look again—the look of an
animal that’sees its way of escape from an
enemy. I read the look—for all your at-
tempt at concealment. I followed it —and
there were the other four brothers of the
Red One, stalking stealthily down the hill-
side from rock to rock, knowing the danger,
but not vet knowing what it was.
Now was no time to waste. None should
take you from me. You were mine. I swore
; t an d were his brothers four times four
still" would I claim you as mine. And were
they four times four, still did the blood of
mv own kinsmen call for vengeance. There
was the spirit of battle seething in me
And as I watched them coming I thought
me of the smoke-hole above. What I had

longer time to roll the rock over it from
above —and I cursed my cunning that it had
failed me in not making this place of attack
secure when yet the way was clear to do so.
Something—l know not what —made me turn
to you. You would know the way of its
closing from within, and you must show me.
With this I turned on you—it was all in
a far shorter space than I take to
tell it —and spat out the order through my
tightened lips. I would have killed you had
you failed me. I think you read this in my
face, for straightway you got a long thong
of greenhide, and. throwing it through the
hole, looped it over the hanging rock. Then
you drew the thong tight, pulling with both
hands. I saw it was more than your strength
could compass, and, like a madman, > I
snatched it from you and pulled till the veins
of my neck purpled and were like to burst.
The rock swayed for an instant. I pulled
anew, and, with a loud thud, it fell into
its place, completely covering the hole.
Now I was safe from that quarter —and
not too soon either. I pushed you to your
corner again, and, as I looked out the cave
opening, there they were, the four of them,
almost within hurling distance of the cave.
1 took his club from the wall, and posting
myself behind a ledge of rock beside the
opening, waited their attack.
I had not long to wait. Crash—crash —
crash —crash. Four great rocks hurtled past
me into the cave, doing me no hurt because
of the protecting ledge. Perhaps you were
hurt —the thought flashed through me. No,
there you were behind me. with a look in
your eyes so wondrous strange that I knew
not what it was. But it made me grip mv
club the tighter, ready for their rush-
made the battle lust so riot in my blood that
I could scarce restrain myself from rushing
out upon the four of them, there in the
open. „ , _ , .
But still I was the Crafty One—and I
waited. I would let you see the worth of
me. Poor indeed would you hold the Bed
One then. If you chose to attack me from
behind when I was in the thick of the
combat —it was well. But again that some-
thing told me you would not —that look in
vour eyes I think it was that told me so.
‘ But hark! The patter of bare, rushing
feet on the sandy entrance to the cave. -they
were coming. I stood back a step with my
club held ready for the swing. Yes, it was
they! I saw a shadow flash for an instant
—I caught just a glimpse of rushing faces
then I leapt a step forward and swung.
Was ever blow like it! It smashed
through one man’s head as were it so much
rotten tinder —smashed through it, and,
scarcely stayed in its might, felled another
to the ground with. the same swing. Oh,
it was grand ; I shrieked in my joy . They
were upon me —there were still two. And
how we fought! A blow on my side, its
force part spent on my swinging club,
crashed against my ribs. I felt them broken
and crunching against one another within
me. Another, aimed too high, chipped my
scalp. Then I smote as never man smote
he f ore _ an( j the third went down to me,
fell a slaughtered lump of flesh at my feet.
But it was too late. A blow broke my
striking arm at the shoulder, and bone
stuck out through the battered flesh, .tag-
ging my cheek as I moved. There was only
one man left, but my time was come.. I
was reeling like a sapling in the whirlwind,
and blood poured from my mouth and ears
in a torrent.
Yet even then I thought of you—strange
that it was you and not my murdered
brothers of whom I thought in that last
moment. And it gave me new strength,
this thought of you. I threw down my
club, and with a yell grappled my man,
my legs round his body, my one hand
clutching bis windpipe to tear it from his
throat. We swayed together—l felt his
hot, gasping breath on my cheek—he was
bearing me down-; —soon he would have me
at. a length to kill and. . . .
Crash.... then the rock came that
crushed his face before my eyes—the rock
that you threw. His club fell to the
ground—his grip relaxed, and I felt his
legs twitching against mine in his agony.
I tried to turn towards you—l could not.
All was blackness —and we fell together,
he, the last of the four, and I.
All this was ten thousand years. ago—
and yet to-day. when you passed me in your
silks' and your high-heeled boots and your
Paris hat. you did not know me!
At which of us. him or me, did you hurl
that rock?
BERNARD INGLERY.
Under Police Protection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LISTEN !” cried Alice tragically.
“It’s the wind!” I suggested
tremulously.
“Yes. of course it's the wind ! chatteied
Grace. But her grip on my arm tightened
as she said it, and my own heart fairly
thudded. We all listened breathlessly.
Three nervous girls in an isolated villa
on a dark, gusty night, a masked burglar
scare in full blast, and a well-read evening
paper full of tragedies lying open on the
table. Here were all the ingredients tor a
panic, waiting only the imagined stealthy
footfall, or the rattle of a loose window sash
“There! A footstep on the hack
verandah! What shall we do?” Alice rose
swiftly to her feet and looked wildly at me.
“Are you sure?" I faltered.
Grace whispered, half to herself, I—l m
almost sure I heard something!”
That was sufficient. Alice simply flung
herself at the telephone. In an incredibly
short period she had got the local police
station and was gasping out her story.
There was a strange man prowling round
Mr. Smithjones’ place in King Avenue, and
would they please send a constable down at
once. A rich Hibernian voice—(l was lis-
tening intently at the odd end of the instru-
ment and can vouch for it) —promised very
heartily that they would, and “not to be
frightened now, but just wait a few min-
“Oh, thank you!” sobbed Alice. (The
vay she said it would have enslaved a chief
commissioner.) , ~ ,
“Sure, don’t let it trouble you,” cooed that
most divine voice. “If ever you’re ner-rvous
about these villyuns ring up th’ station.
“So GOOD of you!” moaned Alice, re-
luctant to let him go.
“Th’ man will be there soon. Good -night,
Miss.” (This somewhat hurriedly. I fancy
his wife or a sub-inspector must have
dropped in on him.)
“Good-night, and thank you ever so
much !”
The tinkling ring-off made us all shiver
with loneliness in the dimly-lit hall. We
crowded back to the dining-room and
huddled round the fire, where we told off
the minutes till something vast but unmis-
takable tramped heavily on to the verandah
and battered thunderously on the door. W e
opened it to the very largest policeman in
the whole State.
“Good evenin’,” he said, and his voice
sounded like the cathedral organ in its in-
trospective moments. “Th’ Sergeant sent
me down to take charge.”
“Please come in!” we chorused, and led
the immense, solemn man down the hall, to
the cosy dining-room. At our earnest solici-
tation, he lowered himself into the great
leather armchair in which our middle-weight
uncle looks like a half-grown boy. He filled
it completely with brawn, blue serge tunic
and bright silver buttons. Then very slowly
and deliberately he laid on the table one hel-
met, one pair of handcuffs, one baton and
one terrible-looking service revolver fully
loaded. Then, being incited thereto, he
found one pipe and filled it with uncle’s
tobacco. When he had lit it and -felt thor-
oughly at home, he told us in plain official
language of riots and raids and arrests till
the mere money-making existence of dear
but absent uncle seemed a wretched thing
by comparison. We girls crouched round
the fire listening to his rumbling voice and
the wind, and gloating secretly over his
superb physique. The night had no terrors
for us now!
“Fancy,” whispered Grace, when he was
explaining some legal technicality to Alice;
“just fancy any larrikin trying to knock him
down and kick him !”
At 11.30 Alice remarked in an apologetic
way that she was afraid we really must go
to bed now. Before we went we laid out a
nice little supper, put the decanter, cigars and
papers handy, and implored him to make
himself comfortable. He respectfully pro-
mised that he would, but intimated that he
must keep on the lookout for the man we
had seen. (Alice gave a guilty start at this.
Somehow that man did not seem to be such
a real terror now.)
We fluttered thankfully off to bed, assur-
ing one another that to-night at least we
would be able to sleep soundly. Eventually
we ceased talking in the dark, and snuggled
down on our untroubled pillows.... Half-
way to dreams, a rasping, explosive sound
shocked me back to complete consciousness.
“What was that?” three meek voices
chimed together.
“It must have been him clearing his
throat.”
Of course. We sighed once each and re-
sumed our pillows.
He cleared his splendid, bronzed throat
three times before I beat him and won to
sleep. But I simply had to wake up half an
hour later when he creaked down the hall on
his first round of inspection. There were
numerous things in that dark hall to fall
over, and he fell over them all with the
thoroughness of a large, patient animal with
large unbreakable shins. There were loose
boards all along the verandah, and he tried
them all with his 1& —or 26 —stone of brawn.
The house —solid and comfortable enough
hitherto-seemed suddenly old and ram-
shackle, and jerry-built.
“I do wish he wouldn’t make such a
noise!” moaned a voice—Grace’s.
“You ought to be very thankful he’s here !”
retorted his devoted admirer. (For an en-
gaged girl, I really think Alice rather over-
did it.)
“But why doesn’t he w 7 alk on his toes?” 1
asked plaintively.
Grace snapped back: “But that won’t
make him any lighter, xvill it?”
The careful closing of the front door, and
another subdued but wall-shaking cough
shamed us into silence.
As I drew the clothes over my head I heard
him put down his tremendous revolver and
livht a match. The delightful aroma of one
of uncle’s cigars hung in the air as I dozed
off. . . .
“ He's been whistling that tune through
his teeth for the last half-hour!” Never
have I heard a more vindictive whisper than
Grace’s. It was “The Minstrel Boy”—a
rather imperfect rendering of the well-known
classic, but still recognisable.
“Perhaps he’ll stop soon,” sighed Alice.
“Well, I hope so!”
“Must be past two now,” I ventured. “I
suppose it’s awfully lonely-for him in there.”
Silence. . . .
“Oh, it’s no good ! I can’t sleep !” I
awoke blinking to find the gas blazing and
Grace in a blazing temper sitting up straight
in bed.
“What’s the matter now?”
“Oh, it’s all very well for you! You just
lie there and sleep through it all, but I hear
every move that great hulking brute makes !
Do you know how many times he’s been
round the house?”
“Yes, I do! He’s been round just five
times, and he’s cleared his throat 45 times.
He dropped his baton on the fender once
and knocked out his pipe on the bars of the
grate three times. There!”
“Oh, I see you’ve been aw’ake some of the
time !”
“Every minute!” I replied grimly.
Alice rose up > in bed haggard-eyed.
“What’s the time?”
“Half-past five. Been awake long?”
“From the start of ‘The Minstrel Boy,’ ”
she sighed, and lay down like a woman with
a broken heart.
Thenceforward we lay in our little beds
and waited without speaking for the dawn.
At six, loyal Alice got up, put on a kimono
and made tea for our faithful protector.
He absorbed three cups, received her heart-
felt thanks, and creaked heavily off the
trembling premises into a dew-drenched
world.
He was a magnificent specimen of a man
—the sort of specimen that should be kept
out in the solid street, or in a stone-flagged
temple—anywhere, in fact, where there are
no loose boards or light furniture. Nowa-
days we all bow to Muldoon (its name was
Muldoon), and are very nice to the fatherly
old Sergeant. They acknowledge our bows
with grave dignity. Of course, it wasn’t
their fault that all uncle's valuable fowls
were stolen during the long Muldoonic vigiL
Still the roost remains.
NANCY O'FERRALL.

SUNSHINE ENGINES
for driving Chaffcutters, Saw Benches, Pumps, Dynamos, Graders, Hoists, Mortar Mills, etc.
H. V. McKAYSunshine, Vic. Adelaide Brisbane Sydney Melbourne.
The ALBANY Patent Water-sealed Rotary Pump.
PATENTED IN ALL COUNTRIES.
The most important feature of these pumps is the fact that slip and leakage have been reduced to a minimum by the introduction of grooves cut
along the faces and edges of the teeth, so that, in rotating, a body of water lodges in the grooves, forming an absolute, sealed, water-tight joint or
cushion between the casing and rotating rollers, thereby producing an efficient vacuum.
The position of the pump has become assured wherever electrically-driven pumps are required; and in the improved type all wear is eliminated
from the internal members by the introduction of steel spur wheels, fitted in a suitable gear box outside the pump cover—and by means of which
the whole of the Power is Transmitted.
NO SKILLED MECHANIC REQUIRED. NO VALVES REQUIRED.
Suitable for boiler feed, deep wells, hot liquids, boiling soap, oil, tar, crude petroleum, fats, and other liquids of high gravity, also gas exhausters.
The smaller sizes are specially adapted for forced lubrication.
